Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Participation in Disjunctive Propositions (First Type)
Author classifies participation in first type of disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of participation between the two parts of a disjunctive proposition for the first type (where parts are categorical) and provide an example for each from the passage.

Core Idea
In disjunctives of the first type, the two parts can share subject, predicate, both, or neither.

Disjunctive propositions (sharṭiyya munfaṣila) present alternatives. The passage breaks down how the two parts of such a disjunction can logically relate by sharing components. This classification is essential for understanding the strength of a disjunction and for determining which conclusions follow from it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first type of disjunctive
The passage discusses three types of disjunctives. The first type (marked [أ]) is where the two parts are categorical propositions (e.g., 'every human is an animal' or 'no human is inanimate'). Identify the parts as the first disjunct and the second disjunct.
The examples given are all about humans and animals or inanimate objects.
2
Check subject sharing
Determine whether the subjects of the two disjuncts are the same. In 'either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal', the subject 'every human' is the same. In 'either every human is an animal or every human is inanimate', again the subject is shared.
The subject can be identical or different (e.g., 'every human' vs. 'some sentient being').
3
Check predicate sharing
Determine whether the predicates of the two disjuncts share the same concept. In 'either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal', the predicate 'animal' appears in both (with negation in the second). In 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal', the predicate 'animal' is the same but subjects differ.
Predicate sharing can be exact or with negation.
4
Classify into one of four types
Based on the two checks, place the disjunction into one of four categories: (1) shared subject and predicate, (2) subject only, (3) predicate only, (4) neither. The passage gives an example for each.
Example for category 1: 'either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal' (same subject and predicate, but second is negated). Category 2: 'either every human is an animal or every human is inanimate' (same subject, different predicate). Category 3: 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal' (different subject, same predicate). Category 4: 'either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive' (neither subject nor predicate match).
5
Verify with the passage's examples
Go through the examples after [أ] in the Arabic text. For each, identify which participation type applies. The passage lists them in order: first example = both, second = subject only, third = predicate only, fourth = neither.
None of these disjunctions are true; they are used only to illustrate participation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طالعة كان النهار موجودًا لكان كلما كان العالم مضيئًا كان النهار موجودًا؛ الرابع لو كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا لكان كلما كان العالم مضيئًا كان الليل معدومًا.
[ت] وعلى هذا في الثالث منها : إذ الأول كقولنا لو كان كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا لكان هذا العدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ الثاني كقولنا لو كان كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما يكون فردًا لكان كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن لا يكون زوجا؛ الثالث كقولنا لو كان كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا لكان كل عدد إما أن لا يكون فردًا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ الرابع كقولنا لو كان كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا لكان كل عدد إما أن يكون منقسما بقسمين متساويين وإما أن لا يكون.
وأما إذا كانت من المنفصلات فكذلك [ أ ] ؛ إذ المقدم في الأول من الثلاثة لا يخلو من أن يكون مشاركًا للتالي في الموضوع والمحمول، أو في الموضوع دون المحمول، أو في المحمول دون الموضوع، أو لا يكون مشاركًا: أما الأول فكقولنا إما أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانا وإما أن لا يكون كل إنسان حيوانا؛ وأما الثاني فكقولنا إما أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانًا وإما أن يكون كل إنسان جمادًا؛ وأما الثالث فكقولنا إما أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانًا وإما أن يكون كل جماد حيوانا؛ وأما الرابع فكقولنا إما أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانا وإما أن لا يكون بعض الناطق حساسًا.
[ب] وكذلك المقدم في الثاني منها فإنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مشاركًا للتالي في المقدم والتالي، أو في المقدم دون التالي أو في التالي دون المقدم أو لا يكون مشاركًا: فالأول هو كقولنا إما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن لا يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود؛ والثاني كقولنا إما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود؛ والثالث إما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون كلما كان الليل موجودًا فالنهار موجود؛ الرابع إما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون كلما كان العالم مضيئًا فالليل موجود.
[ت] وعلى هذا في الثالث منها؛ إذ الأول كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ والثاني كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن لا يكون زوجا؛ الثالث كقولنا إما كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن لا يكون فردًا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ الرابع كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون منقسما بقسمين متساويين وإما أن لا يكون.
ثم في هذا المقام يجب أن تعلم بأن المحمول أو التالي إذا لم يكن شاملًا لا وجوده ولا عدمه لكل ما يكون متصفا به، كما في الثالث من القسم الأول مثلا كان التعاند بين المقدم والتالي لازما، وهذا هو أصل مفصل في الباب، ولنقتصر على هذا التقدير فإن الاطلاع عليه مما يطلعك على ما في الغير من الصور من المشاركات بين جزئي المقدم والتالي في سائر الشرطيات.
If the sun rose, then the day would exist; so that whenever the world is illuminated, the day exists; the fourth: if whenever the sun rose the day existed, then whenever the world is illuminated, the night is non-existent. [T] And similarly for the third of them: for the first is like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then this number is either even or odd; the second like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either even or not even; the third like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either not odd or odd; the fourth like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either divisible into two equal parts or not. As for when they are from the disjunctives, it is likewise [A]; for the antecedent in the first of the three is not free from being a participant with the consequent in subject and predicate, or in subject without predicate, or in predicate without subject, or not being a participant: as for the first, it is like our saying: either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal; as for the second, like our saying: either every human is an animal or every human is an inanimate; as for the third, like our saying: either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal; as for the fourth, like our saying: either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive. [B] And likewise the antecedent in the second of them is not free from being a participant with the consequent in antecedent and consequent, or in antecedent without consequent, or in consequent without antecedent, or not being a participant: the first is like our saying: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or it is not the case that whenever the sun rises then day exists; the second like our saying: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the sun rises then night exists; the third: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever night exists then day exists; the fourth: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the world is illuminated then night exists. [T] And similarly for the third of them; for the first like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is either even or odd; the second like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is either even or not even; the third like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is not odd or odd; the fourth like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is divisible into two equal parts or not. Then in this place you must know that if the predicate or the consequent is not comprehensive—neither its existence nor its non-existence—for everything that is described by it, as in the third of the first section for example, then the contradiction between the antecedent and consequent is necessary. And this is a detailed principle in this chapter, and let us confine ourselves to this amount, for studying it will inform you about other forms of participation between the parts of the antecedent and consequent in all conditionals.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a technical classification of the types of logical participation (musharaka) between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in conditional (muttasila) propositions, and between the two parts of disjunctive (munfasila) propositions. The author begins by continuing a discussion on conditional propositions from the previous page, presenting four cases for conditionals: the fourth case is illustrated with the example that if whenever the sun rises the day exists, then whenever the world is illuminated the night is non-existent. Then, marked with [ت], the same pattern is applied to the third type using number examples: each number is either even or odd. The first case gives 'if every number is even or odd, then this number is even or odd'; the second gives 'then every number is even or not even'; the third gives 'then every number is not odd or odd'; the fourth gives 'then every number is divisible into two equal parts or not'.

Next, the passage moves to disjunctive propositions (munfasila). Marked with [أ], the author states that the antecedent in the first of the three types cannot escape being a participant with the consequent in one of four ways: (1) in subject and predicate together, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal'; (2) in subject only, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or every human is inanimate'; (3) in predicate only, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal'; (4) in neither, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive'. 

Similarly, for the second type of disjunctive, marked with [ب], the antecedent's participation with the consequent can be: (1) in the whole antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or it is not the case that...'; (2) in the antecedent only, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the sun rises then night exists'; (3) in the consequent only, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever night exists then day exists'; (4) in neither, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the world is illuminated then night exists'. 

Then for the third type, marked with [ت], the pattern is applied to number propositions: four cases of disjunctives with 'every number is either even or odd' as the first part, and various negations or alterations in the second part.

Finally, the author imparts a key lesson: if the predicate or consequent is not comprehensive in existence or non-existence for everything it describes—as in the third case of the first section—then a necessary contradiction (ta'ānud) arises between the antecedent and consequent. This is a detailed principle in this chapter, and the author limits the discussion to this extent, promising that understanding it will reveal the participation patterns in all conditionals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the disjunction 'either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive', what is the participation type?
A) Shared subject and predicate
B) Shared subject only
C) Shared predicate only
D) Neither subject nor predicate

Key Takeaway
In the first type of disjunctive, the two parts can share subject and predicate, subject only, predicate only, or neither, each producing a different logical relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disjunction 'Either every obligation is commanded by Allah or every obligation is not commanded by Allah.' Identify the participation type.
?
From the passage, take the example 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal'. State the participation type and explain why.

Propositions
advanced
12 min
Participation in Disjunctive Propositions (Second and Third Types)
Author extends participation to second and third types and gives comprehensive principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of participation for the second and third types of disjunctives (where parts are conditionals or mixed) and identify the comprehensive principle about necessary contradiction.

Core Idea
For complex disjunctives, participation can involve the antecedent and consequent of the conditionals within.

The passage extends the participation analysis to more complex disjunctives: those where each part is itself a conditional (second type) or where parts contain mixed forms (third type). Finally, it introduces a key principle: if the consequent is not comprehensive, a necessary contradiction arises. This is the logical foundation for many arguments in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second type of disjunctive
The second type (marked [ب]) is where the two parts are conditional propositions themselves. For example, 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or it is not the case that...' The participation is now between the antecedent and consequent of the conditionals.
The author says participation can be in the whole antecedent-and-consequent, in the antecedent only, in the consequent only, or in neither.
2
Identify the four subcases for the second type
The passage gives four examples: (1) the second part is the negation of the first, so they share both antecedent and consequent; (2) they share the antecedent 'whenever the sun rises' but differ in consequent ('day exists' vs. 'night exists'); (3) they differ in antecedent but share consequent 'day exists'; (4) they share neither antecedent nor consequent.
These are analogous to the first type but applied to conditionals.
3
Understand the third type of disjunctive
The third type (marked with [ت] again) is where the parts are disjunctive propositions themselves (e.g., 'either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that...'). The analysis follows the same pattern: four participation cases.
The examples given mirror those of the first type, but now each part is itself a disjunction.
4
Grasp the comprehensive principle
The passage ends with a crucial insight: if the predicate or consequent is not comprehensive—i.e., its existence or non-existence does not apply to everything that can be described by it—then a necessary contradiction (ta'ānud) arises between the antecedent and consequent.
This is illustrated by the third example of the first type: 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal' – the predicate 'animal' is not comprehensive, so a contradiction arises.

Commentary
This passage is a technical classification of the types of logical participation (musharaka) between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in conditional (muttasila) propositions, and between the two parts of disjunctive (munfasila) propositions. The author begins by continuing a discussion on conditional propositions from the previous page, presenting four cases for conditionals: the fourth case is illustrated with the example that if whenever the sun rises the day exists, then whenever the world is illuminated the night is non-existent. Then, marked with [ت], the same pattern is applied to the third type using number examples: each number is either even or odd. The first case gives 'if every number is even or odd, then this number is even or odd'; the second gives 'then every number is even or not even'; the third gives 'then every number is not odd or odd'; the fourth gives 'then every number is divisible into two equal parts or not'.

Next, the passage moves to disjunctive propositions (munfasila). Marked with [أ], the author states that the antecedent in the first of the three types cannot escape being a participant with the consequent in one of four ways: (1) in subject and predicate together, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal'; (2) in subject only, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or every human is inanimate'; (3) in predicate only, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal'; (4) in neither, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive'. 

Similarly, for the second type of disjunctive, marked with [ب], the antecedent's participation with the consequent can be: (1) in the whole antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or it is not the case that...'; (2) in the antecedent only, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the sun rises then night exists'; (3) in the consequent only, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever night exists then day exists'; (4) in neither, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the world is illuminated then night exists'. 

Then for the third type, marked with [ت], the pattern is applied to number propositions: four cases of disjunctives with 'every number is either even or odd' as the first part, and various negations or alterations in the second part.

Finally, the author imparts a key lesson: if the predicate or consequent is not comprehensive in existence or non-existence for everything it describes—as in the third case of the first section—then a necessary contradiction (ta'ānud) arises between the antecedent and consequent. This is a detailed principle in this chapter, and the author limits the discussion to this extent, promising that understanding it will reveal the participation patterns in all conditionals.

The Four Kinds of Disjunctive Conditionals
Author enumerates four types of disjunctive conditionals with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of disjunctive conditionals and distinguish between them based on the relationship between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals vary in logical force depending on the opposition between the two clauses.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions are divided into conjunctive (al-muttasila) and disjunctive (al-munfasila). This lesson focuses on the disjunctive type, which presents a choice between two possibilities. The author classifies disjunctive conditionals into four kinds, each with a distinct logical relationship between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). Understanding these types is crucial for evaluating arguments that employ disjunctions, especially in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of a disjunctive conditional
A disjunctive conditional typically uses 'إما' (either...or) to connect an antecedent and a consequent. In the examples, the antecedent itself is often a conditional, forming a compound structure.
The antecedent is a conditional proposition like 'whenever the sun rises, daytime exists'.
2
Identify the two main categories
First category: the consequent contradicts the antecedent's consequent directly (e.g., either if A then B, or if A then not B). Second category: the consequent is a different proposition that is opposite or unrelated.
The author gives four examples with sun and daytime.
3
Classify the examples from the passage
For each of the four examples, determine the relation: (1) consequent negates the antecedent's consequent; (2) consequent is its opposite (night); (3) consequent is a different conditional linking night to daytime; (4) consequent uses a different predicate (illuminated world to night).
4
Apply to numerical examples
The second set replaces content with numbers being even or odd. Map each example to the corresponding type from the first set.
Recognize that the logical relation is the same regardless of subject matter.
5
Understand the principle of necessary opposition (ta'anud)
When the consequent is not exhaustive (its existence and non-existence do not cover all cases), there is necessary opposition between antecedent and consequent. This is crucial for deciding whether a disjunctive conditional is strict or inclusive.
The author highlights this principle using the third type as example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكون مشاركًا: فالأول هو كقولنا إما أن يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن لا يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود؛ والثاني كقولنا إما أن يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود؛ والثالث إما أن يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون كلَّما كان الليل موجودًا فالنهار موجود؛ الرابع إما أن يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون كلَّما كان العالم مضيئًا فالليل موجود.
[ت] وعلى هذا في الثالث منها؛ إذ الأول كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ والثاني كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن لا يكون زوجا؛ الثالث كقولنا إما كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن لا يكون فردًا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ الرابع كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون منقسما بقسمين متساويين وإما أن لا يكون.
ثم في هذا المقام يجب أن تعلم بأن المحمول أو التالي إذا لم يكن شاملًا لا وجوده ولا عدمه لكل ما يكون متصفّا به، كما في الثالث من القسم الأول مثلا كان التعاند بين المقدم والتالي لازما، وهذا هو أصل مفصل في الباب، ولنقتصر على هذا التقدير فإن الاطلاع عليه مما يطلعك على ما في الغير من الصور من المشاركات بين جزئي المقدم والتالي في سائر الشرطيات.
It is not shared: the first is like our saying: 'Either whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists, or not whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists'; the second like our saying: 'Either whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists, or whenever the sun is rising then night exists'; the third: 'Either whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists, or whenever night exists then daytime exists'; the fourth: 'Either whenever the sun is rising then daytime exists, or whenever the world is illuminated then night exists.' [ت] And similarly for the third of them: for the first is like our saying: 'Either every number is either even or odd, or not every number is either even or odd'; the second: 'Either every number is either even or odd, or not every number is either even or not even'; the third: 'Either every number is either even or odd, or not every number is either not odd or odd'; the fourth: 'Either every number is either even or odd, or not every number is either divisible into two equal parts or not.' Then in this place you must know that the predicate or the consequent, if it is not comprehensive in its existence nor its non-existence for everything that is described by it, as in the third of the first section for example, then the opposition between the antecedent and the consequent is necessary. This is a detailed principle in the chapter, and we will confine ourselves to this appreciation, for examining it informs you of what is in other forms of the participations between the two parts of the antecedent and consequent in the rest of the conditionals.

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of disjunctive conditionals. The author begins by presenting the first three (or four?) types using the example of the sun and daytime. The first type is a logical disjunction where the consequent is either true or false given the antecedent (e.g., 'Either when the sun rises daytime exists, or it does not'). The second type is a complete opposition (تعاند) where the consequent is the opposite (night). The third type connects the consequent to another condition (night implying daytime). The fourth type mixes different predicates (illuminated world implies night). The author then repeats the classification using mathematical examples with numbers being even or odd. After enumerating, the author explains that if the consequent is not exhaustive (its existence and non-existence do not cover all cases), then there is necessary opposition (تعاند) between antecedent and consequent, as in the third type. This principle is foundational for understanding the relationships in conditionals.

Key Takeaway
The four kinds of disjunctive conditionals are defined by the logical relationship between the antecedent and consequent, ranging from direct negation to entirely different propositions, and understanding them prevents misinterpretation of disjunctions in Islamic arguments.

Particles of Implication in Conditional Propositions
Classification of Arabic conditional particles by implication strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Arabic conditional particles into those indicating necessary implication (luzūm), those indicating opposition (ʿinād), and those indicating neither, and assess the strength of each particle based on logical usage.

Core Idea
Not every 'if' implies necessity; only 'in' carries strong logical implication.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions are built with specific particles that signal the type of relationship between antecedent and consequent. This passage from the Mantiq Kabir classifies these particles, emphasizing that only 'in' (إن) strongly indicates necessary implication (luzūm), while others like 'mata' (متى) are weak, and 'idhā' (إذا) is purely temporal. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly interpreting logical arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of particles
Read the opening sentence and find the threefold division: particles indicating implication (luzūm), opposition (ʿinād), or neither. This sets the framework.
The author explicitly states: 'minhā mā yadullu ʿalā al-luzūm wa-minhā mā yadullu ʿalā al-ʿinād wa-minhā mā lā yadullu ʿalā hādhā wa-lā dhālik.'
2
Examine the particle 'in' (إن) as the strongest indicator
The author gives two examples: 'If human exists, animal exists' (valid, necessary) and 'If human exists, human is a spouse' (invalid as a logical conditional because the consequent is accidental). Note that 'in' requires the consequent to follow necessarily from the antecedent alone.
The author says 'in' is 'shadīdat al-quwwa fī al-dalāla ʿalā al-luzūm' (very strong in indicating implication).
3
Compare the other particles: mata, idh, idhā, kullamā, lammā
Rank the particles: 'mata' is weak, 'idh' is intermediate, 'idhā' and 'kullamā' indicate no implication at all (temporal or iterative), 'lammā' is neutral. Understand why 'idhā' does not imply logical necessity.
The author explicitly states that 'idhā' and 'kullamā' 'lā tadullu ʿalā al-luzūm al-batta.'
4
Recognize the four divisions based on particle position
The passage begins to discuss whether the connective particle occurs before or after the subject, which yields four types for both connectives and disjunctives. This sets the stage for later classification.
The text is cut off, but the idea is that the particle's position affects the logical structure.

أما في المتّصلات [أ] فكلمة «إن يدل على اللزوم، ولهذا يقال إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالحيوان موجود لما أن التالي بالضرورة يلزم من وضع المقدم، ولا يقال إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالإنسان زوج بل يقال متى كان الإنسان موجودًا فالإنسان زوج، إذًا التالي في مثل هذا المثال لا يلزم من وضع المقدم بل، وإن لزم فإنما يلزم باعتبار ما عداه من الأمور فيشبه أن يكون لفظة (إن) شديدة القوة في الدلالة على اللزوم. [ب] ومتى» ضعيفة في ذلك، [ت] و «إذ كالمتوسط، [ث] ولفظة «إذا» في قولنا إذا كان كذا كان كذا لا تدل على اللزوم البتة. [ج] وكذلك لفظة «كلما» فإنها لا تدل على اللزوم أيضًا، [ح] ولفظة «لما» في قولنا لما كان كذا كان كذا تصلح للأمرين، ولا يوجب أحدهما. والمقدم في الشرطي المتصل يدل على الوضع فقط ليس فيه أن يكون المقدم الذي وضع موجودًا أو لا موجودًا أصلا بل قد يمكن أن يكون موجودًا وقد لا يمكن. وقد كان من الحروف ما عدا الحروف المذكورة لكنه من جملة ما سلف لك فلا حاجة إلى التعرُّض لكل واحد منها بأنه ما هو وكيف هو؛ إذ هو من الوظائف النحوية بل الحاجة إلى التعرُّض لما هو من اللوازم ذكره؛ وذلك إن حرف الاتصال لا يخلو من أن يكون واقعا قبل الموضوع في المتصلات أو بعده.
وكذلك حرف الانفصال في المنفصلات فيكون الكل على أربعة أقسام: أما الأول وهو الذي يكون حرف الاتصال فيه قبل الموضوع، فذلك من
Chapter on the particles used in conditionals. And what is used from particles in conditionals is many: from them are those that indicate implication (luzūm), from them are those that indicate opposition (‘anād), and from them are those that indicate neither. As for connectives (muttasilāt), the word 'in' indicates implication; therefore it is said: 'If human exists, then animal exists' because the consequent necessarily follows from positing the antecedent; and it is not said 'If human exists, then human is a spouse' but rather 'Whenever human exists, human is a spouse'. So the consequent in such an example does not follow from positing the antecedent, but if it follows, it only follows by consideration of other matters. It seems that the word 'in' is very strong in indicating implication. 'Mata' is weak in that. 'Idh' is intermediate. The word 'idhā' in our saying 'when such is such' does not indicate implication at all. Likewise the word 'kullamā' does not indicate implication either. The word 'lammā' in 'when such is such' is suitable for both matters and does not necessitate either. And the antecedent in the conditional conjunctive indicates only the positing; it does not imply that the posited antecedent actually exists or not at all, but it may be possible to exist and may not be possible. And there are particles other than those mentioned, but they are among what has preceded you, so there is no need to delve into each one what it is and how it is, for that is among grammatical functions. Rather the need is to address what is necessary to mention, which is that the connective particle cannot be other than occurring before the subject in connectives or after it. Similarly the disjunction particle in disjunctives. So the whole is of four divisions. As for the first, which is that the connective particle is before the subject, that is from...
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a chapter (فصل) on the particles used in conditional propositions. The author classifies particles into three categories: those indicating implication (luzūm), those indicating opposition (‘anād), and those indicating neither. Focusing on connectives (muttasilāt), the author discusses specific particles: 'in' (if) is very strong in indicating necessary implication, exemplified by 'If human exists, animal exists' (a necessary truth) vs. 'If human exists, human is a spouse' (not necessary, but accidental). 'Mata' (whenever) is weak in implying necessity. 'Idh' is intermediate. 'Idhā' (when) does not indicate logical implication at all; it is temporal. 'Kullamā' (every time) also does not imply necessity. 'Lammā' (when) can be used for both but does not necessitate either. The author then notes that the antecedent merely posits a condition, not actual existence. The discussion then shifts to the position of the connective particle relative to the subject (before or after), which yields four divisions of connectives and disjunctives, though the text is incomplete.

Particles in Conditionals and the Four Divisions
Author categorizes conditional particles and classifies conditionals by particle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional particles based on their indication (luzum, inad, neither) and distinguish the four divisions of conditionals based on particle position relative to the subject.

Core Idea
Conditional particles vary in strength; particle position determines purity or reducibility to categorical.

Conditional propositions in Islamic logic rely on specific particles to express implication or incompatibility. This passage not only classifies these particles by their semantic force but also introduces a structural division based on the particle's position relative to the subject, yielding four distinct types of conditionals. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for analyzing logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of indication
List the three types of indication: implication (luzum), incompatibility ('inad), and neither. Note that particles like 'in' indicate strong implication, while 'idha' indicates none.
2
Compare the strength of particles in conjunctive conditionals
Examine the author's assessment: 'in' is very strong, 'mata' weak, 'idh' moderate, 'idha' and 'kullama' indicate no implication, 'lama' is ambiguous.
3
Recognize the four divisions based on particle position
The particle of conjunction or disjunction can appear before or after the subject, giving: 1) conjunctive particle before subject (pure conjunctive), 2) conjunctive particle after subject (close to categorical), 3) disjunctive particle before subject (pure disjunctive), 4) disjunctive particle after subject (force of categorical).
4
Differentiate the two types of disjunctive conditionals
Division 3 (particle before subject) is 'man'at al-jam'' (prevents both true) allowing both false. Division 4 (particle after subject) is 'man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww' (prevents both true and both false). The difference stems from whether the judgment applies to the universality or nature of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في ما يستعمل من الحروف في الشرطيات ] وما يستعمل من الحروف في الشرطيات فذلك متعدد: منها ما يدل على اللزوم ومنها ما يدل على العناد ومنها ما لا يدل لا على هذا ولا ذلك. أما في المتّصلات [أ] فكلمة «إن يدل على اللزوم، ولهذا يقال إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالحيوان موجود لما أن التالي بالضرورة يلزم من وضع المقدم، ولا يقال إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالإنسان زوج بل يقال متى كان الإنسان موجودًا فالإنسان زوج، إذًا التالي في مثل هذا المثال لا يلزم من وضع المقدم بل، وإن لزم فإنما يلزم باعتبار ما عداه من الأمور فيشبه أن يكون لفظة زماء (إن) شديدة القوة في الدلالة على اللزوم. [ب] ومتى» ضعيفة في ذلك، [ت] و «إذ كالمتوسط، [ث] ولفظة «إذا» في قولنا إذا كان كذا كان كذا لا تدل على اللزوم البتة. [ج] وكذلك لفظة «كلما» فإنها لا تدل على اللزوم أيضًا، [ح] ولفظة «لما» في قولنا لما كان كذا كان كذا تصلح للأمرين، ولا يوجب أحدهما. والمقدم في الشرطي المتصل يدل على الوضع فقط ليس فيه أن يكون المقدم الذي وضع موجودًا أو لا موجودًا أصلا بل قد يمكن / أن يكون موجودًا وقد لا يمكن. وقد كان من الحروف ما عدا الحروف المذكورة لكنه من جملة ما سلف لك فلا حاجة إلى التعرُّض لكل واحد منها بأنه ما هو وكيف هو؛ إذ هو من الوظائف النحوية بل الحاجة إلى التعرُّض لما هو من اللوازم ذكره؛ وذلك إن حرف الاتصال لا يخلو من أن يكون واقعا قبل الموضوع في المتصلات أو بعده. وكذلك حرف الانفصال في المنفصلات فيكون الكل على أربعة أقسام: أما الأول وهو الذي يكون حرف الاتصال فيه قبل الموضوع، فذلك من المتصلات الخالصة كقولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وأما الثاني وهو الذي يكون حرف الاتصال فيه بعد الموضوع، فذلك قريب من الحملي كقولنا الشمس كلما كانت طالعة كان النهار موجودًا، فإنك أخَّرت عن الشمس بأن حكمها كذا. وأما الثالث وهو الذي يكون حرف الانفصال فيه قبل الموضوع، فذلك من المنفصلات الخالصة كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد زوجا وإما أن يكون كل عدد فردًا. وأما الرابع وهو الذي يكون حرف الانفصال فيه بعد الموضوع، فذلك في قوة الحملي كقولنا كل عــدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا، فإنك أخرتَ عن كل عدد أنه شيء من شأنه أن لا يخلو عن أحد هذين الوصفين فالفرق بينه وبين ما تقدم وهو الثالث أن هذا هو المانعة من الجمع والخلو؛ وذلك هو المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو، فإنه يمكن اجتماع الطرفين فيه على الكذب إذا كان الصدق في البعض من الكل دون البعض، وإن كان لا يمكن اجتماعهما على الصدق البتة. وأما لمية الفرق فهي أن الحكم في الثالث يتعلق لكلية العدد؛ إذ المورد هو كلية العدد، وفي الرابع بطبيعة العدد؛ إذ المورد هو الطبيعة. ثمَّ الشرطية متصلة كانت أو منفصلة فإنه يمكن ردها إلى الحملية على الخصوص إذا كانت متّصلة يكون المقدم فيها مشاركًا للتالي في الموضوع كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الجسم متحركًا بالإرادة فهو حساس فإنه في قوة قولنا كل جسم متحركًا بالإرادة حساس.
Section: [On the particles used in conditionals] The particles used in conditionals are multiple: some indicate implication, some indicate incompatibility, and some indicate neither. As for in the conjunctives [a] the word 'in' indicates implication, for this reason it is said: 'If man exists, then animal exists' because the consequent necessarily follows from positing the antecedent; and it is not said: 'If man exists, then man is a spouse', rather it is said: 'Whenever man exists, man is a spouse', since the consequent in such an example does not follow from positing the antecedent, but if it follows, it only follows by consideration of other matters, so it appears that the word 'in' is very strong in indicating implication. [b] And 'mata' is weak in that, [t] and 'idh' is moderate, [th] and the word 'idha' in our saying 'when such is the case, such is the case' does not indicate implication at all. [j] Likewise the word 'kullama' also does not indicate implication, [h] and the word 'lama' in our saying 'since such was the case, such was the case' is suitable for both matters, and does not necessitate either one. The antecedent in a conjunctive conditional indicates only the positing; it does not entail that the antecedent posited exists or does not exist at all, but rather it may be possible to exist and may not be possible. And among the particles there are others besides those mentioned, but they are part of what has preceded for you, so there is no need to address each one individually regarding what it is and how it is; for that is among grammatical functions, but the need is to address that which is necessary to mention. That is because the particle of conjunction in conjunctives is either positioned before the subject or after it. And likewise the particle of disjunction in disjunctives; thus the whole is divided into four divisions: The first is that in which the particle of conjunction is before the subject, and that is among pure conjunctives, like our saying: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists.' The second is that in which the particle of conjunction is after the subject, and that is close to the categorical, like our saying: 'The sun, whenever it is shining, daytime exists', for you have delayed [the particle] after the sun, implying its judgment is such. The third is that in which the particle of disjunction is before the subject, and that is among pure disjunctives, like our saying: 'Either every number is even or every number is odd.' The fourth is that in which the particle of disjunction is after the subject, and that is in force of the categorical, like our saying: 'Every number is either even or odd', for you have stated about every number that it is something that cannot be free of one of these two descriptions. The difference between it and the preceding, i.e., the third, is that this is the preventing of both conjunction and disjunction (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww), while that is preventing conjunction only (man'at al-jam' without al-khuluww), for it is possible for both sides to be false if the truth is in some of the whole without some [others], although they can never be true together. As for the reason for the difference, it is that the judgment in the third pertains to the universality of number, because the subject is the universality of number, while in the fourth [it pertains] to the nature of number, because the subject is the nature. Then the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, can be reduced to the categorical, specifically when it is conjunctive and its antecedent shares the subject with the consequent, as in our saying: 'Whenever a body is in motion by will, it is sentient'; it is in force of our saying: 'Every body in motion by will is sentient.'
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of particles used in conditional propositions (shartiyyat) and then an analysis of how the position of the particle relative to the subject yields four types of conditionals. The author begins by stating that particles used in conditionals are multiple, falling into three categories: those indicating implication (luzum), those indicating incompatibility ('inad), and those indicating neither. Focusing first on conjunctive conditionals (muttasilat), the author examines specific particles: 'in' (إن) is very strong in indicating implication because the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent; 'mata' (متى) is weak; 'idh' (إذ) is moderate; 'idha' (إذا) and 'kullama' (كلما) do not indicate implication at all; 'lama' (لما) is ambiguous, suitable for both implication and non-implication. The author then notes that the antecedent in a conjunctive merely denotes the positing (al-wad'), not actual existence. After clarifying that other particles are not discussed because they belong to grammar, the author turns to the position of the particle relative to the subject (al-mawdu') in both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, which yields four divisions: (1) conjunctive with particle before the subject (pure conjunctive), (2) conjunctive with particle after the subject (close to categorical), (3) disjunctive with particle before the subject (pure disjunctive), and (4) disjunctive with particle after the subject (in force of categorical). Examples are provided for each. The author then explains the difference between the third and fourth divisions: in the third, it is a 'man'at al-jam'' (preventing conjunction only), while in the fourth, it is a 'man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww' (preventing both conjunction and disjunction), because the judgment in the third pertains to the universality of number (i.e., the whole class) whereas in the fourth it pertains to the nature of number. Finally, the author remarks that any conditional can be reduced to a categorical when the antecedent and consequent share the same subject, as in the example 'Whenever a body is in motion by will, it is sentient' reducing to 'Every body in motion by will is sentient.' Thus the passage instructs the reader on the nuanced semantics of conditional particles and the structural classification of conditionals based on particle placement.

Affirmative and Negative Conjunctive Conditionals
Author defines affirmative and negative conditional propositions and begins to discuss their divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an affirmative conjunctive conditional from a negative one by identifying whether it asserts or denies implication (luzum).

Core Idea
Affirmative conditionals assert that one proposition implies another, regardless of existential status.

This lesson unpacks the basic definition of affirmative and negative conjunctive conditionals. The author makes a crucial point: the implication (luzum) that defines an affirmative conditional does not depend on whether the propositions are about existence (wujūdī) or non-existence ('adamī). Understanding this will help you accurately classify conditional statements in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of implication
Look for the core judgment: does the proposition assert that one thing necessarily follows from another? That is the luzum.
The implication is the relation, not the truth of the parts.
2
Determine if the implication is affirmed or denied
If the proposition asserts the implication, it is affirmative (mūjiba). If it denies the implication, it is negative (sāliba).
Negation here removes the luzum, not necessarily the truth of the components.
3
Consider the nature of the propositions
The author notes that the implication holds whether the antecedent and consequent are existential, non-existential, or neither. Do not confuse 'affirmative' with 'existential'.
A proposition about non-existence (e.g. 'If there is no creator, then there is no world') can still be an affirmative conditional.
4
Classify the conditional
Based on steps 1-3, decide if the proposition is an affirmative or negative conjunctive conditional.
The passage focuses only on conjunctives (muttasilāt); disjunctives are treated separately.

Source Text

Section: [On the affirmative and negative of conjunctives and disjunctives] As for the affirmative of conjunctives, it is that which involves the judgment of the implication of one proposition by another, whether the implied and the implying are existential, or non-existential, or neither existential nor non-existential; and that is of two divisions as their story has preceded before. And since affirmation in conjunctives is the establishment of this implication, negation in them is the removal [of that implication].
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the affirmative and negative forms of conditional propositions, both conjunctive (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). The author first defines the affirmative of conjunctive conditionals: it is a statement that judges that one proposition implies another (luzum). This implication holds whether the two propositions are existential (wujudiyyan), non-existential (‘adamiyyan), or neither existential nor non-existential (i.e., modal or other). The author then notes that affirmative conditionals are divided into two types, referring back to an earlier discussion ('as their story has preceded'). Next, the author begins to define the negative conjunctive, stating that since affirmation is the establishment (ithbat) of this implication, negation is its removal (raf'). The text cuts off here, so the full discussion of negative conditionals and the treatment of disjunctives are not included in this window. The passage sets up the basic distinction: affirmative conditional asserts a necessary relation, negative conditional denies it.

Conjunctive Conditionals: Particle Before vs. After Subject
Author classifies conjunctive conditionals into two subtypes based on particle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conjunctive conditional has the particle before or after the subject, and explain how the second type approximates a categorical proposition.

Core Idea
The position of the conditional particle changes the logical form: before subject is pure conditional, after subject is categorical in disguise.

The author distinguishes two types of conjunctive conditionals based on where the particle 'in' (if) or 'kullama' (whenever) appears. Understanding this distinction is crucial for unpacking the logical structure of conditionals used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conditional particle
In the Arabic sentence, find the word 'إن' (if) or 'كلما' (whenever). This is the connective particle.
The particle signals that the sentence is a conditional.
2
Check if the particle is before or after the subject
The subject is the first noun phrase. If the particle comes before it, it is type 1 (pure conditional). If after, it is type 2 (close to categorical).
For example, 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة' has 'إن' before 'الشمس' (the sun).
3
Convert type 2 to categorical form
Rewrite 'S, whenever it is A, it is B' as 'Every S that is A is B'. This shows the proximity to categorical propositions.
This conversion is a key analytical operation used in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتصلات الخالصة كقولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وأما الثاني وهو الذي يكون حرف الاتصال فيه بعد الموضوع، فذلك قريب من الحملي كقولنا الشمس كلما كانت طالعة كان النهار موجودًا، فإنك أخَّرت عن الشمس بأن حكمها كذا. وأما الثالث وهو الذي يكون حرف الانفصال فيه قبل الموضوع، فذلك من المنفصلات الخالصة كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد زوجا وإما أن يكون كل عدد فردًا. وأما الرابع وهو الذي يكون حرف الانفصال فيه بعد الموضوع، فذلك في قوة الحملي كقولنا كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا، فإنك أخرتَ عن كل عدد أنه شيء من شأنه أن لا يخلو عن أحد هذين الوصفين فالفرق بينه وبين ما تقدم وهو الثالث أن هذا هو المانعة من الجمع والخلو؛ وذلك هو المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو، فإنه يمكن اجتماع الطرفين فيه على الكذب إذا كان الصدق في البعض من الكل دون البعض، وإن كان لا يمكن اجتماعهما على الصدق البتة. وأما لمية الفرق فهي أن الحكم في الثالث يتعلق لكلية العدد؛ إذ المورد هو كلية العدد، وفي الرابع بطبيعة العدد؛ إذ المورد هو الطبيعة. ثمَّ الشرطية متصلة كانت أو منفصلة فإنه يمكن ردها إلى الحملية على الخصوص إذا كانت متّصلة يكون المقدم فيها مشاركًا للتالي في الموضوع كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الجسم متحركًا بالإرادة فهو حساس فإنه في قوة قولنا كل جسم متحركًا بالإرادة حساس.
The pure conjunctive [conditionals] like our saying: If the sun is risen then daytime exists. As for the second, which is [the case] where the conjunctive particle occurs after the subject, that is close to categorical, like our saying: The sun whenever it is risen, daytime exists, for you have delayed [the judgment] from the sun that its judgment is such. As for the third, which is [the case] where the disjunctive particle occurs before the subject, that is of the pure disjunctive conditionals, like our saying: Either every number is even or every number is odd. As for the fourth, which is [the case] where the disjunctive particle occurs after the subject, that is in the force of a categorical, like our saying: Every number is either even or odd, for you have delayed from every number that it is something whose nature is to not be free of one of these two descriptions. The difference between it and what preceded, i.e., the third, is that this (the fourth) is the [type] that prevents both conjunction and emptiness (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw); whereas that (the third) is the [type] that prevents conjunction only (man'at al-jam' duna al-khuluw), for it is possible for both extremes to be false together if the truth is in some of the whole not in some [other], although it is impossible for both to be true together at all. As for the reason for the difference, it is that the judgment in the third pertains to the universality of number, since the subject is the universality of number; and in the fourth pertains to the nature of number, since the subject is the nature. Then, the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, can be reduced to a categorical in particular: if it is conjunctive where the antecedent shares the subject with the consequent, as in our saying: Whenever a body moves by will, it is sentient, for it is equivalent to our saying: Every body moving by will is sentient.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of conditional propositions into four types based on the position of the conditional particle (حرف الاتصال or حرف الانفصال) relative to the subject. The author first distinguishes pure conjunctive conditionals where the particle 'in' (if) occurs before the subject, like 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists.' The second type has the conjunctive particle after the subject, like 'The sun whenever it is risen, daytime exists,' which is close to a categorical universal affirmative. Similarly, for disjunctives, the third type has the disjunctive particle before the subject, e.g., 'Either every number is even or every number is odd,' which is a pure disjunction that prevents conjunction (man'at al-jam') but allows both to be false. The fourth type has the particle after the subject, e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd,' which is in the force of a categorical and prevents both conjunction and emptiness (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw). The author explains the difference: in the third type, the judgment applies to the universality of number, so it is possible that both disjuncts are false if truth is in some numbers; whereas in the fourth, the judgment applies to the nature of number, so each individual number must be either even or odd, and both cannot be false together. Finally, the author notes that conditionals can be reduced to categoricals when the antecedent and consequent share the same subject, as in 'Every body moving by will is sentient.'

Key Takeaway
The position of the connective particle in a conjunctive conditional determines whether it is a pure conditional or one that can be reduced to a categorical universal.

Disjunctive Conditionals: Exclusive vs. Exhaustive Types
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals into two subtypes based on particle position and logical force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of disjunctive conditionals based on particle position and logical force (man'at al-jam' vs. man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), and explain the difference using the example of number parity.

Core Idea
The logical force of a disjunctive conditional changes dramatically based on whether the particle is before or after the subject.

The author extends the classification to disjunctives, revealing that the particle's position determines whether the disjunction prevents both disjuncts from being true (only) or also prevents both from being false. This distinction is vital for accurate logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive particle
In the Arabic, look for 'إما' (either) which signals a disjunction.
The particle appears at the beginning of the first disjunct.
2
Check if the particle is before or after the subject
If 'إما' appears before the subject (the first noun), it is type 3. If after the subject, it is type 4.
Example: 'إما أن يكون كل عدد زوجا' has 'إما' before 'كل عدد' (every number) -> type 3.
3
Determine the logical force
Type 3 (particle before) is man'at al-jam' duna al-khuluw: both disjuncts cannot be true simultaneously, but both can be false. Type 4 (particle after) is man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw: both cannot be true and both cannot be false.
Apply the test: can both be false? If yes (while both cannot be true), it's third type. If no, it's fourth type.
4
Understand the reason for the difference
In type 3, the judgment applies to the universality of the subject (collectively), so it is possible that neither universal statement holds if truth is distributed. In type 4, the judgment applies to the nature of each individual, so each individual must have one of the two properties, making both false impossible.
The author's example: 'Either every number is even or every number is odd' (type 3) vs. 'Every number is either even or odd' (type 4).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definitions and the Critical Distinction: Negation of Implication vs. Implication of Negation
Definitions and the key distinction between negation of implication and implication of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define affirmative and negative conjunctive conditionals and correctly identify the contradictory (naqid) as the negation of implication, not the implication of negation.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a conditional is not 'if P then not Q' but 'P does not imply Q'.

This lesson tackles the foundational definitions of conditionals in classical Islamic logic. The author carefully distinguishes between two easily confused operations: negating an implication (salb al-luzum) and asserting an implication of negation (luzum al-salb). Understanding this difference is crucial for accurate logical reasoning, especially when dealing with conditional statements in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of an affirmative conjunctive conditional
Find how the author defines an affirmative conjunctive: it is a judgment of implication between two propositions. The implicant and implied can be existential, non-existential, or neither.
2
Understand what negation means
Since affirmation is establishing implication, negation is simply removing that implication. Notice that negation does not involve the truth values of the parts.
3
Distinguish between 'negation of implication' and 'implication of negation'
The author explicitly contrasts these. 'Negation of implication' (salb al-luzum) is a genuine negation, while 'implication of negation' (luzum al-salb) is itself an affirmative claim about a necessary connection.
4
Identify the contradictory (naqid) of an affirmative conjunctive
The contradictory is that the consequent is not necessary for the antecedent, not that the non-existence of the consequent is necessary for the antecedent.
5
Extend the same logic to disjunctive conditionals
For disjunctives, affirmation is judgment of mutual contrariety, and negation is removing that contrariety. The principle remains the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الموجبة والسالبة من المتصلات والمنفصلات ] أما الموجبة من المتّصلات فإنها عبارة عما يكون الحكم فيها بلزوم قضية لأخرى سواء كان اللازم والملزوم وجوديين، أو عدميين، أو لا وجوديين ولا عدميين؛ وذلك على قسمين كما سبقت قصتها من قبل. ولما كان الإيجاب في المتّصلات عبارة عن إثبات هذا اللزوم كان السلب فيها عبارة عن رفع هذا اللزوم. والفرق بين سلب اللزوم ولزوم السلب ظاهر لما كان الأول منهما في حكم السلب، والثاني في حكم الإيجاب، فنقيض المتصلة الموجبة هو أن يحكم بأن ذلك التالي ليس بلازم لذلك المقدم لا أن يحكم بأن عدم ذلك التالي لازم لذلك المقدم، فإن ذلك في حكم الإيجاب. وكذلك القول في المنفصلات فإن الإيجاب فيها عبارة عن الحكم بالمعاندة بين المقدم فيها والتالي فيكون السلب عبارة عن رفع تلك المعاندة. وكما أن السلب والإيجاب ليستا لسلب الأجزاء وإيجابها فكذلك الصدق والكذب ليستا لصدق الأجزاء وكذبها : فالصادقة من المتّصلات قد تكون من صادقتين، وقد تكون من كاذبتين، وقد تكون من مختلفتين صادقة وكاذبة أو كاذبة وصادقة، وقد تكون لا من صادقة على التعين ولا من كاذبة كذلك: أما الأول فظاهر وأما الثاني فكذلك؛ لأنه متى لزمت صادقة صادقة كان نقيض المقدم لازمًا لنقيض التالي وهما كاذبتان، فلو قلت مثلا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا. فإنها صادقة من صادقتين وقد لزم من صدق هذه القضية صدق قولنا كلما لم يكن النهار موجودًا لم تكن الشمس طالعة وهي صادقة أيضًا. ثم لقائل أن يقول الصدق في الأولى لا يكون متعينا، والكذب في الثانية كذلك؛ وقد كانت كل واحدة من القضيتين في الأولى والثانية قابلة لأن تكون صادقة وقابلة لأن تكون كاذبة . لكنا نقول هذا مسلم لكنه لا يمتنع أن يكون الصدق في الأولى دون الثانية فيفرض في مثل هذه الصورة. فلو كان الأمر على العكس لكان الحكم على العكس أيضًا، وقد صدق قولنا متى لزمت صادقة صادقة كان نقيض هذه الصادقة لازمة لنقيض تلك الصادقة كما مر .
Section on affirmative and negative of conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals. As for the affirmative of conjunctive conditionals, it is an expression of the judgment of the implication of one proposition by another, whether the implicant and the implied are existential, or non-existential, or neither existential nor non-existential; and that is of two types as their story has preceded before. And since affirmation in conjunctive conditionals is an expression of establishing this implication, negation in them is an expression of removing this implication. And the difference between negation of implication and implication of negation is apparent, since the first of them is in the judgment of negation, and the second is in the judgment of affirmation. So the contradictory of an affirmative conjunctive conditional is that one judges that the consequent is not necessary for that antecedent, not that one judges that the non-existence of that consequent is necessary for that antecedent, for that is in the judgment of affirmation. And likewise the statement concerning disjunctive conditionals: for affirmation in them is an expression of the judgment of mutual contrariety (mu'anada) between the antecedent and the consequent, so negation is an expression of removing that contrariety. And just as negation and affirmation are not [dependent on] the negation and affirmation of the parts, so too truth and falsity are not [dependent on] the truth and falsity of the parts. For a true conjunctive conditional may be from two true [parts], and may be from two false, and may be from two different ones: true and false, or false and true, and may be not from a determinate true nor from a determinate false likewise. As for the first, it is obvious; as for the second likewise, because whenever a true [proposition] implies a true [proposition], the contradictory of the antecedent becomes necessary for the contradictory of the consequent, and both are false. So if you say, for example, 'Whenever the sun is risen, daytime exists,' it is true from two true propositions, and from the truth of this proposition follows the truth of our saying 'Whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not risen,' and it is also true. Then one may say: the truth in the first is not determinate, and the falsity in the second is likewise; and each of the two propositions in the first and second is capable of being true and capable of being false. But we say: this is granted, but it does not prevent that the truth is in the first without the second, so it is assumed in such a form. If the matter were reversed, the judgment would also be reversed, and our saying is true: whenever a true [proposition] implies a true [proposition], then the contradictory of this true [proposition] is necessary for the contradictory of that true [proposition] as has passed.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This section opens a new discussion on affirmative and negative conditionals. The author defines the affirmative conjunctive conditional as a judgment that one proposition implies another, regardless of whether the components are existential or non-existential. Negation is then defined as the removal of this implication claim. A critical distinction is drawn between 'negation of implication' (salb al-luzum) and 'implication of negation' (luzum al-salb): the former is a genuine negation of the conditional, while the latter is itself an affirmative claim about a necessary connection. Thus, the contradictory of an affirmative conjunctive is simply that the consequent is not necessary for the antecedent, not that the contradictory of the consequent is necessary for the antecedent. The same applies to disjunctives, where affirmation is judgment of mutual contrariety between parts. The author then explains that truth and falsity of conditionals do not derive from the truth or falsity of their components. A true conjunctive may have two true parts, two false parts, or a mix, and may even be indeterminate in its parts. An example is given: 'Whenever the sun is risen, daytime exists' is true with both true parts, and it implies its contrapositive. An objection is raised that the truth of the first is not determinate, but the author counters that it does not matter; the logical relationship holds regardless. The section underscores the independence of conditional truth from the truth values of its atomic parts.

سلب اللزوم

Key Takeaway
The negation of a conditional is simply denying the implication, not asserting a negative implication.

Truth Conditions of Conjunctive Conditionals: Independence from Parts
Truth conditions of conjunctive conditionals and independence from part truth values.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the truth of a conjunctive conditional does not depend on the truth values of its components and identify the possible combinations of truth values for the antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional can be true even when both its parts are false – only the implication matters.

This lesson explores a key insight: the truth or falsity of a conditional statement is separate from the truth or falsity of its individual parts. The author provides examples and addresses a subtle objection. This understanding is essential for correctly evaluating conditional proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
The author states that truth and falsity of conditionals are not dependent on the truth and falsity of their parts. This is analogous to negation and affirmation not depending on the parts.
2
List the possible truth-value combinations
The author enumerates: a true conjunctive can come from two true parts, two false parts, one true and one false (both orders), and even cases where the truth or falsity is not determinate.
3
Analyze the example
The example 'Whenever the sun is risen, daytime exists' is true with both parts true. Its contrapositive 'Whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not risen' is also true.
4
Understand the objection and response
An objector says the truth in the first is not determinate and the falsity in the second is likewise, and each part could be true or false. The author responds that this does not prevent the truth of the conditional; the logical relationship holds regardless.
5
Apply to a new example
Consider a conditional like 'If a person is qualified, then they may testify.' Determine its truth based on the implication, not on the actual truth of the antecedent or consequent.

Distinguishing Negation of Implication from Implication of Negation
Author distinguishes between negating implication and implying negation in conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'negation of implication' (salb al-luzum) and 'implication of negation' (luzum al-salb) in conditional propositions and correctly identify the contradictory of a given conditional or disjunctive statement.

Core Idea
Negating an implication is not the same as implying a negation; the former is negative, the latter affirmative.

In Islamic logic, it is crucial to know exactly what it means to negate a conditional proposition. A common mistake is to confuse 'denying that B follows from A' with 'affirming that not-B follows from A.' The author of this passage clarifies that the first operation belongs to negation and the second to affirmation, using conditional (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions as examples. Mastering this distinction protects your reasoning from a subtle but serious fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two operations
Locate the two phrases 'salb al-luzum' (negation of implication) and 'luzum al-salb' (implication of negation) in the Arabic. The author states the first is in the category of negation (salb) and the second in affirmation (ijab).
2
Apply to a conditional example
Take an affirmative conditional like 'Whenever it rains, the ground is wet.' The contradictory (naqid) is the negation of the implication: 'It is not the case that whenever it rains, the ground is wet.' This is 'salb al-luzum'. The other statement 'Whenever it rains, the ground is not wet' is 'luzum al-salb' and is actually an affirmative claim.
Do not confuse the two; they have opposite truth conditions.
3
Extend to disjunctive propositions
For a disjunctive like 'Either this action is wajib or it is mubah,' the affirmation is the judgment of opposition (mu'anada) between the two parts. Its negation is the removal of that opposition: 'It is not the case that either this action is wajib or it is mubah.' This is the correct contradictory, not 'Either this action is not wajib or it is not mubah,' which is affirmative.
4
Check the logical type of each operation
Remember: 'salb al-luzum' is a negative judgment (it denies a relation), while 'luzum al-salb' is an affirmative judgment (it asserts a negative relation). Use this as a litmus test when analyzing any conditional claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بين سلب اللزوم ولزوم السلب ظاهر لما كان الأول منهما في حكم السلب، والثاني في حكم الإيجاب، فنقيض المتصلة الموجبة هو أن يحكم بأن ذلك التالي ليس بلازم لذلك المقدم لا أن يحكم بأن عدم ذلك التالي لازم لذلك المقدم، فإن ذلك في حكم الإيجاب. وكذلك القول في المنفصلات فإن الإيجاب فيها عبارة عن الحكم بالمعاندة بين المقدم فيها والتالي فيكون السلب عبارة عن رفع تلك المعاندة.
And the difference between the negation of implication and the implication of negation is apparent because the first of them is in the category of negation, and the second is in the category of affirmation. So the contradictory of the affirmative conditional is that one judges that the consequent is not implied by that antecedent, not that one judges that the non-existence of that consequent is implied by that antecedent, for that is in the category of affirmation. And likewise the statement concerning disjunctives, for the affirmation in them is an expression of the judgment of opposition between the antecedent and the consequent, so the negation is an expression of the removal of that opposition.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the critical distinction between two logical operations often confused in conditional and disjunctive propositions. The author begins by stating that the difference between 'salb al-luzum' (negation of implication) and 'luzum al-salb' (implication of negation) is evident. The former belongs to the category of negation (salb), i.e., denying that a consequent follows from an antecedent, while the latter belongs to affirmation (ijab), i.e., asserting that the negation of the consequent follows from the antecedent. To clarify, the author explains that the contradictory (naqid) of an affirmative conditional proposition (mutasila muthbata) is the judgment that the consequent is not implied by the antecedent, not the judgment that the non-existence of the consequent is implied by the antecedent. For example, if the original conditional is 'Whenever it rains, the ground is wet,' its contradictory is 'It is not the case that whenever it rains, the ground is wet' – not 'Whenever it rains, the ground is not wet,' which is an affirmative claim. The same principle applies to disjunctive propositions (munfasilat), where affirmation is the judgment of mutual opposition (mu'anada) between antecedent and consequent, and negation is the removal of that opposition. Thus, the contradictory of 'Either this is wajib or it is mubah' is 'It is not the case that either this is wajib or it is mubah,' not 'Either this is not wajib or it is not mubah' (which is also an affirmative). This nuance is crucial for correct logical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Key Terms
سلب اللزوم
لزوم السلب
المتصلة
المنفصلة
المعاندة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The ground is wet only if it rains.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of an affirmative conditional is the negation of the implication, not the implication of the negation; the former is a negative judgment, the latter is affirmative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Five Types of True Conditional Propositions
Author lists five types of true conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any true conditional proposition into one of five types based on the truth values of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Truth of a conditional is about implication, not the truth of its parts.

This passage establishes a crucial principle: the truth or falsity of a conditional proposition depends on the implication relation between antecedent and consequent, not on the actual truth of those parts. The author then lists five types of true conditionals, each with distinct characteristics. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic logic, especially in usul al-fiqh where conditionals are used in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key principle
Notice that the author begins by analogizing conditional truth to the independence of negation/affirmation from parts. The truth of a conditional is about the relation of implication (luzum), not the truth values of antecedent and consequent individually.
This principle is fundamental: a conditional can be true even if both parts are false, as long as the implication holds.
2
Learn the five types
The passage enumerates five types: (1) both parts true, (2) both parts false, (3) true antecedent implying false consequent (impossible), (4) false antecedent implying true consequent, (5) parts with indeterminate truth values. Focus on the examples given for each.
Type 3 is deemed impossible, while type 4 is possible. Type 5 is common in everyday language.
3
Analyze the examples
For each type, the author provides an example. Work through the example for type 4: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is animal' – antecedent false, consequent true, but implication holds because being a horse necessitates being an animal. Understand why type 3 (true implies false) cannot occur.
Type 2 example uses contrapositive: the original conditional 'sun rising implies daytime' has both true parts, but its contrapositive has both false parts (when sun not rising, daytime not existing) yet the conditional is true.
4
Distinguish determinate vs indeterminate
The author discusses that in some conditionals, truth values of parts are not fixed (type 5). For example, 'If Abdallah writes, he moves his hand' – the truth of the conditional depends on whether the implication holds in reality, not on whether Abdallah is actually writing.
This shows that conditionals can be true even when we don't know the truth values of the parts, as long as the implication relation is established.
5
Apply the classification
Given a new conditional statement, identify whether it is true based on the implication relation, then determine which of the five types it falls into. Practice with Islamic examples.
Be careful: type 3 is impossible, so any conditional with a true antecedent and false consequent is automatically false.
