وكما أن السلب والإيجاب ليستا لسلب الأجزاء وإيجابها فكذلك الصدق والكذب ليستا لصدق الأجزاء وكذبها : فالصادقة من المتّصلات قد تكون من صادقتين، وقد تكون من كاذبتين، وقد تكون من مختلفتين صادقة وكاذبة أو كاذبة وصادقة، وقد تكون لا من صادقة على التعين ولا من كاذبة كذلك: أما الأول فظاهر وأما الثاني فكذلك؛ لأنه متى لزمت صادقة صادقة كان نقيض المقدم لازمًا لنقيض التالي وهما كاذبتان، فلو قلت مثلا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا. فإنها صادقة من صادقتين وقد لزم من صدق هذه القضية صدق قولنا كلما لم يكن النهار موجودًا لم تكن الشمس طالعة وهي صادقة أيضًا. ثم لقائل أن يقول الصدق في الأولى لا يكون متعينا، والكذب في الثانية كذلك؛ وقد كانت كل واحدة من القضيتين في الأولى والثانية قابلة لأن تكون صادقة وقابلة لأن تكون كاذبة . لكنا نقول هذا مسلم لكنه لا يمتنع أن يكون الصدق في الأولى دون الثانية فيفرض في مثل هذه الصورة. فلو كان الأمر على العكس لكان الحكم على العكس أيضًا، وقد صدق قولنا متى لزمت صادقة صادقة كان نقيض هذه الصادقة لازمة لنقيض تلك الصادقة كما مر . وأما الثالث فذلك من جملة ما لا يمكن أن يتحقق فإن من المحال أن تكون الكاذبة لازمة للصادقة. وإن قيل إنه ضرورة تدعو إلى التعرُّض لهذا المثال؛ وقد كان من الأمثلة ما يكون الصدق فيه معينًا، والكذب كذلك كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان موجودا كان الحيوان موجودا فكلما لم يكن الحيوان موجودًا لم يكن الإنسان موجودا. فنقول الكلام في مثل هذا المثال نحو الكلام في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره. وذلك لأن الصدق لا يكون معينا في الأولى دون الثانية إلا في البعض من الأزمان وكما أن الصدق في الأولى دون الثانية معين في البعض من الأزمان، فكذلك عليه وهو الصدق في الثانية دون الأولى في غير ذلك البعض من الأزمان. فلا يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان فرسًا ولا يلزم من كون الشيء حيوانًا أن يكون فرسا. وأما الرابع وهو عكسه فذلك من جملة ما يمكن أن يتحقق كقولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان حيوانا؛ إذ الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون فرسًا إلا وأن يكون حيوانا. ثم التالي يمكن أن يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى المقدم؛ والأعم يمكن أن يتحقق بدون الأخص فلو كان الأخص متحققاً في مثل هذه الصورة تحقيقا أو تقديرًا كان الأعم متحققا كذلك. وأما الخامس وهو الذي لا يتعين فيه الصدق والكذب فذلك مثل ما يقال إن كان عبد الله يكتب فإنه يُحرّك يده.
And just as negation and affirmation are not regarding the negation and affirmation of the parts, likewise truth and falsity are not regarding the truth and falsity of the parts: for the true conditionals may be from two true parts, or from two false parts, or from two different parts, one true and one false, or from neither determinate true nor false likewise. As for the first, it is obvious. As for the second, likewise; because whenever a true implies a true, the contradictory of the antecedent implies the contradictory of the consequent, and both are false. For example, if you say 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists,' it is true from two true parts, and from the truth of this proposition follows the truth of our statement 'Whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not rising,' which is also true. Then one might say that truth in the first is not determinate, and falsity in the second likewise; each of the two propositions in the first and second is capable of being true and capable of being false. But we say: this is conceded, but it does not prevent truth being in the first without the second, so it is posited in such a case. If the matter were the opposite, the judgment would also be opposite, and our statement holds: whenever a true implies a true, the contradictory of this true is implied by the contradictory of that true, as has passed. As for the third, that is among what cannot be realized, for it is impossible for a false to be implied by a true. And if it is said that necessity calls for presenting this example, and there are examples where truth is determinate and falsity likewise, such as our statement 'Whenever man exists, animal exists,' so 'Whenever animal does not exist, man does not exist.' We say: the discussion about such an example is similar to the discussion about the example that passed. That is because truth is not determinate in the first without the second except in some times, and just as truth in the first without the second is determinate in some times, so likewise upon it, which is truth in the second without the first in other times. So it is not said 'Whenever man is animal, he is a horse,' and it is not necessary from a thing being animal that it be a horse. As for the fourth, and it is its converse, that is among what can be realized, like our statement 'Whenever man is a horse, he is animal'; for a thing cannot be a horse unless it is animal. Then the consequent may be more general relative to the antecedent; and the more general can be realized without the more specific, so if the more specific is realized in such a case actually or hypothetically, the more general is realized likewise. As for the fifth, which is that in which truth and falsity are not determinate, that is like what is said: 'If Abdallah writes, then he moves his hand.'
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage begins by establishing a key principle: just as negation and affirmation in a proposition do not depend on the negation or affirmation of its parts, similarly the truth or falsity of a conditional does not depend on the truth or falsity of its antecedent and consequent individually. The author then proceeds to enumerate five types of true conditional propositions (al-sadiqa min al-muttasilat). The first type is a conditional composed of two true parts, e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' The second is composed of two false parts, e.g., the contrapositive of the first: 'Whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not rising' (both parts false when the original is true). The author notes that one might object that truth/falsity is not determinate in these examples, but he responds that it is possible to assume a specific time where the first is true and the second false, etc. The third type, a true conditional where a false antecedent implies a true consequent, is deemed impossible because a false cannot be implied by a true. The author discusses an example like 'Whenever man exists, animal exists' and its contrapositive, showing that truth is not absolute but temporally relative. The fourth type is the converse of the third: a true conditional where a true antecedent implies a false consequent? Actually the fourth is 'its converse' (عكسه) meaning the reverse of the third? The author gives an example: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is animal' – this is true because if the impossible (man being horse) occurred, then animality follows. This is a conditional where the antecedent is false and the consequent is true, but it is true because the implication holds (if antecedent, then consequent). The fifth type is where truth/falsity is not determinate, like 'If Abdallah writes, then he moves his hand' – the truth of the conditional depends on whether the antecedent actually implies the consequent, not on the actual truth values of the parts. This classification is essential for understanding how conditionals function in logical arguments, especially in Islamic legal reasoning where conditional premises are common.

C) Because the author says so
D) Because falsity cannot be implied by truth, as that would violate the nature of implication

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional proposition is determined solely by the implication relation between antecedent and consequent, allowing for five distinct types based on the truth values of the parts.

The Five Types of False Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates five types of false conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify false conditional propositions into the five types based on the truth values of their antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional can be false even when both parts are true; the key is the failure of implication.

In the previous lesson, we examined true conditionals. Now we turn to false ones. The author shows that false conditionals fall into the same five categories based on the truth values of antecedent and consequent. The crucial difference is that the implication does not hold. Understanding these categories helps us avoid logical errors in arguments, especially in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the five divisions of conditionals
Remember the five combinations of truth values: both true, both false, true antecedent and false consequent, false antecedent and true consequent, and indeterminate.
These five types apply to both true and false conditionals.
2
Identify the first type of false conditional
The first type is a false conditional from two true parts. Example in the text: 'Whenever man exists, a horse exists.' Both parts can be true, but the conditional is false because the implication does not hold.
The truth of the parts does not guarantee the truth of the conditional.
3
Understand why the implication fails
The conditional asserts a necessary connection between antecedent and consequent. Here, man's existence does not necessitate a horse's existence. The failure of this necessary connection makes the conditional false.
This is the core insight: falsity in conditionals is about the relation, not the parts.
4
Generalize to the remaining types
The other four types follow the same pattern. For example, from two false parts, from true antecedent and false consequent, etc. In each case, the conditional is false because the implication relation fails.
The classification helps in identifying invalid arguments in dialectical contexts.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Whenever fasting is obligatory, the sun rises.' Both antecedent and consequent are true at many times, but the implication is false because fasting does not cause sunrise.
This example illustrates the first type using an Islamic context.

Source Text

وأما الكاذبة منها أي من المتصلات فإنها منقسمة إلى الأقسام الخمسة التي مر ذكرها. وذلك لأنها من صادقتين، أو من كاذبتين، أو من صادقة وكاذبة أو من كاذبة وصادقة، أو ما لا يتعين فيه الصدق والكذب لا في المقدم منه ولا في التالي: أما الأول فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان موجودا كان الفرس
As for the false ones, i.e., from among the conditionals, they are divided into the five divisions that have been mentioned. That is because they are from two true (parts), or from two false (parts), or from a true and a false, or from a false and a true, or what is not determinate in truth and falsity neither in its antecedent nor in its consequent. As for the first, that is like our statement: 'Whenever man exists, a horse exists.'

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of false conditional propositions (al-kadhiba min al-muttasilat). The author states that false conditionals are divided into the same five categories previously mentioned for true conditionals, based on the truth values of the antecedent and consequent. The first type is a false conditional composed of two true parts, exemplified by 'Whenever man exists, a horse exists.' Although both 'man exists' and 'horse exists' are true at many times, the implication is false because the existence of man does not necessitate the existence of a horse. This illustrates that the falsity of a conditional arises from a failure of implication, not from the falsity of its components. The text begins the listing but is cut off; the remaining types (two false parts, true antecedent and false consequent, false antecedent and true consequent, and indeterminate) would follow the pattern of the true conditionals but with the opposite truth condition for the implication. This classification is important for dialectical and demonstrative contexts where one must identify false premises or invalid implications.

Three Categories of True Conditionals
Author explains the third, fourth, and fifth categories of true conditionals with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given conditional proposition into one of the five categories and explain why the third type is impossible.

Core Idea
A true conditional demands necessary entailment, not mere temporal coincidence.

In this passage, the author completes the classification of true conditional propositions (al-muttasilat al-sadiqa) by explaining the third, fourth, and fifth types. The key insight is that truth values alone do not determine a conditional's validity; what matters is whether the consequent is logically necessary given the antecedent (luzum). Understanding this distinction helps you evaluate arguments in jurisprudence and theology that rely on conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third category
The author states that a true antecedent cannot necessitate a false consequent (impossible). Reflect on why: if the antecedent is true and the consequent false, the conditional 'if A then B' would be false because the truth of A would not guarantee B. This category is therefore impossible.
The example given is 'Whenever man exists, animal exists' — but this does not imply a necessary connection; it merely shows temporal coincidence.
2
Examine the fourth category
This is the converse of the third: a false antecedent can necessitate a true consequent (possible). For example, 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal' — even though the antecedent is false (no man is a horse), the conditional holds because if it were true, the consequent would follow.
The author notes that the consequent can be more general than the antecedent, which allows this relationship.
3
Understand the fifth category
Here, truth and falsehood are not fixed in either part. The example is 'If Abdullah writes, he moves his hand' — writing entails hand movement, but the hand movement could also occur without writing (e.g., gesturing), so the truth values are not uniquely determined.
This category highlights that a conditional can be true even when both parts are not always true together, as long as the entailment holds in the intended context.
4
Apply the luzum test
For any conditional, ask: Is the consequent truly necessary given the antecedent? If the antecedent can be true while the consequent is false, the conditional is false. This test separates genuine conditionals from accidental co-occurrences.
This is the core logical tool to evaluate conditionals in Islamic legal reasoning (e.g., if a contract is valid, then dower is due).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث فذلك من جملة ما لا يمكن أن يتحقق فإن من المحال أن تكون الكاذبة لازمة للصادقة. وإن قيل إنه ضرورة تدعو إلى التعرُّض لهذا المثال؛ وقد كان من الأمثلة ما يكون الصدق فيه معينًا، والكذب كذلك كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان موجودا كان الحيوان موجودا فكلما لم يكن الحيوان موجودًا لم يكن الإنسان موجودا. فنقول الكلام في مثل هذا المثال نحو الكلام في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره. وذلك لأن الصدق لا يكون معينا في الأولى دون الثانية إلا في البعض من الأزمان وكما أن الصدق في الأولى دون الثانية معين في البعض من الأزمان، فكذلك عليه وهو الصدق في الثانية دون الأولى في غير ذلك البعض من الأزمان. فلا يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان فرسًا ولا يلزم من كون الشيء حيوانًا أن يكون فرسا. وأما الرابع وهو عكسه فذلك من جملة ما يمكن أن يتحقق كقولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان حيوانا؛ إذ الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون فرسًا إلا وأن يكون حيوانا. ثم التالي يمكن أن يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى المقدم؛ والأعم يمكن أن يتحقق بدون الأخص فلو كان الأخص متحققاً في مثل هذه الصورة تحقيقا أو تقديرًا كان الأعم متحققا كذلك. وأما الخامس وهو الذي لا يتعين فيه الصدق والكذب فذلك مثل ما يقال إن كان عبد الله يكتب فإنه يُحرّك يده.
As for the third, that is among what cannot be actualized, for it is impossible for a false proposition to be necessary for a true one. And if it is said that it is necessary to expose this example; and there have been examples where truth is specific, and falsehood likewise, as in our statement: 'Whenever man exists, animal exists; so whenever animal does not exist, man does not exist.' Then we say: The discussion regarding such an example is similar to the discussion regarding the example that passed. That is because truth is not specific in the first without the second except in some times. And just as truth in the first without the second is specific in some times, likewise the opposite, which is truth in the second without the first, exists in other times. So it is not said: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is a horse', and it does not follow from a thing being an animal that it is a horse. As for the fourth, which is its converse, that is among what can be actualized, like our statement: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal'; for a thing cannot be a horse without being an animal. Then the consequent can be more general relative to the antecedent; and the more general can be actualized without the more specific, so if the more specific were actualized in such a form, actually or hypothetically, the more general would likewise be actualized. As for the fifth, which is where truth and falsehood are not specific, that is like what is said: 'If Abdullah writes, then he moves his hand.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage defines three categories of true conditional propositions (muttasilat sadiqa) among the five total. The author begins with the third type, which is impossible: a true antecedent cannot necessitate a false consequent. He discusses an example ('Whenever man exists, animal exists') to illustrate that temporal coincidence does not imply necessary entailment; truth may be specific to certain times without being universal. The fourth type is possible: a false antecedent leading to a true consequent (converse of the third). The example 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal' shows that if the antecedent is false, the consequent can still be true and the conditional holds. The author notes that the consequent can be more general than the antecedent. The fifth type is indeterminate, where truth and falsehood are not fixed in either part, as in 'If Abdullah writes, he moves his hand'—the action of moving the hand does not necessarily follow from writing in all cases. Throughout, the author uses examples from common knowledge (man, animal, horse) to clarify logical relationships, avoiding any secular or non-Islamic content.

Identifying False Conditionals: The Five Types
Author enumerates five types of false conditionals and distinguishes causal vs accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of false conditionals based on truth values of antecedent and consequent, and distinguish causal from accidental conditionals.

Core Idea
A conditional can be false even when both parts are true.

This passage completes the analysis of conditional propositions by examining false ones. The author systematically enumerates five types based on truth values of antecedent and consequent, then distinguishes causal (luzumiyya) from accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals. This classification is essential for evaluating the validity of conditional statements in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
List the five truth combinations
Identify the five criteria: both true, both false, true-false, false-true, and indeterminate (neither fixed). These are the bases for dividing false conditionals.
2
Match combinations to examples
For each combination, find the author's example: (1) both true: 'Whenever man exists, horse exists'; (2) both false: 'Whenever man is a horse, horse is a lion'; (3) true-false: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is a horse'; (4) false-true: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is rational'; (5) indeterminate: 'If Zayd writes, then he does not move his hand'.
3
Distinguish causal vs accidental
In causal (luzumiyya) conditionals, all five combinations yield false conditionals. In accidental (ittifaqiyya), the both-true case cannot produce a false conditional (since accidental connection makes it true).
4
Apply the distinction
Whenever you encounter a conditional, first determine if the connection is causal or accidental. If accidental and both parts are true, the conditional is automatically true. If causal, any truth combination can be false if the entailment is lacking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكاذبة منها أي من المتصلات فإنها منقسمة إلى الأقسام الخمسة التي مر ذكرها. وذلك لأنها من صادقتين، أو من كاذبتين، أو من صادقة وكاذبة أو من كاذبة وصادقة، أو ما لا يتعين فيه الصدق والكذب لا في المقدم منه ولا في التالي: أما الأول فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان موجودا كان الفرس موجودًا؛ وأما الثاني فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان الفرس أسدا؛ وأما الثالث فكقولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان فرسا كما مرَّ من قبل؛ وأما الرابع فكقولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان عاقلا؛ وأما الخامس فكقولنا إن كان زيد يكتب فإنه لا يُحرّك يده، هذا في اللزومية. وأما في الاتفاقية فالكاذبة من الصادقتين محال دون الكاذبتين فغيرهما من الأقسام المذكورة.
As for the false ones among them, i.e., among conditionals, they are divided into the five divisions that were previously mentioned. That is because they are from two true [propositions], or two false, or a true and a false, or a false and a true, or that in which truth and falsehood are not specific, neither in its antecedent nor consequent. As for the first, that is like our statement: 'Whenever man exists, horse exists.' As for the second, that is like our statement: 'Whenever man is a horse, horse is a lion.' As for the third, like our statement: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is a horse,' as passed before. As for the fourth, like our statement: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is rational.' As for the fifth, like our statement: 'If Zayd writes, then he does not move his hand.' This in the causal [luzumiyya]. As for the accidental [ittifaqiyya], the false from two true is impossible, but from two false and the other mentioned divisions [are possible].
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five types of false conditional propositions (kadhiba min al-muttasilat) in the causal (luzumiyya) sense. The author lists them based on the truth values of the antecedent and consequent: 1) both true, 2) both false, 3) first true second false, 4) first false second true, and 5) indeterminate (neither fixed). Examples are provided for each: 'Whenever man exists, horse exists' (both true, but false conditional because horse does not necessarily follow); 'Whenever man is a horse, horse is a lion' (both false, but the conditional is false because the entailment is invalid); 'Whenever man is an animal, he is a horse' (true antecedent, false consequent; false conditional); 'Whenever man is a horse, he is rational' (false antecedent, true consequent; false conditional); 'If Zayd writes, then he does not move his hand' (indeterminate, as writing may or may not involve hand movement). The author then distinguishes between causal (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals: in accidental conditionals, a false conditional with both true parts is impossible (since if both are true, the conditional would be true if there is any accidental connection), but false conditionals from two false or other combinations are possible. This classification helps in analyzing the validity of conditional statements in logical reasoning.

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
False conditionals arise from any of five truth combinations; in causal conditionals all are possible, while in accidental conditionals the both-true combination cannot yield false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: For each of the following conditionals, determine which of the five types it belongs to, assuming the truth values as they would be in reality: (a) 'Whenever the sun rises, it is day.' (b) 'Whenever a human is a stone, he is invisible.' (c) 'If the prayer is valid, then wudu was performed.' Identify whether each is causal or accidental.
?
Task 2: Explain why, in accidental conditionals, the statement 'If Zayd is writing, then he is moving his hand' cannot be false if both 'Zayd is writing' and 'he is moving his hand' are true.

Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions in Islamic Logic
Author classifies disjunctive propositions into three types and explains when each is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of disjunctive propositions (munfasilat) and determine whether each is necessarily true or can be false based on the truth values of its parts.

Core Idea
Disjunctives are not all equal: some allow both false, some allow both true, and one allows neither.

Disjunctive propositions ('either...or...' statements) are essential for reasoning about alternatives. In Islamic logic, they are classified into three types based on what combinations of truth values are allowed. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for constructing and evaluating arguments that involve alternatives, especially in legal (fiqh) and theological (kalam) reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
List the three types from the passage: man’at al-jam‘ wal-khulu (exclusive, exactly one true), man’at al-khulu (inclusive, at least one true), and man’at al-jam‘ (exclusive but not exhaustive, both can be false).
Memorize the Arabic names.
2
Understand the truth conditions for each type
For each type, determine which combinations of truth values (true/false) are allowed and which are prohibited. Man’at al-jam‘ wal-khulu prohibits both true and both false; man’at al-khulu prohibits both false; man’at al-jam‘ prohibits both true.
Use the truth table approach.
3
Apply the given examples
Analyze the author's examples: 'This thing is either not a human or an animal' (man’at al-khulu, always true) and 'This is either a tree or a stone' (man’at al-jam‘, false if the object is neither).
Explain why each example fits its type.
4
Test with your own Islamic example
Construct a disjunctive proposition from fiqh, e.g., 'This act is either wajib or haram.' Determine which type it is and whether it is necessarily true. (Answer: man’at al-jam‘ wal-khulu? Actually wajib and haram cannot both be true and cannot both be false if the act is legal, so it's man’at al-jam‘ wal-khulu, always true.)
Try other pairs like 'This food is halal or haram' (also exclusive).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في المنفصلات [أ] فالحقيقة منها وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو لا تكون كاذبة أصلا وإلا يلزم كذب النقيضين. [ب] وكذلك المانعة من الخلو فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء خاليا عن أحد جزأيها، كما في قولنا هذا الشيء إما لا إنسان وإما حيوان مثلا والشيء لا يخلو عن كونه حيوانًا أو لا يكون. [ت] وأما المانعة من الجمع فإنها قد تكون كاذبة وقد لا تكون كما في قولنا هذا إما شجر وإما حجر . فإنه إذا كان خاليًا عن كل واحد منهما فهي كاذبة وإلا فلا. وأما الأجزاء في الحقيقة فلا يمكن أن يكون الكل صادقا ولا أن يكون الكل كاذبًا بل الصادق هو الواحد ليس إلا، وفي المانعة من الجمع يمكن أن يكون الكل كاذبًا لكن لا يمكن أن يكون صادقًا، وفي المانعة من الخلو على العكس، وهذا ظاهر.
As for the disjunctives: [A] The real one, which is man’at al-jam‘ wal-khulu (preventing both conjunction and void), is never false, otherwise falsehood of both contradictories would follow. [B] Similarly, man’at al-khulu (preventing void), for it is impossible for a thing to be devoid of either of its two parts, as in our statement: 'This thing is either not a human or an animal,' for example, and the thing is not devoid of being an animal or not. [C] As for man’at al-jam‘ (preventing conjunction), it may be false and may not, as in our statement: 'This is either a tree or a stone.' For if it is devoid of both, it is false; otherwise not. As for the parts in the real [disjunctive], it is impossible for all to be true or all false; rather the true is exactly one. In man’at al-jam‘, it is possible for all to be false but impossible to be true; in man’at al-khulu, the opposite. And this is clear.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines three types of disjunctive propositions (munfasilat). The first is 'man’at al-jam‘ wal-khulu' (exclusive disjunction: neither both nor neither can be true), which is always true because it covers all possibilities without overlap or gap. The second is 'man’at al-khulu' (inclusive disjunction: at least one true, cannot have neither), which is also always true because the two parts are exhaustive contradictories. The third is 'man’at al-jam‘' (exclusive disjunction: cannot both be true, but both can be false), which is false only when both are false. The author then discusses the truth values of the parts: in the 'real' disjunctive (first type), exactly one part is true; in man’at al-jam‘, all parts can be false but not all true; in man’at al-khulu, all parts can be true but not all false. Examples: 'This is either not a human or an animal' (man’at al-khulu, always true); 'This is either a tree or a stone' (man’at al-jam‘, false if the object is neither tree nor stone). This classification is crucial for understanding the logical behavior of disjunctions in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway

Quantification of Conditional Propositions
Author introduces rules for quantifying conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is universal or particular based on the universality of its entailment or opposition.

Core Idea
A conditional's universality depends on the entailment, not on its parts.

In categorical propositions, we consider the judgment to determine negation or affirmation. Similarly, for conditional propositions, the key to quantifying them is the universality of the relationship between antecedent and consequent. The author explains that even if both parts are particular, the conditional can be universal if the entailment holds universally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
Determine whether the proposition is muttasila (conditional) or munfasila (disjunctive). This affects which relationship (luzum or ta‘anud) to evaluate.
2
Find the relationship
For muttasila, look for the entailment (luzum) between antecedent and consequent. For munfasila, look for the opposition (ta‘anud).
3
Assess universality of the relationship
Ask: Does this entailment/opposition hold in every case? If yes, the relationship is universal; if only in some cases, it is particular.
4
Determine the conditional's quantity
The conditional's quantity is the same as the quantity of the relationship. The quantifiers of the parts (universal/particular) are irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان حصر الشرطيات وإهمالها. وكما أن الاعتبار في السلب والإيجاب بالحكم فيها لا بالمحكوم عليه وبه؛ فكذا الاعتبار في كليتها بكليي اللزوم والتعاند لا بكلية الطرفين. فإنك إذا قلت كلَّما كان بعض الحيوان إنسانًا فبعض الحيوان ناطق، فالقضية كلية ضرورة كون اللزوم كليًّا. إذا عرفت هذا سهل عليك معرفة الحصر والإهمال وغيرهما كما مر من قبل في الحمليّات. ثم إنها أي الشرطيات على ثمانية أقسام
Chapter: On explaining the quantification and non-quantification of conditionals. Just as the consideration in negation and affirmation is by the judgment in them, not by the subject and predicate; likewise the consideration in their universality is by the universality of the entailment and opposition, not by the universality of the two sides. For if you say: 'Whenever some animal is human, then some animal is rational,' the proposition is universal necessarily because the entailment is universal. If you understand this, knowledge of quantification and non-quantification and other matters becomes easy, as previously in categoricals. Then the conditionals are of eight kinds.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on the quantification (hasr) and non-quantification (ihmal) of conditional propositions. The author draws an analogy with categorical propositions: just as in categoricals the negation or affirmation is considered by the judgment (hukm) and not the subject/predicate, in conditionals universality is determined by the universality of the entailment (luzum) or opposition (ta‘anud), not by the universality of the antecedent or consequent. He provides an example: 'Whenever some animal is human, then some animal is rational' – even though the antecedent and consequent are particular (some animal), the conditional as a whole is universal because the entailment holds universally. This principle makes it easy to determine quantification. Finally, the author announces that conditionals are divided into eight kinds, though the enumeration is not given here (likely continues on the next page). This foundational principle is essential for correctly categorizing conditional propositions in logical analysis.

Quantification of Conditional Propositions: Particular, Absolute, and Universal
Explaining quantification of conditionals by implication universality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition is particular, absolute, or quantified, and explain why its universality is based on the generality of states rather than subject term.

Core Idea
Conditionals are quantified by the universality of implication, not by terms.

In this section, we learn how to determine the quantity of conditional propositions. Just as in categoricals the judgment is what matters, in conditionals the universality comes from the implication being universal in all states, not from the antecedent or consequent being universal. We will distinguish three types: particular (time-restricted), absolute (unquantified), and quantified (with a quantifier). This understanding is essential for correctly analyzing conditional arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the analogy with categoricals
The passage begins by stating that just as in categoricals negation and affirmation apply to the judgment (copula), not the subject or predicate, so in conditionals universality is determined by the universality of the implication (luzum) or opposition (ta'and), not by the universality of the antecedent or consequent terms. Note the parallel.
2
Identify the three types of conditionals
The author divides conditionals into three categories based on quantification: particular (makhsusa) when the judgment is restricted to a specific time; absolute (muhmala) when there is no quantifier and no time restriction; and quantified (mahsura) when a quantifier like 'whenever' is present making it universal or particular.
Quantified conditionals come in two forms: universal and particular, just like categoricals.
3
Understand the basis of universality
The crucial point: universality in conditionals is based on 'umum al-ahwal' (generality of states), not on the subject being universal. For example, 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves his hand' is universal even though Zayd is a singular term, because in every state where Zayd writes, he moves his hand.
Similarly, even if the antecedent is a fixed eternal matter like 'God is knowing', the conditional 'Whenever God is knowing, God is living' is universal because it holds in every state.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Practice identifying the type of each conditional: (i) 'If AB at time t, then CD' is particular. (ii) 'If AB, then CD' with no quantifier is absolute. (iii) 'Whenever AB, then CD' is quantified universal. Check that you can justify each classification.
Remember: particular has a time restriction, absolute has none, quantified has a quantifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان حصر الشرطيات وإهمالها. وكما أن الاعتبار في السلب والإيجاب بالحكم فيها لا بالمحكوم عليه وبه؛ فكذا الاعتبار في كليتها بكليي اللزوم والتعاند لا بكلية الطرفين. فإنك إذا قلت كلَّما كان بعض الحيوان إنسانًا فبعض الحيوان ناطق، فالقضية كلية ضرورة كون اللزوم كليًّا. إذا عرفت هذا سهل عليك معرفة الحصر والإهمال وغيرهما كما مر من قبل في الحمليّات. ثم إنها أي الشرطيات على ثمانية أقسام بحسب الحصر والإهمال نحو الحمليّات؛ وذلك لأن الحكم فيها لا يخلو من أن يكون مختصا بوقت معين، وحينئذ تكون القضية مخصوصة وقد تسمى شخصية أيضًا. وإما أن لا يكون وإنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مقترنا بما يدل على كلية اللزوم أو العناد أو على جزئيتهما، أو لا يكون فإن لم يكن مقترنا بذلك فهي مهملة، وإن كان فهي محصورة كلية أو جزئية. (أ) أما المخصوصة من المتصلات فمثالها أن يقال إن كان «أب» وقت كذا كان ج د أو ليس إن كان «أب» وقت كذا كان (ج) د موجبةً وسالبة. ثم الاختصاص بالوقت المعين من اللوازم وإنه لازم فيما نصبتُ لك من المثال وإلا لا تكون مخصوصة؛ والاختصاص هو الإفراد وقطع الشركة. (ب) وأما المهملة فهي كقولنا إن كان «أب» فـ ج د أو ليس إن «أب» فـ ج د موجبةً وسالبة، فلا اختصاص للحكم فيهما بالوقت المعين ولا وجود للسور فيها وهو الذي يدل على كلية اللزوم أو العناد أو على جزئيتهما كما مر، فلا يُعلم أنه في جميع الأوقات أو في البعض منها دون البعض. (ت) وأما المحصورة فإنها قد تكون كلية كقولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» أو ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ ج د موجبة وسالبة. ثم الكلية فيهما بحسب عموم الأحوال لا بكلية الموضوع في المقدم، فإنك إذا قلت كلما كان زيد يكتب فزيد يحرك يده، فالقضية كلية مع شخصية الموضوع في المقدم والتالي، ولا بعمومية المراد في المقدم حتى يكون معناه كل مرة يكون فيها «أب» فـ ج د. فإن من الجائز أن يكون المقدم أمرًا ثابتا لا تغير فيه ولا تكرر أصلا كما يقال كلما كان الله تعالى عالما كان حيًّا بل اللازم فيها عمومية الأحوال كما مر. فإن الشيء الثابت لا يمتنع أن تقترن به شروط كثيرة في أحوال كثيرة فكان معناه أنه لا يفرض حال
Section: On explaining the quantification of conditionals and their neglect. Just as consideration in negation and affirmation is of the judgment in them, not of the subject and predicate, so likewise consideration in their universality is by the universality of the implication and the opposition, not by the universality of the two sides. For if you say: 'Whenever some animal is human, some animal is rational,' the proposition is universal due to the necessity of the implication being universal. If you understand this, it becomes easy for you to know quantification and neglect and the like, as previously mentioned in categoricals. Then conditionals are of eight divisions according to quantification and neglect, like categoricals. That is because the judgment in them is either specific to a particular time, in which case the proposition is particular (makhsusa) and also called personal; or it is not specific, and then either it is accompanied by an indicator of the universality of the implication or opposition or their particularity, or it is not accompanied—if not accompanied, it is absolute (muhmala); if accompanied, it is quantified (mahsura), universal or particular. (A) As for the particular among connected conditionals, its example is to say: 'If AB at time such-and-such, then CD,' or 'If AB at time such-and-such, then not CD,' positive and negative. Then the specificity to a particular time is among the necessary conditions, and it is necessary in the example I set for you; otherwise it would not be particular. And specificity is singling out and cutting off sharing. (B) As for the absolute, it is like our saying: 'If AB, then CD,' or 'If AB, then not CD,' positive and negative, with no specificity of the judgment to a particular time and no quantifier (sawr) present—which indicates the universality of implication or opposition or their particularity, as mentioned—so it is not known whether it is in all times or some of them. (C) As for the quantified, it may be universal, like our saying: 'Whenever AB, then CD,' or 'Absolutely not: if AB, then CD,' positive and negative. Then universality in them is according to the generality of states, not by the universality of the subject in the antecedent. For if you say: 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves his hand,' the proposition is universal despite the singularity of the subject in the antecedent and consequent, and not by the generality of the intended meaning in the antecedent such that its meaning is 'every time AB, then CD.' For it is possible that the antecedent is a fixed matter with no change or repetition at all, as is said: 'Whenever God is knowing, He is living.' Rather, the necessary (condition) in them is the generality of states, as mentioned. For a fixed thing is not prevented from being accompanied by many conditions in many states, so its meaning is that it is not assumed a state...
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of how conditional propositions are quantified (hasr) or left unquantified (muhmala). The author begins by drawing an analogy with categorical propositions: just as in categoricals the negation and affirmation apply to the judgment (the copula) rather than the subject and predicate, so in conditionals universality is determined by the universality of the implication (luzum) or opposition (ta'and), not by the universality of the antecedent or consequent terms. He gives an example: 'Whenever some animal is human, some animal is rational' is universal because the implication holds in all cases. He then states that conditionals have eight divisions with respect to quantification, similar to categoricals. The judgment in a conditional is either specific to a particular time (makhsusa, also called shakhsiyya), or not specific. If not specific, it is either accompanied by a quantifier (sawr) indicating universal or particular implication, or it is not, in which case it is absolute (muhmala). He provides examples: (A) A particular conditional mentions a specific time, e.g., 'If AB at time T, then CD.' (B) An absolute conditional has no time restriction and no quantifier, e.g., 'If AB, then CD,' leaving it unknown whether it holds in all times or some. (C) A quantified conditional has a quantifier like 'whenever' (kullama) indicating universality. The author then clarifies a crucial point: universality in conditionals is based on the generality of states (states of affairs), not on the universality of the subject of the antecedent. He demonstrates with the example 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves his hand'—the subject Zayd is singular, yet the proposition is universal because in every state where Zayd writes, he moves his hand. Similarly, even if the antecedent is a fixed, non-repeating matter like 'God is knowing' (which is eternal and unchanging), the conditional 'Whenever God is knowing, God is living' is universal because in every possible state (or every moment of existence), the implication holds. Thus, universality pertains to the generality of circumstances, not to temporal repetition or subject generality. The passage ends mid-explanation, likely continuing on the next page.

Classification of Conditional Propositions: Makhsusa, Muhmala, Mahsura
Author classifies conditional propositions into three types by quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given conditional proposition is makhsusa, muhmala, or mahsura (and if mahsura, whether kulliya or juz'iyya) by examining its quantifier and scope.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are classified by quantifier just like categoricals, but their universality hinges on generality of states, not subject terms.

In this lesson, we dive into the classification of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya al-muttasila) as laid out by the author. Just as categorical propositions are divided into particularized, indeterminate, and quantified, conditionals follow a parallel division. However, a key nuance is that universality in conditionals is about the generality of states (umum al-ahwal) rather than the universality of the subject term. This distinction is critical for correctly analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier (sawr)
Look for words like 'كلما' (whenever) for universal, 'قد' or 'ربما' for particular, or absence of any quantifier. Also check if a specific time is mentioned (e.g., 'at time T').
In Arabic texts, the quantifier may be implicit; context helps.
2
Determine if the judgment is restricted to a specific time
If the conditional explicitly mentions a particular time (e.g., 'at dawn') or a unique instance, it is makhsusa (particularized). This is the first distinction.
Makhsusa is also called 'shakhsiyya' (personal).
3
Check for the presence of a quantifier (sawr)
If no quantifier and no time restriction, it is muhmala (indeterminate). If a quantifier is present, it is mahsura (quantified).
Muhmala leaves the scope unknown; it could be universal or particular in different contexts.
4
Distinguish between kulliya and juz'iyya in mahsura
If the quantifier indicates that the implication holds in all states (like 'كلما'), it is kulliya. If it indicates only some states (like 'قد'), it is juz'iyya. Remember: universality is about states, not the subject term.
Example: 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves his hand' is kulliya even though 'Zayd' is a particular subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحسب الحصر والإهمال نحو الحمليّات؛ وذلك لأن الحكم فيها لا يخلو من أن يكون مختصا بوقت معين، وحينئذ تكون القضية مخصوصة وقد تسمى شخصية أيضًا. وإما أن لا يكون وإنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مقترنا بما يدل على كلية اللزوم أو العناد أو على جزئيتهما، أو لا يكون فإن لم يكن مقترن بذلك فهي مهملة، وإن كان فهي محصورة / كلية أو جزئية.
[أ] أما المخصوصة من المتصلات فمثالها أن يقال إن كان «أب» وقت كذا كان ج د أو ليس إن كان «أب» وقت كذا كان (ج) د موجبةً وسالبة . ثمَّ الاختصاص بالوقت المعين من اللوازم وإنه لازم فيما نصبتُ لك من المثال وإلا لا تكون مخصوصة ؛ والاختصاص هو الإفراد وقطع الشركة.
[ب] وأما المهملة فهي كقولنا إن كان «أب» فـ «ج د» أو ليس إن كان «أب فـ اج د» موجبةً وسالبةٌ، فلا اختصاص للحكم فيهما بالوقت المعين ولا وجود للسور فيها وهو الذي يدل على كلية اللزوم أو العناد أو على جزئيتهما كما مرَّ ، فلا يُعلم أنه في جميع الأوقات أو في البعض منها دون البعض.
[ت] وأما المحصورة فإنها قد تكون كلية كقولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» أو ليس البتة إذا كان «أ ب» فـ «ج د» موجبة وسالبة. ثم الكلية فيهما بحسب عموم الأحوال لا بكلية الموضوع في المقدم، فإنك إذا قلت كلما كان زيد يكتب فزيد يحرك يده، فالقضية كلية مع شخصية الموضوع في المقدم والتالي، ولا بعمومية المراد في المقدم حتى يكون معناه كل مرة يكون فيها «أب» فـ «ج د». فإن من الجائز أن يكون المقدم أمرًا ثابتا لا تغير فيه ولا تكرر أصلا كما يقال كلما كان الله تعالى عالما كان حيًّا بل اللازم فيها عمومية الأحوال كما مر . فإن الشيء الثابت لا يمتنع أن تقترن به شروط كثيرة في أحوال كثيرة فكان معناه أنه لا يفرض حال من الأحوال ولا زمان من الأزمنة يتقرر فيه إن «أب» إلا ويتقرر معه إن «ج د».
According to quantification and non-quantification, like categorical [propositions]; because the judgment in them is either specific to a particular time, and then the proposition is particularized (makhsusa) and also called personal. Or it is not specific, and it is either accompanied by what indicates the universality of the implication or opposition or their particularity, or not; if it is not accompanied by that, it is indeterminate (muhmala), and if it is, it is quantified (mahsura): universal or particular. [A] As for the particularized among conditionals, its example is to say: 'If A is B at time T, then C is D' or 'not: if A is B at time T, then C is D' — affirmative and negative. Then the specification to the particular time is necessary, and it is necessary in what I set for you as an example, otherwise it would not be particularized; and specification is individuation and removal of commonality. [B] As for the indeterminate, it is like our saying: 'If A is B, then C is D' or 'not: if A is B, then C is D' — affirmative and negative. There is no specification of the judgment to a particular time, and there is no quantifier in them — which indicates the universality of implication or opposition or their particularity, as before — so it is not known whether [the judgment holds] in all times or in some of them without others. [C] As for the quantified, it may be universal like our saying: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' or 'absolutely not: if A is B, then C is D' — affirmative and negative. Then universality in them is according to the generality of states, not by the universality of the subject in the antecedent. For if you say: 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves his hand', the proposition is universal despite the particularity of the subject in the antecedent and consequent, and not by the generality of the intended [person] in the antecedent so that its meaning would be 'every time A is B, then C is D'. For it is possible that the antecedent is a fixed matter with no change and no repetition at all, as is said: 'Whenever God is knowing, God is living.' Rather, the necessary [element] in them is the generality of states, as before. For the fixed thing does not prevent that many conditions be attached to it in many states, so its meaning is that no state of states and no time of times is assumed in which 'A is B' holds except that 'C is D' holds with it.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies conditional propositions (mutasilat) based on the presence and type of quantifier (sawr), analogous to the classification of categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that the judgment in a conditional proposition is either restricted to a specific time (makhsusa, also called personale), or it is not restricted. If not restricted, it may either have a quantifier indicating universality or particularity of the implication or opposition (sawr of kulliya or juz'iyya), or be without any quantifier (muhmala). Thus three types: makhsusa, muhmala, and mahsura (which further divides into kulliya and juz'iyya). Under [A], the author gives examples of makhsusa: 'If A is B at time T, then C is D' (affirmative) and its negative. The specification to time is essential; without it the proposition would not be makhsusa. Specification here is individuation (ta'yin) and removal of commonality (qat' al-shirka). Under [B], muhmala is illustrated: 'If A is B, then C is D' without time restriction or quantifier. We do not know if it holds in all times or only some. Under [C], mahsura is quantified. It can be universal (kulliya) like 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' or its absolute negation. The author clarifies that universality in conditionals is based on the generality of states (abul states) not on the universality of the subject term in the antecedent. An example: 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves his hand' is universal even though 'Zayd' is a particular subject. The meaning is that for every state in which the antecedent holds, the consequent holds. The antecedent may be a fixed, unchanging matter (like 'God is knowing') — even then the universal form implies that in every state where the antecedent is true, the consequent is true. Thus the universal quantifier (كلما) indicates 'no state is assumed where A is B without C is D'.

Key Terms
مخصوصة
مهملة
محصورة
كلية
سور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of conditional proposition is 'If Zayd writes, then Zayd moves his hand'?
A) Makhsusa (particularized)
B) Muhmala (indeterminate)
C) Mahsura kulliya (quantified universal)
D) Mahsura juz'iyya (quantified particular)

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are classified by quantifier into makhsusa (time-restricted), muhmala (no quantifier), and mahsura (quantified), with universality defined by generality of states rather than subject universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following conditional propositions as makhsusa, muhmala, or mahsura (and if mahsura, specify kulliya or juz'iyya): (1) 'If the sun rises, the day breaks.' (2) 'If Zayd is asleep at night, he is not fasting.' (3) 'Whenever the imam recites aloud, the congregation listens.' (4) 'If he divorces her three times, she becomes forbidden to him until she marries another.' For each, explain your reasoning based on quantifier and time restriction.
?
Find a conditional proposition from a fiqh text (e.g., from al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa or a manual of Shafi'i fiqh) and classify it using the three types. Write a brief analysis.

Two Interpretations of Universal Accidental Conditionals
Author examines truth conditions of universality in accidental conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the essentialist and existential interpretations of a universal accidental conditional and determine which yields truth.

Core Idea
Universality in accidental conditionals is only true when read as a statement about essences, not actual existence.

In conditional propositions where the connection between antecedent and consequent is accidental, the universal quantifier 'whenever' can be interpreted in two ways. The author explains that one interpretation considers the essential nature of the terms, while the other considers their actual co-occurrence in the external world. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly evaluating the truth of such conditionals in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two types of conditionals
Identify that the passage discusses accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya) conditionals. For this lesson, focus on accidental ones.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author states that for accidental conditionals, universality can be meant by 'consideration of the essence' (i'tibar al-haqiqa) or 'consideration of external existence' (i'tibar al-wujud al-khariji).
3
Evaluate the essentialist reading
Under the essentialist reading, the universal means: in any assumed state, if man exists and is speaking, then donkey exists and is braying. This is true because it is about definitions.
It does not require actual co-existence.
4
Evaluate the existential reading
Under the existential reading, the universal means: in every actual state where a man exists and speaks, a donkey also exists and brays. This is false because the two can exist separately.
5
Conclude the correct interpretation
The author shows that only the essentialist reading makes the universal accidental conditional true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أن ننظر في هذه الكلية أنها كيف تصدق في الاتفاقية واللزومية. [أ] أما الاتفاقية فهذه الكلية إما أن يكون المراد منها اعتبار الحقيقة أو اعتبار الوجود الخارجي: فإن كان الأول كان معناه أنه لا حال يفرض فيه أن يكون الإنسان متى كان موجوداً كان ناطقاً إلا ويفرض فيه أن يكون الحمار متى كان موجوداً كان ناهقا، وذلك حق. وإن كان الثاني كان معناه أنه لا حال يكون الإنسان فيه موجودا في الخارج وموصوفًا بكونه ناطقا إلا ويكون الحمار فيه موجودًا في الخارج وموصوفًا بكونه ناهقًا، وهذا من جملة ما لا يحكم عليه أنه حق. فإن من الجائز أن يكون بعض الأزمنة بحيث يوجد فيه أحدهما دون الآخر. [ب] وأما اللزومية فالكلية فيها عندهم مشروطة بشرط أن يكون المقدم فيها على الوجه الذي لا يمتنع وقوعه عليه كما في قولك كلَّما كان هذا إنسانًا على النحو الذي لا يمتنع وقوع الإنسان عليه كان حيوانًا فإنه إذا لم يعتبر هذا الشرط لم تصدق الكلية. فإن من جملة الأحوال التي يمكن فرضها للمقدم أن لا يلزمه التالي ولو فرض المقدم بحال أن لا يلزمه التالي فلا يلزمه التالي. والحق فيه أنها لا تكون مشروطة بهذا الشرط؛ إذ الكلام فيما يكون مستلزما للتالي نحو الحيوان للإنسان مثلا. ولو كان مستلزما للتالي فعلى أي حال يفرض يلزمه التالي وإلا لا يكون مستلزما له، فالإنسان مثلا إذا فرض أنه موجود بدون الحيوان كان من اللوازم لم يكن الحيوان موجودا وإلا لا يكون هذا الفرض فرض وجود الإنسان. ولما كان الفرض فرض وجود الإنسان والإنسان هو الحيوان كان الحيوان موجودًا بالضرورة، غاية الأمر أن يقال لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان الحيوان موجودًا مع اللا حيوانية على ذلك التقدير. لكنا نقول: إذا كان ذلك التقدير ممتنعًا فلا يمتنع هذا والممتنع قد يكون مستلزمًا
And we must examine this universality, how it is true in accidental and necessary [conditionals]. [A] As for the accidental, this universality either is meant by consideration of the essence or consideration of external existence: if the former, its meaning is that there is no state assumed in which man, whenever he exists, is speaking, except that there is assumed in it that the donkey, whenever it exists, is braying — and that is true. And if the latter, its meaning is that there is no state in which man exists externally and is described as speaking, except that the donkey exists externally and is described as braying — and this is among that which is not judged to be true. For it is possible that some times are such that one of them exists without the other. [B] As for the necessary, universality in it according to them is conditioned by the condition that the antecedent be in a manner that its occurrence is not impossible, as in your saying: 'Whenever this is a human in the manner that the occurrence of humanity is not impossible, it is an animal.' For if this condition is not considered, the universality is not true. For among the states that can be assumed for the antecedent is that the consequent does not follow from it; and if the antecedent is assumed in a state where the consequent does not follow, then the consequent does not follow. But the correct [view] is that it is not conditioned by this condition; because the discussion is about what is necessarily entailing the consequent, like animal to human, for example. And if it were necessarily entailing the consequent, then in any state it is assumed, the consequent follows; otherwise it would not be entailing it. For example, if man is assumed to exist without animal, then from the necessary entailments, animal would not exist; otherwise this assumption would not be an assumption of the existence of man. And since the assumption is the assumption of the existence of man, and man is [essentially] animal, then animal necessarily exists. The utmost that can be said is: if it were as mentioned, then animal would exist with non-animality on that estimation. But we say: if that estimation is impossible, then this [implication] is not impossible, and the impossible may be entailing [something].
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage discusses how universal quantification (kulliya) applies in conditional propositions, specifically distinguishing between accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya) conditionals. The author poses the question: how does the universal quantifier (e.g., 'كلما') yield truth in these two types? Under [A] for accidental conditionals (where the connection between antecedent and consequent is not intrinsic but coincidental), the author presents two interpretations of the universal statement. The first interpretation considers the essential nature (اعتبار الحقيقة): e.g., 'Whenever man exists, he is speaking' paired with 'Whenever donkey exists, it is braying'. If taken as a statement about essences, it means in any assumed state, if man exists speaking, then donkey exists braying; this is true because it is a hypothetical about the definitions. The second interpretation considers actual external existence (اعتبار الوجود الخارجي): it would require that in every actual state where a man exists and speaks, a donkey also exists and brays — which is false, since there are times when only one exists. So universality in accidental conditionals is true only under the essentialist reading. Under [B] for necessary conditionals (where the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent, like 'human implies animal'), some logicians (عندهم) impose a condition: the antecedent must be assumed in a manner that its occurrence is not impossible. For example, 'Whenever this is a human in a way that it is not impossible for it to be human, then it is an animal.' The idea is that if we consider the antecedent under a description that prevents the implication (e.g., assuming human exists without animal), the universal would fail unless we restrict the antecedent to its normal possibility. The author then gives the correct view (الحق): such a condition is not needed. If the antecedent truly entails the consequent (مستلزم), then in every possible assumption of the antecedent, the consequent follows. Using the example 'human entails animal': if we assume a human existing without an animal, this is impossible because human is essentially animal. The assumption of 'human' already includes animal. The author argues that any impossible estimation (تقدير ممتنع) may still entail something, but here the entailment is necessary. The text ends with a note that the impossible may be entailing, perhaps continuing a rebuttal.

Key Terms
اتفاقية (ittifaqiyya)
كلية (kulliya)
اعتبار الحقيقة (i'tibar al-haqiqa)
اعتبار الوجود الخارجي (i'tibar al-wujud al-khariji)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes the universal accidental conditional 'Whenever man exists, he is speaking' true under the essentialist interpretation?
A) It requires that in every actual moment, a speaking man exists alongside a braying donkey.
B) It means that in any assumed state where man is speaking, the donkey is braying, regardless of actual existence.
C) It requires that man and donkey always exist together in reality.
D) It is false under any interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Universal accidental conditionals are true only when interpreted as statements about essential natures, not about actual external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the accidental conditional 'Whenever the adhan is called, the congregation stands for prayer.' Identify the two possible interpretations (essential nature vs external existence) and state which one would typically be true in a masjid setting.

Necessary Conditionals: Do They Require a Possibility Condition?
Author argues that necessary conditionals do not need a condition of possibility for the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why necessary conditionals do not require restricting the antecedent to possible states, and identify the author's argument that genuine entailment holds in all assumptions.

Core Idea
If the antecedent truly entails the consequent, the consequent follows under any assumption, even impossible ones.

Some logicians argued that in a necessary conditional, the universal quantifier only holds if we restrict the antecedent to states where its occurrence is not impossible. The author rejects this view, arguing that genuine entailment (istilzam) is unconditional. Let's examine his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claimed condition
Some logicians say that for a necessary conditional to be universally true, the antecedent must be taken in a manner that its occurrence is not impossible. Example: 'Whenever this is a human (in a way that it is not impossible to be human), it is an animal.'
2
Identify the problem with the condition
If the condition is not considered, they argue the universal would be false because we could assume the antecedent in a state where the consequent does not follow.
E.g., assuming human without animal.
3
The author's move
The author says the correct view is that no such condition is needed. If the antecedent truly entails the consequent (مستلزم), then in any state you assume the antecedent, the consequent follows.
4
Test with example
Take 'human entails animal'. If we assume a human exists without animal, that assumption is impossible because human is essentially animal. But even in that impossible assumption, the entailment holds because 'human' includes 'animal'.
5
Conclude
Therefore, necessary conditionals are unconditional; the extra condition is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

D) Because the author agrees with the condition.

Key Takeaway
Necessary conditionals are unconditional: genuine entailment holds in all assumptions, even impossible ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the necessary conditional 'Whenever the Qur'an is the word of Allah, it is miraculous.' According to the author, is it valid to restrict the antecedent to 'the Qur'an as it is known to be revealed'? Explain why or why not.

Truth Conditions of Universal Conditionals: Accidental vs Implicative
Analysis of truth conditions for universal quantified conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accidental and implicative universal conditionals and evaluate the truth conditions of each, including the debate about the antecedent possibility condition.

Core Idea
A universal conditional is only true if the connection holds in every possible state—but whether the antecedent must be possible is contested.

In logic, conditionals can be quantified universally ('whenever X, then Y'), but how do we determine if such a claim is true? This passage from a logic commentary unpacks the difference between accidental conditionals (mere coexistence) and implicative conditionals (necessary entailment). The author critiques a common requirement that the antecedent be possible, and extends the discussion to particular conditionals. Mastering this distinction is key to evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of universal conditionals
The passage distinguishes between 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental) and 'luzumiyya' (implicative). In accidental, the connection between antecedent and consequent is not necessary; in implicative, it is a necessary entailment. Understand this basic contrast.
2
Analyse the accidental universal
The author offers two interpretations for the accidental universal: essential (haqiqa) and external existence (wujud khariji). Under the essential interpretation, the universal is true because it refers to the essence of things (e.g., every man is rational by definition). Under the external existence interpretation, it claims actual coexistence in the world, which can be false because times may differ.
Contrast these interpretations; the accidental universal is trivially true only under the essential reading.
3
Examine the implicative universal and the condition of possibility
The standard view ('they') imposes a condition: the antecedent must be taken in a manner whose occurrence is not impossible (i.e., the antecedent must be possible). The author argues against this: if the implication is essential, the antecedent's impossibility does not break the implication. Using the example of man and animal, he shows that assuming man without animal is impossible, but that very impossibility is part of the entailment—an impossible antecedent can imply an impossible consequent.
Follow the dialectic: the author rejects the possibility condition, maintaining that essential implications hold without it.
4
Understand particular conditionals and the objection from uniformity of nature
The passage discusses particular quantified conditionals like 'Sometimes if every A is B, then C is D'. An objection says that if the nature of the antecedent necessitates the consequent, then implication should be universal. The author replies that the antecedent's nature may not be essentially implicative; implication could be through accidental attributes. This allows particular conditionals to be true even if the universal is not, because accidents can vary across individuals and times.
The author also addresses a composition of two universals, showing that temporal accidents can make a particular conditional true.

And we must consider how this universality is true in the accidental and the implicative. [A] As for the accidental, this universality either intends consideration of the essence or consideration of external existence: if the first, its meaning is that there is no state assumed in which man, whenever he exists, is rational, except that it is assumed that the donkey, whenever it exists, is braying; and that is true. If the second, its meaning is that there is no state in which man exists externally and is described as being rational, except that the donkey exists externally and is described as being braying; and this is among that which is not judged to be true. For it is possible that some times are such that one of them exists without the other. [B] As for the implicative, universality in it according to them is conditioned by the condition that the antecedent be in the manner whose occurrence is not impossible, as in your saying: "Whenever this is a man in the manner whose occurrence is not impossible, it is an animal." For if this condition is not considered, the universal is not true. For among the states that can be assumed for the antecedent are those in which the consequent does not follow; and if the antecedent is assumed in a state in which the consequent does not follow, then the consequent does not follow. And the truth / in it is that it is not conditioned by this condition; for the discussion is about what is a necessary implication of the consequent, like animal for man for example. And if it is a necessary implication of the consequent, then in whatever state it is assumed, the consequent follows; otherwise it is not a necessary implication of it. So man, for example, if assumed to exist without animal, it would be among the necessary implications that animal does not exist; otherwise this assumption would not be an assumption of the existence of man. And since the assumption is an assumption of the existence of man, and man is animal, animal necessarily exists; the end of the matter is that it might be said: if it were as mentioned, then animal would exist with non-animality on that estimation. But we say: If that estimation is impossible, then this is not impossible, and the impossible may imply the impossible. Then the estimation is not impossible in this example that passed, except if man in it is a man in his reality, not what is a man only in name. Then universality may be in the accidental and may be in the implicative, as passed concerning affirmation and negation; and negation in the accidental is to deny agreement, and in the implicative to deny implication. And just as the absolute conditional is more general than the implicative, so the negative implicative is more general than the absolute negative, so that our saying "It is not at all that if man is rational, it follows that the donkey is braying" is valid along with the truth of our saying "Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying." And it, i.e., the quantified, may be particular as well, like our saying "Sometimes if every A is B, then C is D" or "Not whenever every A is B, then C is D" affirmative and negative. And it might be said concerning our saying "Sometimes if A is B, then C is D": The judgment of a single nature is one; if the nature of the antecedent necessitates the consequent, then it would be necessary that the implication hold in all cases; otherwise there is no implication in it. But we say: If the nature of the antecedent is a necessary implication of the consequent in its essence, then what you mentioned is valid, but it is not necessary that it be a necessary implication; for that which implies something may imply it in its essence or may not, but rather through the nature of the necessary implication, like the specificity of the species from the genus, for the implication of the differentia for it is in its essence, rather the essence of the differentia. And if it were so, then it would be possible that some individuals of a single nature imply something while others do not. And if it is said: "Granted that it is so, but when it is composed of two universals, as in our saying 'Sometimes if every A is B, then every C is D,' it is not true unless the universal is true along with it," we say: It is possible that B is an accident for every A at a time when D is an accident for every C at that time. And among accidents, some are separable at one time and not separable at another; and among times, some are such that it agrees with every individual.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the truth conditions for universal quantified conditional propositions (al-muttasila al-kulliyya). The author begins by distinguishing between two types of conditional: accidental (ittifaqiyya) and implicative (luzumiyya). For the accidental universal, he considers two interpretations: either it refers to the essence (al-haqiqa) or to external existence (al-wujud al-khariji). If essence, the universal claim is that every time one thing exists, another necessarily coexists in the realm of conceptual possibility—like every man being rational and every donkey braying. This is true because it is an essential definition. If external existence, the claim would be that in reality, whenever a man exists as rational, a donkey also exists as braying—which is false because times may differ. Thus, the accidental universal is only trivially true when understood essentially. For the implicative universal, the standard view imposes a condition that the antecedent must be taken in a manner that is not impossible (i.e., the antecedent must be possible). Without this condition, the universal may fail because one can imagine a state where the antecedent is true but the consequent false. However, the author argues against this condition: if the antecedent genuinely implies the consequent, then in every possible state the consequent must follow; otherwise it would not be a true implication. He uses the example of man and animal: if man is assumed without animal, that is impossible because man is essentially animal—hence the implication is necessary. The estimation of man without animal is impossible, and an impossible antecedent can imply impossible consequences. He then notes that universality can apply to both accidental and implicative conditionals, and similarly for negation. The negative accidental denies coexistence, while the negative implicative denies implication. The absolute conditional (without specification) is more general than the implicative, so a negative implicative is more general than an absolute negative, allowing statements like 'It is not at all that if man is rational, donkey is braying' to be true even though the affirmative universal may also be true (because the affirmative accidental universal is true essentially, and the negative implicative denies necessary implication, which is absent). Next, he discusses particular quantified conditionals, e.g., 'Sometimes if every A is B, then C is D' or its negation. A potential objection arises: if the antecedent's nature necessitates the consequent, then the implication should be universal, not particular. The author replies that the antecedent's nature may not be essentially implicative of the consequent; implication may be through an accidental attribute (like a differentia that is not essential to the species). This allows that some individuals of the same nature may imply the consequent while others do not, depending on additional attributes. Finally, he addresses a composition of two universals: 'Sometimes if every A is B, then every C is D'. The objector says this cannot be true unless the universal statement is true. The author counters that B might be an accident for every A at one time, and D an accident for every C at that same time; since accidents can be temporal, the universal can hold at that time but not at others, making the particular true. This entire discussion refines the logical understanding of quantified conditionals, showing how they are evaluated in different contexts and defending the possibility of particular conditionals against objections based on uniformity of nature.
