Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of the entailment relations (اللزوم) between universal conditional propositions and their particular counterparts, as well as between different universal conditionals. The author begins by reiterating that if a universal conditional (affirmative or negative) is true, the corresponding particular conditional must also be true. This is analogous to the subalternation in categorical propositions. For example, the universal affirmative 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' implies the particular 'It may be that when A is B, then C is D,' and also the particular 'It may be that when C is D, then A is B' (converse by limitation). The author notes that the universal negative also entails its particular, and thus particular entailments are more numerous than universal ones because the universal entails the particular but not vice versa.

The author then distinguishes between universal conditionals that are mutually entailing (مستلزمة) and those that are not. A universal negative can entail all the entailments of the universal affirmative if they are mutually entailing, as in the pair 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Not at all when A is B is it not that C is D' — these two are mutually entailing. The author explains that the negative entails both the affirmative and negative entailments of the affirmative, and that the affirmatives and negatives are intertwined in entailment relations. The truth of all these entailments is such that the negation of truth in them contradicts the truth in the original (اللازم).

Then the author discusses the universal negative as an entailing proposition, explaining that it entails all the affirmatives and negatives that were necessary for the original affirmative, but not because they are entailments of those affirmatives, but because they are entailments of the negative itself. The same holds for the particular entailments of the original affirmative — they are also entailments of this negative. The author lists specific examples.

Next, the author introduces the non-entailing (اللامستلزمة) universal conditional, which can be true even if the universal affirmative is false, and vice versa. For instance, 'Not at all when A is B is it that D' is not entailing of the affirmatives or negatives mentioned. The reasoning is that a proposition may not entail a specific thing or its contradictory, even if it entails one of the two contradictories absolutely. The author warns that from the negation of entailment in one side, one cannot infer entailment in the other.

Finally, the author anticipates an objection. The objector argues: If a thing necessarily entails one of two contradictories, then if it does not entail one, it entails the other, otherwise it would entail neither. But the author's response is cut off in this passage (but implies a refutation). This objection touches on the logical principle of excluded middle in entailment, but the author likely counters that the assumption of necessary entailment of one of the two is not valid for non-entailing propositions. This whole discussion is crucial for understanding how conditionals relate in logical systems, especially in the context of argumentation and proof.

Deriving True Exclusive Disjunctions from Conditionals
Author shows how universal conditionals yield true exclusive disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a true exclusive disjunction (al-munfaṣila al-ḥaqīqiyya) from a universal conditional proposition and distinguish between the two types of exclusive disjunctions.

Core Idea
A universal conditional yields a true exclusive disjunction by negating the consequent.

This passage introduces a key logical equivalence: a universal conditional 'Whenever A is B, then J is D' is logically equivalent to the exclusive disjunction 'Either A is B or J is not D'. Understanding this transformation is crucial for analyzing propositional relationships and constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic. The author also distinguishes between two types of exclusive disjunctions: one that prevents both conjunction and void, and another that prevents only conjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal conditional form
Locate the statement 'Whenever A is B, then J is D' and recognize it as a universal affirmative conditional (sharṭiyya muttasila kulliyya mūjiba). Note the antecedent (muqaddam) 'A is B' and consequent (tālī) 'J is D'.
2
Derive the exclusive disjunction
From the true conditional, form the disjunction 'Either A is B or J is not D'. This is composed of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent. The author emphasizes that this is a true exclusive (munfaṣila ḥaqīqiyya) which prevents both parts being true together and both being false together.
The passage also lists other combinations, but the simplest form uses the antecedent and negation of the consequent.
3
Verify the type of exclusive disjunction
Check that the derived disjunction is of type 'māni'a min al-jam' wa al-khulū' (prevents both conjunction and void). If the conditional is true, then exactly one of the two disjuncts must be true. Contrast this with the weaker type 'māni'a min al-jam' dūna al-khulū' (prevents only conjunction) which allows both to be false.
The passage begins to discuss the weaker type but cuts off; focus on the main type.
4
Recognize the multiplicity of derived disjunctions
The author notes that many such exclusive disjunctions can be derived from a single conditional by using different combinations of terms (e.g., antecedent and negation of equivalent terms). This multiplicity increases with negative conditionals.

وأما الثاني من المباحث فذلك في اللوازم من المنفصلات الموجبة الكلية التي مرَّ ذكرها والسالبة كذلك. أما الموجبة فنقول : إذا صدق قولنا كلَّما كان أب» (فـ (ج) د فقد صدق من المنفصلات الحقيقية ما يتركب من «ج» د» المقدم ونقيض التالي، فيقال دائما إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن لا يكون «ج د». وكذلك يتركب من أحدهما ونقيض التالي، ومن نقيض أحدهما وعين المقدم أيضًا. وكذلك من عين المقدم ونقيض ما يساوي التالي، ومن نقيض المقدم وعين ما يساوي التالي. وكذلك عين ما يساوي المقدم ونقيض التالي، ومن نقيض ما يساوي المقدم وعين التالي. وعلى هذا في كل واحدة من الموجبات التي مرَّ ذكرها في اللوازم؛ فإنه كما يتركب من المقدم ونقيض التالي شرطية سالبة من المتصلات فكذلك يتركب منهما شرطية موجبة من المنفصلات؛ وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو فيما نحن بصدده. ولو كان كذلك لكانت المنفصلات الحقيقية في هذه الصور متعددة حسب تعدد الموجبات فيها . ومتعددة أيضًا حسب تعدد السالبات لكن التعدد في السوالب أكثر بالنسبة لما مرَّ من قبل أن السالبة لا تفتقر في تحققها إلى ما تفتقر إليه الموجبة. فأما المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو فمثل ما يتركب من عين المقدم ونقيض ما هو الأعم
As for the second of the investigations, that is concerning the necessary consequences from the universal affirmative exclusives that have been mentioned, and likewise the negative. As for the affirmative, we say: if our statement 'Whenever A is B, then J is D' is true, then among the true exclusives there is true that which is composed of the antecedent 'J is D' and the negation of the consequent, so it is always said: 'Either A is B or J is not D'. And likewise it is composed of one of them and the negation of the consequent, and of the negation of one of them and the exact antecedent. And likewise from the exact antecedent and the negation of that which is equivalent to the consequent, and from the negation of the antecedent and the exact of that which is equivalent to the consequent. And likewise the exact of that which is equivalent to the antecedent and the negation of the consequent, and from the negation of that which is equivalent to the antecedent and the exact of the consequent. And on this basis for each of the affirmatives that have been mentioned among the necessary consequences; for just as a conditional negative from the connecteds is composed from the antecedent and the negation of the consequent, so too an affirmative conditional from the exclusives is composed from them; and it is the type that prevents conjunction and void in our discussion. And if it were so, then the true exclusives in these forms would be multiple according to the multiplicity of affirmatives in them. And they are also multiple according to the multiplicity of negatives, but the multiplicity in negatives is greater relative to what has passed, because the negative does not require for its realization what the affirmative requires. Commentary
This passage is the second investigation (al-mabḥath) dealing with the necessary consequences of universal conditionals in the form of exclusive disjunctions (al-munfaṣilāt). The author begins by stating that this investigation concerns both affirmative and negative universal conditionals that have been previously mentioned. For the affirmative case, he gives an example: if the universal conditional 'Whenever A is B, then J is D' is true, then from it we can derive a true exclusive disjunction. Specifically, we take the antecedent 'J is D' (but note: the text seems to have a variable mix-up; it says the disjunction is composed of 'J is D' as the antecedent and the negation of the consequent? Actually the text says 'يتركب من «ج» د» المقدم ونقيض التالي' — it is composed of 'J is D' the antecedent and the negation of the consequent. However, in the example given, the disjunction is 'Either A is B or J is not D'. So the antecedent being 'J is D' likely refers to the antecedent of the conditional? Let's parse: The original conditional is 'كلَّما كان أب فـ ج د' meaning 'Whenever A is B, then J is D'. So the antecedent is 'A is B' and the consequent is 'J is D'. The derived disjunction is 'Either A is B or J is not D', i.e., 'إما أن يكون أب وإما أن لا يكون ج د'. That is composed of the antecedent of the conditional (A is B) and the negation of the consequent (J is not D). So the text's phrasing 'يتركب من «ج» د» المقدم ونقيض التالي' seems to be a scribal error or misreading: it should be 'يتركب من المقدم ونقيض التالي' (composed of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent). The author clarifies: 'فقال دائما إما أن يكون «أب» وإما أن لا يكون «ج د»' (so it is always said: either A is B or J is not D).

The author then lists various ways to compose exclusive disjunctions: by using the antecedent and negation of consequent, by using one of them and negation of the other, by using the exact antecedent and the negation of what is equivalent to the consequent, etc. In each case, the resulting disjunction is a true exclusive (al-munfaṣila al-ḥaqīqiyya) which prevents both conjunction (i.e., both parts being true) and void (i.e., both parts being false). This is the same as the standard logical equivalence between a conditional and a disjunction.

He notes that the number of such true exclusive disjunctions increases according to the number of affirmative conditionals, and even more according to the number of negative conditionals, because a negative conditional does not require as many conditions for its realization as an affirmative does.

Finally, he begins to discuss another type of exclusive: the one that prevents conjunction only (al-māni'a min al-jam' dūn al-khulū), giving an example composed of the exact antecedent and the negation of what is more general. The passage cuts off here.

Deriving Disjunctive Propositions from Conditionals
Author explains how to derive universal disjunctive propositions from conditionals, covering positive and negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a true disjunctive proposition from a true conditional using the rule of antecedent and negation of consequent, and classify the disjunctive type.

Core Idea
A true conditional yields a true disjunctive: either the antecedent or the negation of the consequent.

This lesson unpacks the second topic of the section on concomitants (al-lawāzim). The author provides a systematic method for converting a true conditional statement into a true disjunctive proposition, which is a fundamental operation in syllogistic logic. Understanding this derivation is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic legal theory and theology, where conditional premises often need to be transformed into disjunctions for analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional
Locate the universal conditional proposition in the form 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' (e.g., 'If Zayd prays, he faces the qibla'). The conditional must be true for the derivation to be valid.
The conditional must express necessary concomitance (luzūm).
2
Extract antecedent and consequent
Separate the antecedent (muqaddam, e.g., 'Zayd prays') and the consequent (tālī, e.g., 'he faces the qibla'). Also note their negations.
3
Form the disjunctive with antecedent and negation of consequent
The author states: from the true conditional, compose a real disjunctive using the antecedent and the negation of the consequent: 'Either A is B or C is D is not the case'. This yields a disjunctive that is preventive of both conjunction and emptiness (māniʿatu min al-jamʿi wa al-khuluw).
Other combinations are also possible, but this is the primary derivation.
4
Recognize the disjunctive type
Identify whether the derived disjunctive is preventive of conjunction alone, emptiness alone, or both. The author distinguishes these based on the components used (e.g., exact antecedent and negation of what is more general than the consequent produce preventive of conjunction alone).
The multiplicity of disjunctives follows the multiplicity of 'more general' relations.
5
Extend to universal negatives
Understand that a universal negative conditional (e.g., 'It is not the case that whenever A is B, then C is D') may or may not entail the universal positive disjunctive. If it does, it entails all concomitants of the positive; if not, no entailment holds.
This nuance is critical for assessing the relationship between negation of opposition (ʿinād) and negation of concomitance (luzūm).

وأما الثاني من المباحث فذلك في اللوازم من المنفصلات الموجبة الكلية التي مرَّ ذكرها والسالبة كذلك. أما الموجبة فنقول : إذا صدق قولنا كلَّما كان أب» (فـ (ج) د فقد صدق من المنفصلات الحقيقية ما يتركب من المقدم ونقيض ما هو الأعم من التالي، والتعدد فيهما على حسب التعدد في الأعم، وكذلك من عين التالي ونقيض ما هو الأعم من المقدم. وأما المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع فمثل ما يتركب من عين المقدم وعين ما هو الأعم من نقيض التالي، وكذلك من عين التالي وعين ما هو الأعم من نقيض المقدم، والتعدد فيهما على حسب التعدد في الأعم / أيضًا. وعلى هذا في السوالب فإن الموجبة من المنفصلات لا تصدق حقيقية كانت أو غير حقيقية إلا وأن تصدق في ضمنها سالبة من المنفصلات. وذلك لأن الشيء لا يكون معاندًا للشيء إلا وأن يكون مستلزما لما يعانده، ولا يكون مستلزما لذلك إلا ويصح نفي العناد على ما عرف من قبل، هذا هو الكلام في الموجبة الكلية. وأما السالبة الكلية فالسالبة الكلية قد تكون مستلزمة للموجبة الكلية كما مرّ ذكرها من قبل، وقد لا تكون. أما المستلزمة فإنها كما هي مستلزمة للموجبة فكذلك لجميع ما يكون من اللوازم للموجبة من المنفصلات الحقيقية وغير الحقيقية موجبة وسالبة . وأما اللا مستلزمة فإنها لا تكون مستلزمة لا للموجبات ولا للسوالب لما أن نفي العناد قد يفارق نفي اللزوم، وقد لا يفارق؛ ونفي اللزوم قد يفارق نفي العناد، وقد لا يفارق أيضًا. ولو كان كذلك فلا يحكم بالنفي عند النفي ولا بالإثبات أصلا، فلا يلزمها من الموجبات والسوالب إذن في هذه الصورة على خلاف ما إذا كانت مستلزمة للموجبة الكلية. ثم إذا عرفت من اللوازم ما عرفته في الشرطية التي مر ذكرها بالإيجاب تارةً وبالسلب أخرى، فقد بان لك الطريق في الغير من الشرطيات المتصلة على وجه يمكن التطرق في الكل إلى اللوازم الكلية والجزئية من الموجبات تلك اللوازم، أو من السوالب.
As for the second topic, that is about the concomitants of universal positive disjunctives mentioned earlier and also the negative ones. As for the positive, we say: If our statement 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is true, then from the real disjunctives is true what is composed of the antecedent 'C is D' and the negation of the consequent, so it is always said: 'Either A is B or C is D is not the case.' And similarly it is composed of one of them and the negation of the consequent, and of the negation of one of them and the exact antecedent. And also from the exact antecedent and the negation of what is equal to the consequent, and from the negation of the antecedent and the exact what is equal to the consequent. And also the exact equal of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent, and from the negation of the equal of the antecedent and the exact consequent. And so on for each of the positives mentioned concerning the concomitants; for just as from the antecedent and the negation of the consequent is composed a negative conditional from the conjunctives, so also from them is composed a positive conditional from the disjunctives; and it is the preventive of conjunction and emptiness in what we are dealing with. And if it were so, then the real disjunctives in these forms would be multiple according to the multiplicity of positives in them, and also multiple according to the multiplicity of negatives, but the multiplicity in negatives is greater relative to what has preceded, because the negative does not require for its realization what the positive requires. As for the preventive of conjunction alone without emptiness, it is like what is composed from the exact antecedent and the negation of what is more general than the consequent, and the multiplicity in them is according to the multiplicity of the more general, and likewise from the exact consequent and the negation of what is more general than the antecedent. As for the preventive of emptiness alone without conjunction, it is like what is composed from the exact antecedent and the exact what is more general than the negation of the consequent, and likewise from the exact consequent and the exact what is more general than the negation of the antecedent, and the multiplicity in them is according to the multiplicity of the more general as well. And so on for the negatives, for the positive of disjunctives is not true, whether real or non-real, unless within it a negative of disjunctives is also true. This is because a thing is not opposed to a thing unless it necessitates what opposes it, and it does not necessitate that unless the negation of opposition is valid, as was known before. This is the discussion on the universal positive. As for the universal negative, the universal negative may be necessitating of the universal positive, as mentioned before, or may not. As for the necessitating, just as it necessitates the positive, so also it necessitates all that are among the concomitants of the positive, from real and non-real disjunctives, positive and negative. As for the non-necessitating, it does not necessitate either positives or negatives, because the negation of opposition may depart from the negation of concomitance, or may not; and the negation of concomitance may depart from the negation of opposition, or may not as well. And if it were so, then one does not judge negation upon negation nor affirmation at all, so it does not necessitate from the positives and negatives therefore in this form, contrary to when it is necessitating of the universal positive. Then, when you have known from the concomitants what you have known about the conditional mentioned sometimes affirmatively and sometimes negatively, then the path is clear to you for other conjunctive conditionals, in a way that it is possible to reach in all of them the universal and particular concomitants, from the positives of those concomitants, or from the negatives.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on deriving disjunctive propositions (منفصلات) from conditional propositions (متصلات) that involve necessary concomitance (لزوم). The author is explaining the second topic of this section, focusing on the concomitants of universal positive disjunctives and their negative counterparts. He begins by stating the rule: if a universal conditional 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is true, then from it we can derive a real disjunctive proposition composed of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent, yielding 'Either A is B or C is D is not the case.' He then lists various combinations: using one of the parts and the negation of the other, using the negation of one part and the exact other, and using equals. The author emphasizes that this derivation works for all positive forms mentioned earlier, and just as a negative conditional is composed from the antecedent and negation of the consequent, so too is a positive disjunctive composed from the same two components, specifically the type that prevents both conjunction and emptiness (المانعة من الجمع والخلو). He notes that the multiplicity of real disjunctives follows the multiplicity of positive and negative forms, with negatives having more multiplicity because negatives do not require the same conditions as positives. He then distinguishes two kinds of disjunctives: the preventive of conjunction alone (مانعة من الجمع دون الخلو), which is formed from the exact antecedent and the negation of what is more general than the consequent; and the preventive of emptiness alone (مانعة من الخلو دون الجمع), formed from the exact antecedent and the exact more general of the negation of the consequent. The multiplicity here corresponds to the multiplicity of 'more general' (أعم). Moving to the negative side, the author states that a positive disjunctive, whether real or non-real, is true only if within it a negative disjunctive is also true. This is because opposition (عناد) requires necessity (لزوم), and necessity allows for negation of opposition. Then he discusses the universal negative disjunctive: it may entail the universal positive or not. If it does, it entails all the concomitants of that positive. If it does not, then no entailment holds for either positives or negatives because the negation of opposition and negation of concomitance can diverge. Finally, the author concludes that once these relationships are understood for conditionals, one can apply the same method to all conjunctive conditionals to derive both universal and particular concomitants, whether positive or negative.

Mutual Entailment and Conversion of Conditionals
Author explains when conditionals are mutually entailing and convertible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two conditional propositions are mutually entailing and convertible based on the agreement of their antecedents and consequents.

Core Idea
Conditionals are equivalent only when their structures align in antecedent and consequent.

In Islamic logic, knowing when conditionals imply each other is critical for valid inference, especially in legal reasoning. This lesson unpacks the rule that mutual entailment and conversion depend on how the parts of the conditionals match. We will explore the criteria and apply them to examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditionals
Write out the two conditional statements clearly, labeling antecedent and consequent for each.
2
Compare antecedents and consequents
Check whether the antecedents are identical, partially overlapping, or completely different. Do the same for the consequents.
Agreement can be full, partial, or none.
3
Apply the mutual entailment rule
The author states that conditionals that agree in both parts, or in one part while differing in the other, may be mutually entailing if they fit the pattern. Test if the conditionals satisfy the pattern of equivalence.
The pattern involves a set of conditionals that are all equivalent, as illustrated by the examples.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Take a conditional from fiqh, e.g., 'If the sale is valid, then ownership transfers.' Construct its contrapositive: 'If ownership does not transfer, then the sale is not valid.' Are these mutually entailing? Check the agreement.

وأما الثالث من المباحث فذلك في بيان ما يكون من الشرطيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة وما تقدم ذكره من الضوابط فيها، فذلك من جملة ما يحكى عنها متصلة كانت أو منفصلة بأنها قد تكون متلازمة ومتعاكسة موجبة كانت أو سالبة كلية كانت أو جزئية. أما المتصلات فإنها لا يخلو من أن تكون متوافقة في المقدم والتالي، أو لا تكون بل تكون متواقفة في المقدم دون التالي أو في التالي دون المقدم والمتخالفة في الصورتين إذا كانت متلازمة ومتعاكسة كانت المتصلات كلها مستلزمة ومتعاكسة على هذا المثال: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «هز» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «هز» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب ج» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب ج» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «به» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «به» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب ز» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب ز» وعلى هذا إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما يقال: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «هز» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «هـ ز» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب ج» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «ب د» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب د» فليس «ج د»
As for the third investigation, that is an exposition of what conditionals are mutually entailing and convertible, and the rules previously mentioned concerning them. That is part of what is said about them, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, that they may be mutually entailing and convertible, whether positive or negative, universal or particular. As for conjunctive conditionals, they are not free from either being in agreement in antecedent and consequent, or not, rather they are in agreement in the antecedent without the consequent, or in the consequent without the antecedent. Those differing in the two forms, if they are mutually entailing and convertible, then the conjunctive conditionals are all entailing and convertible according to this example: Whenever 'ab' then 'jd' absolutely not 'ab' then not 'jd'? Whenever 'ab' then 'hz' absolutely not if 'ab' then not 'hz'? Whenever 'ab' then 'bj' absolutely not if 'ab' then not 'bj'? Whenever 'ab' then 'bd' absolutely not if 'ab' then not 'bd'? Whenever 'ab' then 'bh' absolutely not if 'ab' then not 'bh'? Whenever 'ab' then 'bz' absolutely not if 'ab' then not 'bz'? And likewise if the matter is reversed, as it is said: Whenever 'ab' then 'jd' absolutely not if 'ab' then not 'jd' absolutely not if 'hz' then not 'jd'? Whenever 'hz' then 'jd'? Whenever 'bj' then 'jd' absolutely not if 'bj' then not 'jd'? Whenever 'bd' then 'jd' absolutely not if 'bd' then not 'jd'?
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage introduces the third investigation of the section, which concerns the mutual entailment and conversion (تلازم وتعاكس) of conditional propositions, both conjunctive (متصلات) and disjunctive (منفصلات). The author states that conditionals may be mutually entailing and convertible regardless of quality (positive/negative) or quantity (universal/particular). For conjunctive conditionals, they may be in agreement in both antecedent and consequent, or only in one of them. When they differ in form, if they are mutually entailing and convertible, then all such conjunctive conditionals follow a pattern. The author provides a series of examples using letter variables (أب, ج د, هز, ب ج, etc.) to illustrate the pattern. The examples show that if a conditional of the form 'Whenever ab then jd' is true, then 'Whenever ab then not jd' is false, and conversely. The pattern is extended to other antecedents (hz, bj, bd, etc.) with the same consequent jd. The passage ends with examples where the order is reversed, showing the conversion. The precise logical relationships are meant to demonstrate the conditions under which conditionals are equivalent.

Deriving Conditional Implications from Exclusive Disjunction
Author derives conditional implications from exclusive disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four conditional implications from an exclusive disjunction and verify their validity.

Core Idea
An exclusive disjunction logically implies four conditionals that express mutual exclusivity and exhaustiveness.

In this passage, the author shows that from an exclusive disjunction (mana'a min al-jam' wa al-khulu) we can derive a set of conditional statements. These implications are immediate inferences that flow directly from the meaning of exclusivity. Understanding these connections deepens your ability to analyze disjunctive propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive disjunction
The author gives the form 'دائما كل أ إما ب وإما ج' (always, every A is either B or C). Recognize that this is an exclusive disjunction (truth when exactly one disjunct is true).
Exclusive disjunction is also called al-munfaṣila al-ḥaqīqiyya.
2
Derive mutual exclusivity conditionals
From the exclusivity we get: 'whenever it is B, it is not C' and 'whenever it is C, it is not B'. These are direct consequences of the 'māni'a min al-jam' (preventing conjunction) aspect.
These are conditional propositions with antecedents 'B' or 'C' and consequents negating the other.
3
Derive exhaustiveness conditionals
From the exclusivity we also get: 'whenever it is not B, it is C' and 'whenever it is not C, it is B'. These follow from the 'māni'a min al-khulu' (preventing both false) aspect.
These are conditionals with negative antecedents and affirmative consequents.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The author states: 'if a thing implies a thing, it opposes its contradictory'. This is the principle of contraposition, which justifies the derivations.
This principle ensures that the implications are logically valid.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the exclusive disjunction 'كل صلاة إما واجبة أو سنة' (Every prayer is either obligatory or supererogatory). Derive the four conditionals for this example.
Use the same pattern: if obligatory then not supererogatory, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في الأول والثاني من المباحث في اللوازم من المتصلات والمنفصلات. وأما المنفصلات فالحقيقية منها وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو مستلزمة للمتصلات والمنفصلات موجبة وسالبة وكلية وجزئية كذلك لما مر من قبل أن الشيء إذا كان مستلزما للشيء كان معاندا لما يناقضه، وبالعكس. أما المتصلات من اللوازم فبينة؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»، فقد صدق قولنا كلما كان «ب» فليس «ج»، كلما كان «ج» فليس «ب»، كلما لم يكن «ب» فهو «ج»، كلما لم يكن «ج» فهو «ب». وكما أن كونه اب مستلزم لعدم كونه «ج»، فكذلك مستلزم لعدم ما يكون مستلزما لـ«ج». وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون مستلزما له مساويًا كان ذلك المستلزم، أو أخص منه. وكذلك كونه ج لعدم ب ( ولعدم ما يكون مستلزما له ) وإنه متعدد أيضًا حسب تعدد ما يكون مستلزما له. وأيضًا كما أن عدم (ب) مستلزم لـ «ج»، فكذلك مستلزم لوجود ما يكون من لوازمه، وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم وكذلك عدم «ج» لوجود (ب) ولوجود ما يكون من لوازمه؛ وإنه متعدد أيضًا حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم.
Chapter: On the first and second investigations regarding the implications of conditionals and disjunctives. As for the disjunctives, the genuine ones — which are the exclusive disjunction (mana'a min al-jam' wa al-khulu) — imply conditionals and disjunctives, both affirmative and negative, universal and particular, likewise, because of what has passed: that if a thing implies a thing, it opposes its contradictory, and vice versa. As for the conditionals among the implications, they are evident: because if our statement 'always, every A is either B or C' is true, then our statements are true: 'whenever it is B, it is not C', 'whenever it is C, it is not B', 'whenever it is not B, it is C', 'whenever it is not C, it is B'. And just as its being B implies its not being C, so likewise it implies the non-existence of what implies C. And this is multiple according to the multiplicity of what implies it, whether that implicant is equal or more specific. And likewise its being C implies its not being B, and the non-existence of what implies B. And it is also multiple according to the multiplicity of what implies it. Also, just as the non-existence of B implies C, so likewise it implies the existence of whatever is among its implications, and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of what is among the implications. And likewise the non-existence of C implies the existence of B and the existence of whatever is among its implications; it is also multiple according to the multiplicity of what is among the implications.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the first and second investigations concerning the implications (lawāzim) of conditional and disjunctive propositions. The author focuses on the genuine disjunctive (al-munfaṣila al-ḥaqīqiyya), which is the exclusive disjunction (māni'a min al-jam' wa al-khulu), meaning it is true when exactly one of the two disjuncts is true. He states that this exclusive disjunction implies several conditional and disjunctive propositions, both affirmative and negative, universal and particular. The reason given is a principle: if something implies something else, then it opposes its contradictory, and vice versa. This is a restatement of contraposition. The evident conditionals derived from the exclusive disjunction 'دائما كل أ إما ب وإما ج' (always, every A is either B or C) are: (1) whenever it is B, it is not C; (2) whenever it is C, it is not B; (3) whenever it is not B, it is C; (4) whenever it is not C, it is B. The author then extends this: from 'being B' we also infer the negation of anything that implies C, meaning if X implies C, then 'being B' implies 'not X'. Similarly, from 'being not B' we infer the existence of C and also the existence of anything that is implied by C (i.e., its logical consequences). The multiplicity accounts for the fact that there may be many such implications. This analysis shows the rich logical relationships that flow from a single exclusive disjunction.

مانعة من الجمع والخلو
اللوازم
مستلزم
معاند

Key Takeaway
From an exclusive disjunction, we can derive four conditionals that capture mutual exclusivity and exhaustiveness, which are immediate logical consequences.

Three Modal Materials in Conditional Propositions
Classification of modal materials in conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition according to its modal material (necessity, impossibility, possibility) and derive its immediate implications.

Core Idea
Every conditional judgment fits one of three modal materials: necessity, impossibility, or possibility.

Just as categorical propositions can be necessarily true, necessarily false, or contingent, conditional propositions also fall into these three modal materials. This lesson unpacks the parallel classification and the logical entailments that follow, including the layers of modal propositions and their mutual implications. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to evaluate conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modal materials in conditionals
Locate in the passage where the author states that the judgment of implication (luzūm) must be either necessary of existence (material of necessity), necessary of non-existence (material of impossibility), or neither (material of possibility). This mirrors categoricals.
These correspond to māddat al-ījāb, māddat al-imtinā‘, and māddat al-imkān.
2
Examine the entailments for each material
Notice that in necessity, truth is necessary; in impossibility, falsehood is necessary; in possibility, neither truth nor falsehood is forced. The author says: 'truth in material of necessity is necessary, and falsehood in material of impossibility is likewise; in possibility, neither this nor that follows.'
3
Understand the layers and their mutual implications
The author presents three layers: the first for necessity, second for impossibility, third for possibility. Each layer yields two propositions (e.g., 'It is impossible that it is not' and 'It is not impossible that it is not'). Together they produce sixteen mutual implications (sixteen relationships).
The phrase 'ست عشر متلازمات متعاكسات' indicates a table of sixteen cross-implications.
4
Apply the relations of generality and specificity
The author notes that judgments can be more general, more specific, or equal relative to each other. These relations also generate implications. For example, 'It is necessary that whenever it is thus...' implies 'It is possible generally that it is' and 'It is not possible specifically that it is not'.
The terms 'العامي' and 'الخاصي' (general and specific) are used here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في تلازم الشرطيات ] وكما أن الحكم في الحمليات قد يكون في مادة الإيجاب، وقد يكون في مادة الامتناع، وقد يكون في مادة الإمكان. فكذلك الحكم في الشرطيات؛ وذلك لأن الحكم باللزوم لا يخلو من أن يكون ضروري الوجود؛ وإنه في مادة الإيجاب أو ضروري العدم؛ وإنه في مادة الامتناع أو لا ضروري الوجود ولا ضروري العدم؛ وإنه في مادة الإمكان. ثم الصدق في مادة الإيجاب لازم والكذب في مادة الامتناع كذلك، فأما في مادة الإمكان فلا يلزم لا هذا ولا ذلك بل قد يكون هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وعلى هذا إذا كان الحكم بالعناد فإنه لا يخلو من أن يكون ضروري الوجود، أو ضروري العدم، أو لا هذا ولا ذلك؛ وهذه كلها بحسب الحكم مطلقا في هذا وفي ذلك. والمطلق قد يكون بالصدق، وقد لا يكون والحكم أي حكم كان وفي أي مادة كان فإنه قد يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى غيره، وقد يكون أخص، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مساويا؛ وهذه النسب من جملة ما يستلزم الملازمة بين البعض من الأحكام والبعض منها كذلك. وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا واجب أن يكون كلما كان كذا كان كذا؛ يلزمه وينعكس عليه ممتنع أن لا يكون كلما كان كذا كان كذا وكذلك ليس بممكن العامي أن لا يكون كلما كان كذا كان كذا؛ وهذه الملازمات في هذه الطبقات بهذه الصور: الطبقة الأولى للوجوب: ممتنع أن لا يكون كلما كان كذا كان كذا ليس بممتنع أن لا يكون كلما كان كذا كان كذا الطبقة الثانية للامتناع: ممتنع أن يكون ليس بممتنع أن يكون الطبقة الثالثة للإمكان: ممكن أن لا يكون ليس بممكن أن لا يكون فهذه الطبقات مع تقاسمها ست عشر متلازمات متعاكسات، ولكل واحدة منها لوازم أما طبقة واجب أن يكون فيلزمها: ممكن العامي أن يكون ليس بممكن الخاصي أن لا يكون
Chapter: On the implication of conditionals. And just as the judgment in categoricals may be in the material of necessity, and may be in the material of impossibility, and may be in the material of possibility, so too the judgment in conditionals. That is because the judgment of implication is not free from being necessary of existence—and it is in the material of necessity—or necessary of non-existence—and it is in the material of impossibility—or neither necessary of existence nor necessary of non-existence—and it is in the material of possibility. Then truth in the material of necessity is necessary, and falsehood in the material of impossibility is likewise. As for the material of possibility, neither this nor that follows, but sometimes it is this without that, and vice versa. And similarly, if the judgment is of opposition, it is not free from being necessary of existence, or necessary of non-existence, or neither this nor that. And all these are according to the judgment absolutely in this and in that. And the absolute may be with truth or may not be. And the judgment, whatever judgment it is and in whatever material it is, may be more general relative to another, or more specific, or neither but equal. And these relations are among what necessitate implication between some of the judgments and some similarly. That is because if our statement 'It is necessary that whenever it is thus it is thus' is true, there follows from it and is convertible with it 'It is impossible that it is not the case that whenever it is thus it is thus' and also 'It is not possible generally that it is not the case that whenever it is thus it is thus'. And these implications in these layers are in these forms: The first layer for necessity: [ ] 'It is impossible that it is not the case that whenever it is thus it is thus' 'It is not impossible that it is not the case that whenever it is thus it is thus'. The second layer for impossibility: 'It is impossible that it is' 'It is not impossible that it is'. The third layer for possibility: 'It is possible that it is not' 'It is not possible that it is not'. So these layers with their division are sixteen mutual implications, and for each one there are implications. As for the layer of 'It is necessary that it is', there follows from it: 'It is possible generally that it is' and 'It is not possible specifically that it is not'.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and classificatory exposition of the modal materials in conditional propositions, analogous to those in categoricals. The author begins by stating that just as categorical judgments (ḥamliyyāt) can be in three materials—necessity (māddat al-ījāb), impossibility (māddat al-imtinā‘), and possibility (māddat al-imkān)—so too conditional judgments (sharṭiyyāt) follow the same tripartite division. For the judgment of implication (luzūm), it must be either necessarily true (necessity), necessarily false (impossibility), or contingent (possibility). Truth is necessary in the material of necessity, falsehood in material of impossibility; in possibility, neither is fixed. The same applies to the judgment of opposition (‘inād). The author then introduces relations of generality and specificity between propositions, which also produce implications. He illustrates with an example: the statement 'It is necessary that whenever it is thus it is thus' implies 'It is impossible that it is not the case' and also 'It is not possible generally that it is not'. Then he lists three layers of modal propositions: the first layer for necessity gives two propositions (impossibility of negation and not impossibility of negation?); the second for impossibility (impossibility of affirmation and not impossibility of affirmation); the third for possibility (possibility of negation and not possibility of negation). He notes that together these layers produce sixteen mutual implications (each proposition implies others in a cross-pairing). For the layer of necessity, it entails 'It is possible generally that it is' and 'It is not possible specifically that it is not'. This is a dense technical classification that expands the theory of modality from categoricals to conditionals, emphasizing the systematic relationships between modal statements in conditional form.

B) Truth is necessary
C) Truth is possible but not necessary
D) Truth is neither necessary nor impossible

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions, like categoricals, are classified by their modal material—necessity, impossibility, or possibility—which determines the logical necessity of their truth or falsehood and generates systematic immediate implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the modal material of the following conditional from a fatwa: 'If the sale involves gharar (uncertainty), then it is prohibited.' Is it necessity, impossibility, or possibility? Explain using the passage's criteria.
?
Construct a conditional proposition from Islamic inheritance law (e.g., 'If the deceased has a son, then the daughter receives half of the son's share') and classify its material. Then derive one immediate implication (e.g., what is impossible or possible given this conditional).

Absolute and Conditional Necessity in Conditionals
Classification of conditionals into absolute and modal with types of necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional proposition as absolute or modal, and within modal, distinguish absolute necessity from conditional necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity in conditionals is either absolute or conditional on subject existence.

The author now extends the discussion of modalities from categorical to conditional propositions. He addresses an objection similar to one raised before about necessary consequence, then introduces a key classification: conditionals can be absolute (no modality) or modal. Within modal, necessity and perpetuity are central, with necessity being more specific. He further divides necessity into absolute and conditional, providing examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection: a necessary consequence must be different in meaning from that which it follows, otherwise it would be self-implication. The author replies that only partial difference is needed.
This objection parallels one for categoricals.
2
Classify conditionals as absolute or modal
A conditional proposition may be absolute (no modality) or modal (with modality such as necessity or perpetuity). Recognize which type the author introduces.
Absolute conditionals are unqualified; modal ones include a modal operator.
3
Understand necessity vs perpetuity
Necessity is more specific than perpetuity: every necessary judgment is perpetual, but not vice versa. Perpetuity is more specific than absolute generality.
This hierarchy is key for modal reasoning.
4
Distinguish absolute necessity
Absolute necessity holds perpetually without any condition. Example: 'If something is eternal, it is impossible for it to be preceded by another.' The necessity here is unconditioned.
The judgment is always true regardless of subject existence.
5
Distinguish conditional necessity
Conditional necessity holds necessarily only under a condition, typically the perpetual existence of the subject. Example: 'Whenever something is a human, it is necessarily an animal.' This depends on the essence of the subject.
The necessity is tied to the subject's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول في الشرطيات مثل الشبهة التي مرَّ ذكرها في الحمليات إن قولنا واجب أن يكون لا يلزمه قولنا ممتنع أن لا يكون إلا وأن يكون مغايرا له في المعنى، فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء لازما لنفسه، وكذلك لا يلزمه قولنا ليس بممكن العامي أن لا يكون لكنا نقول: لا يلزم أن يكون اللازم مغايرا للملزوم في جميع الوجوه بل يلزم أن يغاير بوجه ما كما مرَّ في الحمليّات. فصل : في جهات الشرطيات واعلم بأن الشرطيات كما يمكن إيجادها مع الحمليات في هذه الأحكام فكذلك يمكن في أحكام الجهات وأقسامها وذلك لأن القضية الشرطية قد تكون مطلقةً؛ وهي المحذوف عنها الجهة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون موجهة بجهة ما؛ وهي بيان حال الحكم بحسب الضرورة أو بحسب الدوام مثلا: فالضرورة أخص من الدوام لما أن كل ضروري دائم؛ ولا ينعكس، والدوام أخص من الإطلاق العام . ثمَّ الضرورة قد تكون مطلقة وهي التي يكون الحكم فيها دائم الوجود لم يزل ولا يزال من غير شرط ضرورة دوام المقدم. وعلى حسب دوام التالي كذلك كما في قولنا لو كان الشيء أزليا لكان من المحال أن يكون مسبوقاً بالغير؛ إذ الضرورة فيها مطلقة غير معلقة بشرط ما البتة. وما يكون كذلك فهو دائم الوجود لم يزل ولا يزال وإلا لكان معلقا بشرط، وقد تكون مشروطة؛ والشرط فيها إما دوام وجود الذات وإما غيره. والغير إما أن يكون في جانب المقدم أو في جانب التالي، أو لا في جانب المقدم ولا في جانب التالي. أما الأول فمثاله كلما كان الشيء إنساناً كان حيوانا بالضرورة؛ إذ الضرورة فيه معلقة بشرط دوام وجود ذات ذلك الشيء. وهذا من جملة ما قد مر الكلام فيه أن الضرورة في هذه الصورة هي الضرورة المشروطة أم غير المشروطة.
Then one may object in conditional propositions similar to the doubt that was mentioned in categoricals: that our statement 'necessary to be' does not necessarily imply our statement 'impossible not to be' unless it is different from it in meaning, for it is impossible for a thing to be necessary to itself, and likewise it does not imply our statement 'not generally possible that it is not'. But we say: it is not necessary that the necessary consequence be different from that which is necessary in all respects; rather it is necessary that it differs in some respect, as was mentioned in categoricals. Chapter: On the Modalities of Conditionals. And know that conditionals, just as they can be considered together with categoricals in these rulings, so too can they be considered in the rulings of modalities and their divisions. That is because a conditional proposition may be absolute, which is that from which modality is removed, or it may not be, rather it is modal with some modality; and it is a statement of the state of the judgment according to necessity or according to perpetuity, for example. So necessity is more specific than perpetuity because everything necessary is perpetual, and the converse does not hold; and perpetuity is more specific than absolute generality. Then necessity may be absolute, which is that in which the judgment is perpetually existent, not ceasing and not ceasing to be, without the condition of the necessity of the perpetuity of the antecedent. And likewise according to the perpetuity of the consequent, as in our statement: if something were eternal, it would be impossible for it to be preceded by another; for the necessity here is absolute, not conditioned by any condition at all. And whatever is like that is perpetually existent, not ceasing and not ceasing to be; otherwise it would be conditioned. And it may be conditional; and the condition in it is either the perpetuity of the existence of the essence or something else. And the other is either in the part of the antecedent or in the part of the consequent, or neither in the part of the antecedent nor in the part of the consequent. As for the first, its example is: whenever something is a human, it is necessarily an animal; for the necessity here is conditioned by the condition of the perpetuity of the existence of the essence of that thing. And this is among the things about which the discussion has passed: whether the necessity in this form is conditional necessity or non-conditional.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding conditionals similar to one raised for categoricals: that a necessary consequence must differ in meaning from that which it follows from, otherwise it would be self-implication. The author replies that the difference need only be in some respect, not all. Then begins a new section on the modalities of conditionals. He explains that conditionals can be absolute (without modality) or modal (with modality such as necessity or perpetuity). He states that necessity is more specific than perpetuity (since everything necessary is perpetual but not vice versa), and perpetuity is more specific than absolute (unmodalized) generality. He then divides necessity into absolute necessity, where the judgment holds perpetually without any condition, and conditional necessity, where the necessity is conditioned on something like the perpetual existence of the subject or something else. He gives examples: absolute necessity: 'if something is eternal, it cannot be preceded by another'; conditional necessity: 'whenever something is a human, it is necessarily an animal' — here the necessity depends on the perpetual existence of that human's essence. The passage ends by referencing a previous discussion about whether such necessity is conditional or not.

ضرورة مطلقة
ضرورة مشروطة
دوام

Key Takeaway
Necessity in conditionals is either absolute (unconditioned) or conditional (dependent on subject existence), and necessity is more specific than perpetuity.

Reply to an Objection on Implication in Conditionals
Author responds to an objection that necessity in conditionals requires complete identity between antecedent and consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection and response regarding the necessity of difference in conditional implications, and determine what kind of difference is required.

Core Idea
Implication does not require complete difference—only a difference in some aspect suffices.

This passage addresses a common doubt about conditionals: that for a conditional to be true, the antecedent and consequent must be entirely different in meaning, otherwise self-implication would be absurd. The author responds by clarifying that only a partial difference is needed, just as was established in categorical logic. Understanding this helps us see why many valid conditionals in Islamic legal reasoning are acceptable despite not having completely distinct terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that 'it is necessary that it is' does not entail 'it is impossible that it is not' unless the consequent differs from the antecedent in meaning. Find the exact phrasing in the Arabic.
Look for the phrase 'لا يلزمه قولنا ممتنع أن لا يكون إلا وأن يكون مغايرا له في المعنى'.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector claims that a thing cannot be necessary for itself, so without difference, the conditional would be a self-implication, which is impossible. Why does he think this follows?
Self-implication (a thing being necessary for itself) is considered invalid.
3
Extract the author's response
The author replies: 'it is not necessary that the necessary thing be different from its associate in all respects; rather, it is necessary that it differ from it in some aspect'. What is the key distinction here?
The author reduces the requirement from complete difference to partial difference.
4
Compare with categoricals
The author refers to 'as was mentioned in categoricals'. Recall that in categorical propositions, identity of subject and predicate is avoided by some distinction. How does this parallel the conditional case?
Both cases require only a difference in some respect, not total difference.
5
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
Does the author's response successfully address the objection? Why or why not? What is the logical move being made?
The response weakens the objector's required condition, making the conditional valid as long as there is some distinction, however slight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول في الشرطيات مثل الشبهة التي مرَّ ذكرها في الحمليات إن قولنا واجب أن يكون لا يلزمه قولنا ممتنع أن لا يكون إلا وأن يكون مغايرا له في المعنى، فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء لازما لنفسه، وكذلك لا يلزمه قولنا ليس بممكن العامي أن لا يكون لكنا نقول: لا يلزم أن يكون اللازم مغايرا الملزومه في جميع الوجوه بل يلزم أن يغايره بوجه ما كما مرَّ في الحمليات
Then an objector may say regarding conditionals, like the doubt that was mentioned in categoricals: that our statement 'it is necessary that it is' does not entail our statement 'it is impossible that it is not' except if it is different from it in meaning, for it is impossible for a thing to be necessary for itself. Likewise, our statement 'it is not possible in the common sense that it is not' does not entail it. But we say: it is not necessary that the necessary thing be different from its associate in all respects; rather, it is necessary that it differ from it in some aspect, as was mentioned in categoricals.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. An objector raises a doubt about conditional propositions similar to one previously discussed in categoricals. The doubt is: a conditional like 'if A, then necessarily B' requires that 'B' be different from 'A' in meaning, otherwise it would be self-implication, which is impossible. Similarly, the statement 'not possibly not B' cannot be entailed without difference. The author's response clarifies that implication does not require complete difference in all respects; a difference in some aspect is sufficient, as was established in categorical logic. This is a standard move in Islamic logic to allow for redundancy in conditionals while maintaining logical structure.

ممتنع أن لا يكون

Key Takeaway
Conditional implication does not require complete difference between antecedent and consequent; a difference in some aspect is sufficient.

Classification of Modal Conditionals
Author classifies necessary conditionals into absolute, general conditioned, specific conditioned, and time-relative types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between absolute, general conditioned, specific conditioned, and time-relative necessity in conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Modal conditionals vary by the type and condition of necessity, from absolute to time-relative.

In this passage, the author classifies conditional propositions based on the modality of necessity. You have already learned about absolute conditionals (without modality). Now, you will see how necessity can be absolute, conditioned by the essence or description, or tied to time. This classification is crucial for evaluating the strength of conditional claims in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hierarchy
The author first establishes that necessity is more specific than permanence, and permanence is more specific than absolute generality. This hierarchy underpins the entire classification.
This hierarchy is: absolute > permanence > necessity.
2
Identify absolute necessity
Absolute necessity has no condition; the judgment is permanently existent without any dependence on a condition. Example: 'If a thing is eternal, it cannot be preceded by another.'
This is the strongest form of necessity.
3
Distinguish conditioned necessity by essence
Conditioned necessity tied to the permanence of the subject's essence: e.g., 'Whenever something is human, it is necessarily animal.' The necessity holds as long as the essence exists.
This is the first type of conditioned necessity.
4
Analyze conditioned necessity by description
When necessity depends on a description of the antecedent or consequent: general conditioned (permanence of description only) vs. specific conditioned (adds a condition of non-permanence). For antecedent: 'Whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer' (general), and '...not always, but as long as he is a writer' (specific). Same for consequent.
Pay attention to the position: side of antecedent or consequent.
5
Categorize time-relative necessity
Necessity is tied to time: general time-relative (specific time, e.g., lunar eclipse), specific time-relative (specific time with non-permanence), general widespread (some unspecified time, e.g., 'every human breathes at some time'), and specific widespread (unspecified time with non-permanence, not fully discussed).
The text cuts off, but the pattern is clear: each type has a general and specific version.

[ب] وإما أن لا يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان كاتبًا كان متحرّكًا بالضرورة لا دائما بل ما دام متحركا وهذه هي المشروطة الخاصة من جانب التالي. وأما الرابع وهو الذي لا تكون الضرورة فيه بحسب كذا وكذا بل الضرورة فيه بحسب الوقت مثلا: [1] فذلك الوقت إما أن يكون وقتا معينا، وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلما كان القمر موجودًا وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس كان منخسفًا، وهذه هي الوقتية العامة. [ب] وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا، كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلَّما كان القمر موجودًا كان منخسفًا لكن لا دائما بل وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهذه هي الوقتية الخاصة. Know that conditional propositions, just as they can be combined with categoricals in these rulings, likewise they can be in the rulings of modalities and their divisions. That is because a conditional proposition may be absolute, which is the one from which modality is removed, and it may not be, but rather be modalized with some modality; and that is a statement of the state of the judgment according to necessity or according to permanence, for example: necessity is more specific than permanence because every necessary is permanent, but not conversely, and permanence is more specific than absolute generality. Then necessity may be absolute, which is the one in which the judgment is permanently existent, unceasing and without the condition of necessity of the permanence of the antecedent. And similarly according to the permanence of the consequent, as in our statement: if a thing is eternal, then it is impossible that it be preceded by another; for the necessity in it is absolute, not conditioned by any condition at all. And whatever is like that is permanently existent, unceasing, otherwise it would be conditioned. And it may be conditioned; and the condition in it is either the permanence of the existence of the essence or something else. And the other is either in the side of the antecedent or in the side of the consequent, or neither in the side of the antecedent nor in the side of the consequent. As for the first, its example is: whenever a thing is human, it is necessarily animal; for the necessity in it is conditioned by the condition of the permanence of the existence of that thing. This is among what has been discussed before, whether the necessity in this form is conditioned necessity or not. As for the second, which is the one in which necessity is according to the description of the antecedent: [a] that is either conditioned by the condition of permanence only, as in our statement: whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer; this proposition is called the general conditioned, as mentioned. [b] Or it may not be, but rather it is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement: whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving not always, but as long as he is a writer; this is the specific conditioned. As for the third, which is the one in which necessity is according to the description of the consequent: [a] that also is either conditioned by the condition of permanence only, as in our statement: whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is moving; this is the general conditioned from the side of the consequent. [b] Or it may not be, but rather it is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement: whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving not always, but as long as he is moving; this is the specific conditioned from the side of the consequent. As for the fourth, which is the one in which necessity is not according to such and such, but rather necessity is according to time, for example: [1] that time is either a specific time, and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, as in our statement: necessarily, whenever the moon exists at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, it is eclipsed; this is the general time-relative. [b] Or it may not be, but rather it is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement: necessarily, whenever the moon exists, it is eclipsed but not always, only at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun; this is the specific time-relative. [t] Or it may not be a specific time, and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, as in our statement: necessarily, whenever a human exists, he breathes at some time; this is the general widespread. [th] Or it may not be, but rather it is conditioned by the condition of non-
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of modal conditional propositions in Islamic logic. The author begins by noting that conditionals can be absolute (without modality) or modalized. Then he explains that necessity is more specific than permanence, and permanence is more specific than absolute. He then divides necessity into absolute and conditioned. Absolute necessity is unconditional and permanent. Conditioned necessity is tied to the permanence of the essence of the subject or something else. The 'something else' can be the description of the antecedent, the description of the consequent, or something else (like time). He provides examples for each: for the first type (conditioned by permanence of essence), the example 'whenever something is human, it is necessarily animal' shows a necessary connection that holds as long as the essence exists. For the second type (conditioned by description of antecedent), he distinguishes between general conditioned (e.g., 'whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer') and specific conditioned (which adds the condition of non-permanence: 'not always, but as long as he is a writer'). Similarly for the third type (conditioned by description of consequent). Finally, the fourth type is time-relative necessity, with subcategories: general time-relative (specific time only), specific time-relative (specific time but with non-permanence condition), general widespread (unspecified time, e.g., 'whenever a human exists, he breathes at some time'), and specific widespread (which would add a non-permanence condition, but the text cuts off). All examples are from natural phenomena or biological facts, reflecting the common practice in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
ضرورة مطلقة (ḍarūra muṭlaqa): Absolute necessity: a judgment that is permanently existent without any condition. [Note: In earlier logic, this term sometimes refers to necessity in the strongest sense.]
ضرورة مشروطة (ḍarūra mashrūṭa): Conditioned necessity: necessity that depends on a condition such as the permanence of the subject's essence or a temporal description.
مشروطة عامة (mashrūṭa ‘āmma): General conditioned proposition: necessity is conditioned by the permanence of the description of the antecedent or consequent only. [Note: In some later texts, this may be called 'ḍarūra mashrūṭa bi-sharṭ al-dawām'.]
مشروطة خاصة (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa): Specific conditioned proposition: necessity is conditioned by non-permanence in addition to the description.
وقتية عامة (waqtiyya ‘āmma): General time-relative: necessity holds only at a specific time, without a condition of non-permanence.
منتشرة عامة (muntashira ‘āmma): General widespread: necessity holds at some unspecified time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The classification of necessity in conditionals reveals that many seemingly necessary truths are actually conditioned by the permanence of the subject or time.

General and Special Conditional Propositions
Author defines two subtypes of conditionals based on antecedent's description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma) and the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) based on the condition of perpetuity or non-perpetuity in the antecedent's description.

Core Idea
The general conditional requires perpetual necessity; the special conditional only as long as the description holds.

In this passage, the author expands on the second division of conditional propositions where necessity is tied to the description of the antecedent. You will learn to identify two subtypes: the general conditional and the special conditional, based on whether the necessity is conditioned by perpetuity or non-perpetuity. These distinctions are crucial for understanding modal logic, especially in theological and jurisprudential arguments where the scope of necessity must be precisely defined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base concept
The passage deals with conditional propositions where necessity is 'with respect to the description of the antecedent' (بحسب وصف المقدم). This means the necessity of the consequent is tied to a specific property or description of the antecedent, not to its mere existence.
2
Distinguish the two subtypes
The author divides these conditionals into two: those conditioned by perpetuity (الدوام) and those conditioned by non-perpetuity (اللا دوام). Read the examples carefully: in the general conditional, the necessity holds 'as long as' (ما دام) the description persists. In the special conditional, it states 'not always, but only as long as' (لا دائما بل ما دام), meaning the necessity is not perpetual but still holds during the description.
The special conditional does not negate necessity; it restricts its duration.
3
Apply to examples
Practice identifying: given a statement like 'Whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer' – that is general. If the same statement adds 'not always' despite the condition, it becomes special. The key is whether the necessity is perpetual or duration-bound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو الذي تكون الضرورة فيه بحسب وصف المقدم: [أ] فذلك إما أن يكون مشروطًا بشرط الدوام أم لا غير كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان كاتبا كان متحركا بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا وهذه القضية تسمى بالمشروطة العامة كما مر . [ب] وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان كاتبا كان متحرّكًا بالضرورة لا دائما بل ما دام كاتبا وهذه هي المشروطة الخاصة.
As for the second, which is that in which necessity is with respect to the description of the antecedent: [A] That is either conditioned by the condition of perpetuity or not, as in our statement: 'Whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer.' This proposition is called the general conditional, as previously. [B] Or it is not, but rather is also conditioned by the condition of non-perpetuity, as in our statement: 'Whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving, not always but only as long as he is a writer.' This is the special conditional.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage defines two subdivisions of conditional propositions where necessity is attached to the description of the antecedent. The author first describes the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma), where the necessity holds with a condition of perpetuity (shatt al-dawam), meaning the necessity holds for as long as the antecedent's description is true. For example, 'Whenever a human is a writer, he is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer' — the necessity of movement is tied to the duration of being a writer. The second subdivision is the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), where the necessity is conditioned by non-perpetuity (shatt al-la dawam), meaning the necessity does not hold always but only for the duration of the antecedent's description. The example clarifies that the movement is necessary not always, but only as long as he is a writer. These distinctions are crucial for understanding modal conditionals in Islamic logic.

المشروطة العامة
المشروطة الخاصة

Four Temporal Subtypes of Conditional Propositions
Author defines four temporal subtypes of conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four temporal subtypes of conditional propositions based on time specificity and perpetuity condition.

Core Idea
Conditional necessity can be qualified by time: specific or not, perpetual or not.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya) may have their necessity tied to time. The author divides such conditionals into four subtypes based on two criteria: whether the time is specified or not, and whether the necessity holds perpetually or only at that time. These distinctions are essential for correctly analysing temporal conditions in arguments from astronomy, biology, and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two binary dimensions
The classification uses two independent dimensions: (1) specific vs non-specific time, (2) general (perpetuity) vs special (non-perpetuity). These combine to give four categories.
These dimensions are not exclusive; every temporal conditional belongs to exactly one cell of the 2x2 matrix.
2
Examine the first subtype: general temporal (al-waqtiyya al-'amma)
This involves a specific time and the condition of perpetuity. The author's example: 'Necessarily, whenever the moon exists at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, it is eclipsed.' The time (opposition) is specific and the eclipse occurs necessarily at that time without a non-perpetuity qualifier.
The necessity is tied to that specific time and holds throughout that time.
3
Examine the second subtype: special temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa)
This also involves a specific time but adds the condition of non-perpetuity. Example: 'Necessarily, whenever the moon exists, it is eclipsed, but not always, rather at the time of interposition.' The necessity is restricted to that time, not perpetual.
The qualifier 'but not always' distinguishes it from the general temporal.
4
Examine the third and fourth subtypes: pervasive (al-muntashira)
These involve non-specific time. The general pervasive (al-muntashira al-'amma) holds at some unspecified time: 'Necessarily, whenever a human exists, he breathes at some time.' The special pervasive (al-muntashira al-khassa) adds non-perpetuity: '...but not always, rather at some time.'
The time is not fixed; the necessity holds at some time, possibly different for each instance.
5
Practice distinguishing the four subtypes
Given a conditional statement, determine its subtype by checking (a) whether the time is specified, and (b) whether there is a non-perpetuity qualifier.
Focus on the words that indicate time specificity and the presence of phrases like 'but not always'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع وهو الذي لا تكون الضرورة فيه بحسب كذا وكذا بل الضرورة فيه بحسب الوقت مثلا: [1] فذلك الوقت إما أن يكون وقتا معينا، وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلما كان القمر موجودًا وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس كان منخسفًا، وهذه هي الوقتية العامة. [ب] وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا، كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلَّما كان القمر موجودًا كان منخسفًا لكن لا دائما بل وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهذه هي الوقتية الخاصة. [ت] وإما أن لا يكون وقتا معينا وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير أيضًا، كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلَّما كان الإنسان موجوداً كان متنفسا في وقت من الأوقات، وهذه هي المنتشرة العامة. [ث] وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا، كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلَّما كان الإنسان موجودا كان متنفسا لكن لا دائما بل في وقت من الأوقات، وهذه هي المنتشرة الخاصة؛ وهذا إذا كانت الضرورة بحسب الوقت.
As for the fourth, which is that in which necessity is not with respect to such and such, but rather necessity is with respect to time, for example: [1] That time is either a specific time, and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, as in our statement: 'Necessarily, whenever the moon exists at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, it is eclipsed.' This is the general temporal. [B] Or it may not be, but is also conditioned by the condition of non-perpetuity, as in our statement: 'Necessarily, whenever the moon exists, it is eclipsed, but not always, rather at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun.' This is the special temporal. [T] Or it may not be a specific time, and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, as in our statement: 'Necessarily, whenever a human exists, he breathes at some time.' This is the general pervasive. [Th] Or it may not be, but is also conditioned by the condition of non-perpetuity, as in our statement: 'Necessarily, whenever a human exists, he breathes, but not always, rather at some time.' This is the special pervasive; this is when the necessity is with respect to time.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of conditional propositions, where necessity is based on time. The author distinguishes two aspects: whether the time is specific or non-specific, and whether the condition is general (with perpetuity) or special (with non-perpetuity). The first subtype is the general temporal (al-waqtiyya al-'amma), where the necessity is tied to a specific time with a condition of perpetuity — the moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition. The second is the special temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa), where the same necessity is qualified by non-perpetuity: the eclipse occurs not always but only at that specific time. The third and fourth subtypes shift to non-specific time: the general pervasive (al-muntashira al-'amma) where the necessity holds at some unspecified time (e.g., human breathes at some time), and the special pervasive (al-muntashira al-khassa) where the necessity holds not always but at some time. The author uses concrete examples from astronomy (eclipse) and biology (breathing) to illustrate these logical forms. These distinctions are essential for correctly analyzing temporal conditions in arguments.

Key Terms
الوقتية العامة (al-waqtiyya al-'amma): A conditional proposition where the necessity is tied to a specific time with the condition of perpetuity at that time.
الوقتية الخاصة (al-waqtiyya al-khassa): A conditional proposition where the necessity is tied to a specific time with the condition of non-perpetuity.
المنتشرة العامة (al-muntashira al-'amma): A conditional proposition where the necessity is tied to a non-specific time with the condition of perpetuity (at some time).
المنتشرة الخاصة (al-muntashira al-khassa): A conditional proposition where the necessity is tied to a non-specific time with the condition of non-perpetuity.

C) Necessarily, whenever the moon exists, it is eclipsed, but not always, only at opposition.

Key Takeaway
Temporal conditionals are classified along two binary dimensions: time specificity (specific vs non-specific) and perpetuity (perpetual vs non-perpetual), yielding four distinct subtypes: general temporal, special temporal, general pervasive, and special pervasive.

Prior and Posterior Necessity and Perpetuity
Classification of necessity and perpetuity types in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish prior necessity from posterior necessity and identify the corresponding divisions of perpetuity in conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Necessity in conditionals is either prior (antecedent) or posterior (consequent); perpetuity mirrors these divisions.

Our author continues refining the modal analysis of conditionals. We have already seen that necessity can apply to different parts of a conditional. Now he formally distinguishes prior necessity (from the antecedent side) from posterior necessity (from the consequent side). He also introduces perpetuity as a parallel modal concept with analogous divisions. Understanding these categories is crucial for correctly analyzing the logical force of conditional statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessity
Locate where the author says 'necessity in all these forms is the prior necessity except what is from the side of the consequent; for it is the posterior necessity.' Notice that prior necessity applies to the antecedent's side, posterior to the consequent's side.
Prior necessity (al-darura al-sabiqa) and posterior necessity (al-darura al-lahiqa) are the two main categories.
2
Map the divisions of perpetuity
Find where the author says 'perpetuity is multiplied according to the multiplicity of necessity and divided into its divisions necessarily.' Observe that perpetuity can be absolute or conditioned, with the condition on the antecedent, consequent, or neither.
Perpetuity (al-dawam) has the same subdivisions as necessity, though different names are used.
3
Compare the names for necessity and perpetuity
Note the author's remark that 'the divisions under necessity are designated by names, and the divisions under perpetuity are designated by other names; and they are those which have been mentioned in categoricals.'
The specific names are not listed here, but refer back to the categorical modal classification.
