ثم الضرورة في جميع هذه الصور هي الضرورة السابقة إلا فيما يكون من جانب التالي؛ إذ هي الضرورة اللاحقة. وأما اللا ضرورة فإنها من حيث إنها [١٢٠] في مقابلة الضرورة متعددة بحسب التعدد / في الضرورة. وأما الدوام فإنه قد يكون مطلقًا أيضًا، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط ما؛ وذلك الشرط إما من جانب المقدم، أو من جانب التالي، أو لا من جانب المقدم ولا من جانب التالي كما مر في الضرورة والضرورة لما كانت مستلزمة للدوام فالدوام متعدد تعدد الضرورة ، ومنقسم إلى أقسامها لا محالة. فلا اختلاف بينهما في تلك الأقسام أصلا وإن كانت الأقسام تحت الضرورة مخصوصة بأسماء، والأقسام تحت الدوام مخصوصة بأسماء أخرى؛ وهي التي مرَّ ذكرها في الحمليات وبالجملة فالقضايا الشرطية بحسب الصورة موجهة بجهات كانت القضايا الحملية موجهة بتلك الجهات لما أنها قد تكون مطلقة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون ضرورية، أو دائمة، أو ممكنة خاصة، أو ممكنة عامة، أو لا ضروية ولا دائمة مثلا من الأقسام التي مرَّ ذكرها في الجهات فأما بحسب المعنى فالشرطية لا تكون إلا وأن تكون ضرورة متصلة كانت أو منفصلة. وذلك لأنه لا يمكن أن يكون الشيء مستلزما للشيء من غير أن يكون اللزوم ضروريا. والمستلزم عبارة عما يمتنع وجوده بدون غيره؛ وهو اللازم ولا يمكن أن يكون الشيء معاندًا للشيء أيضًا من غير أن يكون العناد ضروريًا. والمعاند عبارة عما يمتنع
Then, necessity in all these forms is the prior necessity except what is from the side of the consequent; for it is the posterior necessity. As for non-necessity, it is, insofar as it is opposite to necessity, multiple according to the multiplicity in necessity. As for perpetuity, it may also be absolute, or it may not be, but rather be conditioned by some condition; and that condition is either from the side of the antecedent, or from the side of the consequent, or not from the side of the antecedent nor from the side of the consequent, as passed in necessity. And since necessity entails perpetuity, perpetuity is multiplied according to the multiplicity of necessity and divided into its divisions necessarily. So there is no difference between them in those divisions at all, even though the divisions under necessity are designated by names, and the divisions under perpetuity are designated by other names; and they are those which have been mentioned in categoricals. In summary, conditional propositions, in terms of form, are modalized with the same modes as categorical propositions are modalized, because they may be absolute, or they may not be, but rather necessary, or perpetual, or possible special, or possible general, or neither necessary nor perpetual, for example, from the divisions mentioned in the modes. As for in terms of meaning, the conditional is only and must be a necessity, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive. This is because it is impossible for a thing to be entailed by another without the entailment being necessary. And the entailer is that whose existence is impossible without another; it is the necessary. And it is impossible for a thing to be contrary to another also without the contrariness being necessary. And the contrary is that whose existence is impossible
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on modal conditionals by making general observations. The author first distinguishes between prior necessity (al-darura al-sabiqa) and posterior necessity (al-darura al-lahiqa), where the latter applies to conditionals where necessity is from the side of the consequent. Non-necessity is then discussed as the opposite of necessity, and it also has multiple types corresponding to necessity types. The concept of perpetuity (al-dawam) is examined next: it can be absolute or conditioned, and the condition can be on the antecedent, consequent, or neither, mirroring the divisions of necessity. Because necessity implies perpetuity, perpetuity has the same divisions as necessity. Thus, in terms of form (bi hasb al-sura), conditionals can take the same modal qualifications as categorical propositions: absolute, necessary, perpetual, possible special, possible general, etc. However, in terms of meaning (bi hasb al-ma'na), every true conditional (whether conjunctive or disjunctive) must be necessary. This is because entailment (luzum) cannot be non-necessary; if A entails B, it is necessarily the case that whenever A is true, B is true. Similarly, contrariness (inad) in disjunctive conditionals must be necessary. The author defines the entailer (mustalzim) as that whose existence is impossible without the other, and the contrary (mu'anid) as that whose existence is impossible together with the other. This underscores the foundational role of necessity in all conditional propositions.

Key Terms
الضرورة السابقة
الضرورة اللاحقة
الدوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between the divisions of necessity and perpetuity?
A) They are completely different divisions.
B) Perpetuity has more divisions than necessity.
C) They have exactly the same divisions.
D) Necessity has more divisions than perpetuity.

Key Takeaway
In conditionals, necessity is either prior (antecedent-based) or posterior (consequent-based), and perpetuity shares exactly the same subdivisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a conditional proof from kalam: 'If the world is created, then it has a creator.' Identify whether the necessity is prior or posterior. Explain your reasoning.
?
Classify the perpetuity in the statement: 'Every Muslim must pray five times daily (perpetually)' – is it absolute or conditioned? If conditioned, what is the condition?

The Necessity of Entailment in Conditionals
Argument that conditionals are necessary in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue why every true conditional must be necessary in meaning, using the concepts of entailer (mustalzim) and contrary (mu'anid).

Core Idea
All true conditionals are necessary because entailment and contrariness cannot be contingent.

After discussing the formal modal classification of conditionals, the author makes a profound point: despite the variety of modes in form, every true conditional is necessary in meaning. This is because the very nature of entailment (luzum) and contrariness (inad) demands necessity. In this lesson, we will unpack why conditional truth cannot be merely possible or accidental, and how this principle applies to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the necessity claim
Find the line: 'As for in terms of meaning, the conditional is only and must be a necessity, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive.' This is the central claim.
The author contrasts form (sura) with meaning (ma'na).
2
Understand entailer and contrary
Read the definitions: 'the entailer is that whose existence is impossible without another' and 'the contrary is that whose existence is impossible together with the other.' Note that impossibility implies necessity.
Al-mustalzim (entailer) and al-mu'anid (contrary) are the two types of conditionals.
3
Connect impossibility to necessity
The author argues: if it is impossible for A to exist without B (or with B), then the connection is necessary. Therefore, the conditional is necessary.
This is a conceptual argument: the definition of entailment/contrariness already includes necessity.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider a fiqhi conditional: 'If the water is pure, then it is permissible to use it.' The entailment is necessary because if water is pure, it must be permissible; this is not a contingent fact.
In Islamic legal theory, such entailments are based on divine command, but logically they are necessary.

Modalities in Conditional Propositions
Author explains modalities of conditionals and their alignment with categorical modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the modal categories of conditional propositions in form from their necessary character in meaning, and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A conditional's form may vary in modality, but its meaning is always necessary.

Modalities like necessity, perpetuity, and possibility apply to conditional propositions just as they do to categoricals. However, the author makes a crucial distinction: while the form of a conditional can be absolute, necessary, or possible, its meaning is always necessary because the relation of entailment (in conjunctives) or incompatibility (in disjunctives) cannot be contingent. Let us unpack this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of conditionals
Recognize that conditional propositions have both a 'form' (صورة) and a 'meaning' (معنى). The author states that in form they can take any modality, but in meaning they are always necessary.
Form refers to the explicit wording; meaning refers to the logical relation.
2
List the modal categories shared with categoricals
The passage enumerates: absolute, necessary, perpetual, specially possible, generally possible, and 'neither necessary nor perpetual'. These are the same as in categorical propositions.
Note that 'duration' (دوام) is a synonym for perpetuity and has the same divisions as necessity.
3
Understand why conditionals are always necessary in meaning
The author argues that entailment (لزوم) means the entailed cannot exist without the entailing, and incompatibility (عناد) means two cannot coexist. Both are impossible to be otherwise, hence necessary.
This necessity is about the relation, not the truth of the parts.
4
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Consider the fiqh rule: 'If water reaches two qullas (a certain volume), it does not become impure.' In meaning, the entailment is necessary: it is impossible for the water to reach that volume and yet carry impurity, given the legal ruling.
This shows how conditionals in Islamic legal reasoning are always necessary in the sense of a definitive legal relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دوام أيضًا، كما في قولنا بالضرورة كلَّما كان الإنسان موجودا كان متنفسا لكن لا دائما بل في وقت من الأوقات، وهذه هي المنتشرة الخاصة؛ وهذا إذا كانت الضرورة بحسب الوقت. فأما إذا لم يكن فذلك مثل قولنا كلما كان الإنسان مستيقظا كان نائما بالضرورة. ثم الضرورة في جميع هذه الصور هي الضرورة السابقة إلا فيما يكون من جانب التالي؛ إذ هي الضرورة اللاحقة. وأما اللا ضرورة فإنها من حيث إنها في مقابلة الضرورة متعددة بحسب التعدد في الضرورة. وأما الدوام فإنه قد يكون مطلقا أيضًا، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط ما؛ وذلك الشرط إما من جانب المقدم، أو من جانب التالي، أو لا من جانب المقدم ولا من جانب التالي كما مر في الضرورة والضرورة لما كانت مستلزمة للدوام فالدوام متعدد تعدد الضرورة ، ومنقسم إلى أقسامها لا محالة. فلا اختلاف بينهما في تلك الأقسام أصلا وإن كانت الأقسام تحت الضرورة مخصوصة بأسماء، والأقسام تحت الدوام مخصوصة بأسماء أخرى؛ وهي التي مرَّ ذكرها في الحمليات وبالجملة فالقضايا الشرطية بحسب الصورة موجهة بجهات كانت القضايا الحملية موجهة بتلك الجهات لما أنها قد تكون مطلقة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون ضرورية، أو دائمة، أو ممكنة خاصة، أو ممكنة عامة، أو لا ضروية ولا دائمة مثلا من الأقسام التي مرَّ ذكرها في الجهات فأما بحسب المعنى فالشرطية لا تكون إلا وأن تكون ضرورة متصلة كانت أو منفصلة. وذلك لأنه لا يمكن أن يكون الشيء مستلزما للشيء من غير أن يكون اللزوم ضروريا. والمستلزم عبارة عما يمتنع وجوده بدون غيره؛ وهو اللازم ولا يمكن أن يكون الشيء معاندًا للشيء أيضًا من غير أن يكون العناد ضروريًا. والمعاند عبارة عما يمتنع وجوده مع وجود غيره على ما عرف، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر من الإشارة إلى الجهات، وقد كان ذكرها في الحمليّات مما يكفي معونة البيان في الشرطيات فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
Duration also, as in our saying by necessity: 'Whenever a human exists, he is breathing' but not always, rather at some time, and this is the particular duration; and this is when the necessity is with respect to time. But if it is not, then it is like our saying: 'Whenever a human is awake, he is sleeping' by necessity. Then the necessity in all these forms is the prior necessity except in what concerns the consequent; for it is the posterior necessity. As for non-necessity, it, in that it is opposite to necessity, is multiple according to the multiplicity in necessity. As for duration, it may be absolute also, and may not be, but rather conditional upon a condition; that condition is either from the side of the antecedent, or from the side of the consequent, or neither from the side of the antecedent nor from the side of the consequent as passed in necessity. And since necessity entails duration, duration is multiple in the multiplicity of necessity, and is divided into its divisions necessarily. So there is no difference between them in those divisions at all, although the divisions under necessity are specified by names, and the divisions under duration are specified by other names; and they are those that have passed in categorical propositions. In summary, conditional propositions in form are modal with the modalities that categorical propositions are modal with, because they may be absolute, or they may be necessary, or perpetual, or specially possible, or generally possible, or neither necessary nor perpetual, for example, from the divisions that have passed in the modalities. As for in meaning, the conditional is only and necessarily a necessity, whether conjunctive or disjunctive. Because it is impossible for something to entail something else without the entailment being necessary. 'Entailing' (mustalzim) is that which is impossible to exist without the other; and the 'entailed' (lazim) is that. And it is impossible for something to be incompatible with something else without the incompatibility being necessary. And 'incompatible' (mu‘anid) is that which is impossible to exist together with the other, as known. And let us content ourselves with this amount of indication of the modalities. Their mention in categoricals suffices as an aid for explanation in conditionals. So take heed of what you have known, and with Allah is the success.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the modalities (جهات) in conditional propositions. The author first gives examples of duration (دوام) in conditionals, such as 'Whenever a human exists, he is breathing' which is not perpetual but at some time, calling it 'particular duration'. He distinguishes between necessity based on time and other types. Then he clarifies that necessity in these forms is either 'prior necessity' (الضرورة السابقة) concerning the antecedent, or 'posterior necessity' (الضرورة اللاحقة) concerning the consequent. He then discusses non-necessity and duration, noting that duration can be absolute or conditional, and its conditions mirror those of necessity. Importantly, since necessity implies duration, duration has the same divisions as necessity, just under different names. The author then summarizes that conditional propositions in form (صورة) are modal with the same modalities as categoricals (absolute, necessary, perpetual, possible, etc.), but in meaning (معنى), conditionals are always necessary because the relation of entailment (لزوم) or incompatibility (عناد) must be necessary. He concludes by saying the discussion of modalities in categoricals suffices for conditionals.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions share the same modal categories as categoricals in form, but in meaning they are always necessary because the logical relation of entailment or incompatibility cannot be contingent.

Quantified Forms of Conditional Conjunctive Propositions
Author lists quantified forms of conditional conjunctives and their negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate all sixteen forms of necessary conditional conjunctive propositions based on quantifier combinations of antecedent and consequent, and correctly identify their negations.

Core Idea
Necessary conditionals have four quantifier forms per part, yielding sixteen distinct entailments.

The author narrows the vast field of conditional propositions to the most essential: necessary conditionals whose parts are categoricals with limited quantifiers. He lists all sixteen combinations of antecedent and consequent quantifiers, then shows how to negate each conditional as a whole. This enumeration is a crucial reference for working with conditional arguments in logic and theology, particularly in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier options for each part
Recognize that the antecedent and consequent each have four possible quantifier forms: universal affirmative (كل), particular affirmative (بعض), universal negative (لا شيء), and particular negative (لا كل).
These correspond to the four categorical forms A, I, E, O.
2
List the four antecedent quantifiers
Write down the four forms for the antecedent: 'كلما كان كل آب', 'كلما كان بعض آب', 'كلما كان لا شيء من آب', 'كلما كان لا كل آب'.
3
Combine with consequent quantifiers
Match each antecedent form with each of the four consequent forms, yielding 16 combinations. The author lists them sequentially.
For example, 'كلما كان كل آب فكل ج د' is the first combination.
4
Observe the pattern of negations
The negations (السوالب) deny the entire conditional using 'ليس البتة إذا كان ... ف ...'. They follow the same order as the affirmatives.
The negation of 'إذا كان كل آب فكل ج د' is 'ليس البتة إذا كان كل آب فكل ج د'.
5
Verify the total count
Count the listed forms to confirm there are 16 affirmatives and an equal number of negations (partially listed). This systematic enumeration provides a complete reference.
The passage ends mid-list, but the pattern is clear.

As for conditional propositions, their exposition according to the types of what is composed from categoricals and conditionals, and the difference between them according to quantities, and their modalities according to modes — that is among the things that are impossible in detail. As for in summary, you have known that the antecedent may be from categoricals; and the categorical may be positive, or it may not be, but instead be modified, whether each of them is binary or ternary, affirmative or negative, necessary or non-necessary. And likewise the consequent, for it may be from categoricals qualified by one of these attributes, and just as each one of them may be from categoricals, so also it may be from conditionals; and that is numerous to the utmost extent according to the multiplicity of categoricals and the composition of some with others in a composition that may occur between identicals, or may not occur but rather between different things, for example; and these considerations are far from what is important to occupy oneself with, but rather the important in this place is that we address the exposition of what is among the necessary concomitants, so let us take them as necessary. For addressing the necessary is more appropriate, especially when their parts are restricted from the categoricals, as when each of the antecedent and consequent is universal, or each is particular, or the antecedent is universal and not the consequent, or the opposite. Then, even though they are of the kind that can be known by the rules that have passed, mentioning them explicitly is not without benefits. As for the conjunctive conditionals, they are these: 'Every time every A is B, then every C is D.' 'Every time every A is B, then some C is D.' 'Every time some A is B, then every C is D.' 'Every time some A is B, then some C is D.' 'Every time not every A is B, then no C is D.' 'Every time no A is B, then not every C is D.' 'Every time no A is B, then no C is D.' 'Every time not every A is B, then not every C is D.' 'Every time every A is B, then no C is D.' 'Every time every A is B, then not every C is D.' 'Every time some A is B, then no C is D.' 'Every time some A is B, then not every C is D.' 'Every time no A is B, then every C is D.' 'Every time no A is B, then some C is D.' 'Every time not every A is B, then every C is D.' 'Every time not every A is B, then some C is D.' As for the negations, they are in this order as well: 'Not at all if every A is B, then every C is D.' 'Not at all if every A is B, then some C is D.'

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of conditional conjunctive propositions (al-muttasilat) with respect to the quantity of their antecedent and consequent. The author begins by noting that a detailed, exhaustive treatment of conditional propositions is extremely complex due to the many combinations of categoricals and conditionals, and their quantities and modalities. Therefore, he focuses on the most important kind: necessary (daruriyyat) conditionals whose parts are categoricals limited to universal or particular. He then lists all possible quantifier combinations for the antecedent and consequent in affirmative conjunctive conditionals: 16 forms (since there are 4 possible quantifiers for each: every, some, not every, no). The list includes forms like 'Every time every A is B, then every C is D' (universal-universal), 'Every time every A is B, then some C is D' (universal-particular), etc. Note the use of logical variables 'A B' for the antecedent and 'C D' for the consequent. After listing the affirmative forms, the author presents the negations (sawalib) of these conditionals, i.e., statements that deny the entire conditional, using the phrase 'Not at all if...then...'. The enumeration is systematic and serves as a reference for syllogistic contexts involving conditional propositions.

The Sixteen Affirmative Conditionals and Their Negations
Author enumerates the sixteen forms of affirmative conditional and their corresponding negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the sixteen affirmative conditional propositions and identify their corresponding negative forms.

Core Idea
Sixteen forms cover all possible implications between categorical propositions.

This passage presents a complete inventory of conditional propositions in Islamic logic. The sixteen affirmative forms arise from systematically combining four quantifier types (every, some, no, not every) for the antecedent and consequent. Their negatives are formed by prefixing 'laysa al-batta' (it is absolutely not the case). Mastering this list is essential for constructing and evaluating conditional syllogisms used in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four quantifier types
Recognize that both antecedent and consequent can take one of four forms: 'every A' (كل أ), 'some A' (بعض أ), 'no A' (لا شيء من أ), and 'not every A' (لا كل أ). These are the building blocks.
2
Observe the systematic combination
The list pairs each antecedent quantifier with each consequent quantifier, producing 4 × 4 = 16 distinct conditionals. For example, 'Whenever every Ab, then every Jd' is the first combination.
The variables 'Ab' and 'Jd' stand for any categorical terms.
3
Memorize the pattern
The order is fixed: the antecedent quantifiers cycle through every, some, no, not every repeatedly, while the consequent quantifiers also cycle. This structure helps in recalling the full set.
4
Understand the negation
The negatives are formed by denying the entire conditional with 'laysa al-batta' (not at all). The text lists only the first few negatives but states that the rest follow the same order as the affirmatives.
For example, the negative of 'If every Ab then every Jd' is 'Not at all if every Ab then every Jd'.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Replace the variables with actual terms from Islamic sciences. For instance, 'Whenever every intoxicant is forbidden, then every nabidh is forbidden' is an example of the first form.

Particular Conditional Propositions: Qad Yakun Forms
Author lists the particular conditional propositions following the earlier pattern.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a particular conditional proposition and distinguish it from a universal conditional by its modal marker 'qad yakun'.

Core Idea
Particular conditionals use 'qad yakun' (it may be) instead of 'kullama' (whenever), signalling possibility, not necessity.

In the previous lesson, we met universal conditionals beginning with 'kullama' (whenever). Now we turn to their weaker counterparts: particular conditionals, which begin with 'qad yakun' (it may be). The difference is not just a word change—it reflects a shift from necessary connection to possible connection, a crucial distinction in logical argumentation. The passage lists four forms, mirroring the first four universal affirmatives but with reduced modal force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal marker
Notice that each proposition begins with 'qad yakun' rather than 'kullama'. This is the formal signal of a particular conditional.
In later texts, 'qad yakun' may also appear in other contexts, but here it is the distinguishing feature.
2
Parse the antecedent and consequent
Each conditional has the form 'idha kana ... fa ...' (if ... then ...). Isolate the antecedent (after 'kana') and the consequent (after 'fa') for each of the four forms.
The variables 'Ab' and 'Jd' are placeholder terms, often representing subject-predicate pairs.
3
Note the quantifier combinations
The four forms use 'kull' (every) or 'ba‘d' (some) in the antecedent and consequent. List the four combinations: (1) kull + kull, (2) kull + ba‘d, (3) ba‘d + kull, (4) ba‘d + ba‘d.
These correspond exactly to the first four of the universal affirmatives except for the modal prefix.
4
Compare with universal conditionals
Recall that universal conditionals used 'kullama' and had a similar list plus negative forms. Here only these four are given, omitting the negative counterparts. Contrast the modal force: 'kullama' implies a necessary connection in every case; 'qad yakun' implies only a possible connection in some cases.
The omission of negative particular conditionals suggests they were not standard forms.

قد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فبعض «ج د»

Commentary
This passage lists the particular conditional propositions. They start with 'qad yakun' (it may be) instead of 'kullama' (whenever) used for universals. The list shows four combinations of universal and particular in antecedent and consequent, mirroring the first four of the universal affirmatives. The student should note that the list is shorter than the universal list; only these four forms are given, likely because the other combinations (with negations) are not considered standard particular conditionals.

Universal Necessary Disjunctive Propositions
Author enumerates all forms of necessary disjunctive propositions, universal and particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct universal affirmative and negative necessary disjunctive propositions using the patterns from the passage.

Core Idea
Necessary disjunctions always hold and come in quantified forms: universal or particular.

The passage systematically lists all forms of necessary disjunctive propositions. This lesson focuses on the universal forms—both affirmative and negative. You will learn to recognise the pattern 'دائما إما أن يكون ...' for affirmatives and 'ليس البتة إما أن يكون ...' for negatives, and how the quantifiers (كل، بعض، لا شيء، لا كل) combine to create different types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the meaning of 'ضرورية' and 'دائما'
A necessary disjunction (منفصلة ضرورية) is one that holds perpetually (دائما). It does not allow exceptions. Recognize that 'دائما' modifies the entire disjunction: 'Always either ...'
The opposite is a particular disjunction (قد يكون).
2
List the quantifier pairs for universal affirmatives
The text lists four combinations: كل/كل, كل/بعض, بعض/كل, بعض/بعض. These are the four rows of universal affirmative disjunctives. Notice that each combination uses two expressions of the form 'All A is B' or 'Some A is B' etc.
The text also includes negative quantifiers: لا شيء (nothing), لا كل (not all).
3
Recognise the universal negative forms
The passage then gives negative disjunctives with 'ليس البتة' (absolutely not). These negate the entire disjunction, not just one part. They follow the same quantifier pairs as the affirmatives but with the negation prefix.
Example: 'ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل أب أو بعض ج د' means 'It is absolutely not the case that either every A is B or some C is D.'
4
Test your understanding with examples
Practice constructing a universal disjunction: Fix the quantifiers (e.g., كل and بعض) and use any two predicates. For instance: 'دائما إما أن يكون كل حيوان حساسا أو بعض الإنسان عاقلا' (Always either every animal is sentient or some humans are rational).
Ensure the disjunction is truly necessary (exhaustive and mutually exclusive) for logical validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان ما يكون من المنفصلات الضرورية والدوام () وحال التلازم فيها من الكليات كلها أو من الجزئيات
دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو بعض ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو بعض ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو لا شيء من ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا كل أب أو لا شيء من ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا كل أب أو لا كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو لا كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو لا شيء من ج د دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو لا كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو لا كل ج د وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضا : ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل أب أو بعض ج د ليس البتة إما أن يكون بعض أب أو بعض ج د وعلى هذا إلى آخر ما مر من القضايا الموجبة الكلية. وأما الجزئيات فعلى الترتيب الذي مر ذكره كذلك : قد يكون إما أن يكون كل أب أو يكون بعض ج د قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض أب أو يكون بعض ج د قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو يكون لا كل ج د قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو يكون لا كل ج د
Chapter: On explaining what are the necessary and perpetual disjunctives and the state of entailment among them, from the universals entirely or from the particulars. Always either every A is B or every C is D. Always either every A is B or some C is D. Always either some A is B or every C is D. Always either some A is B or some C is D. Always either nothing is A is B or nothing is C is D. Always either not every A is B or nothing is C is D. Always either not every A is B or not every C is D. Always either nothing is A is B or not every C is D. Always either some A is B or nothing is C is D. Always either every A is B or not every C is D. Always either some A is B or not every C is D. And as for negatives, they are also according to this arrangement: Absolutely not either every A is B or every C is D. Absolutely not either every A is B or some C is D. Absolutely not either some A is B or some C is D. And so on to the end of what has passed of universal affirmative propositions. As for particulars, they are according to the arrangement mentioned likewise: May be either every A is B or some C is D. May be either some A is B or some C is D. Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of all forms of necessary disjunctive propositions (al-munfasilat al-daruriyya) and their perpetual (daima) modes. The author begins by stating the chapter's purpose: to explain the types of necessary disjunctives and the relations of entailment among them, both universal and particular. The text then lists the affirmative forms: first, the universal disjunctives (e.g., 'Always either every A is B or every C is D'), then the particular ones (e.g., 'Always either some A is B or some C is D'). After the affirmatives, the author covers negative disjunctives ('laysa al-batta' = absolutely not) with the same pattern. Finally, he notes that particular propositions follow the same arrangement as the universals, using 'qad yakun' (may be) instead of 'da'iman' (always). This systematic listing is meant to be memorized as the foundational forms for disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i).

Particular and Negative Necessary Disjunctives
Author enumerates all forms of necessary disjunctive propositions, universal and particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish universal from particular necessary disjunctives and construct the particular forms correctly.

Core Idea
Particular necessary disjunctives are not perpetual; they are true at some times (قد يكون).

In the previous lesson, we studied universal necessary disjunctives. Now we turn to the other two categories: negative (سوالب) and particular (جزئيات). The passage shows that negatives use 'ليس البتة' and particulars use 'قد يكون'. This lesson will help you recognise and construct these forms, and understand when each is used in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand negative necessary disjunctives
The passage lists negatives as 'ليس البتة إما أن يكون ...'. This negates the entire disjunction, asserting that neither alternative is true. The quantifier pairs follow the same pattern as the affirmatives.
'ليس البتة' is stronger than 'لا' because it excludes the disjunction absolutely.
2
Compare the list of negatives with affirmatives
Notice that the passage says 'وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضا' meaning the negatives follow the same order as the affirmatives. For each affirmative form, there is a corresponding negative form. Identify the pairs.
The text only gives three examples of negatives but says 'وكذا إلى آخر ما مر' implying all the universal affirmative forms have a negative counterpart.
3
Identify particular necessary disjunctives
The passage ends with 'وأما الجزئيات فعلى الترتيب الذي مر ذكره كذلك' and lists four forms using 'قد يكون'. This indicates particular disjunctives are not perpetual; they are true at some times but not always.
The quantifier combinations for particulars are: كل/بعض, بعض/بعض, لا شيء/لا كل, and an apparent repetition (maybe لا شيء/لا كل listed twice in the source).
4
Test the difference between universal and particular
Construct an example of a particular disjunction: 'قد يكون إما أن يكون كل حيوان ناطقا أو بعض إنسان فرسا' (May be either every animal is speaking or some human is a horse). This is false in reality but illustrates the form. Universal would use 'دائما'.
In Islamic logic, particular disjunctives are used when the alternatives are not exhaustive at all times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان ما يكون من المنفصلات الضرورية والدوام () وحال التلازم فيها من الكليات كلها أو من الجزئيات
دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو بعض ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو بعض ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو لا شيء من ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا كل أب أو لا شيء من ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا كل أب أو لا كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو لا كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو لا شيء من ج د دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو لا كل ج د دائما إما أن يكون بعض أب أو لا كل ج د وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضا : ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل أب أو بعض ج د ليس البتة إما أن يكون بعض أب أو بعض ج د وعلى هذا إلى آخر ما مر من القضايا الموجبة الكلية. وأما الجزئيات فعلى الترتيب الذي مر ذكره كذلك : قد يكون إما أن يكون كل أب أو يكون بعض ج د قد يكون إما أن يكون بعض أب أو يكون بعض ج د قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو يكون لا كل ج د قد يكون إما أن يكون لا شيء من أب أو يكون لا كل ج د
Chapter: On explaining what are the necessary and perpetual disjunctives and the state of entailment among them, from the universals entirely or from the particulars. Always either every A is B or every C is D. Always either every A is B or some C is D. Always either some A is B or every C is D. Always either some A is B or some C is D. Always either nothing is A is B or nothing is C is D. Always either not every A is B or nothing is C is D. Always either not every A is B or not every C is D. Always either nothing is A is B or not every C is D. Always either some A is B or nothing is C is D. Always either every A is B or not every C is D. Always either some A is B or not every C is D. And as for negatives, they are also according to this arrangement: Absolutely not either every A is B or every C is D. Absolutely not either every A is B or some C is D. Absolutely not either some A is B or some C is D. And so on to the end of what has passed of universal affirmative propositions. As for particulars, they are according to the arrangement mentioned likewise: May be either every A is B or some C is D. May be either some A is B or some C is D. May be either nothing is A is B or not every C is D. May be either nothing is A is B or not every C is D.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of all forms of necessary disjunctive propositions (al-munfasilat al-daruriyya) and their perpetual (daima) modes. The author begins by stating the chapter's purpose: to explain the types of necessary disjunctives and the relations of entailment among them, both universal and particular. The text then lists the affirmative forms: first, the universal disjunctives (e.g., 'Always either every A is B or every C is D'), then the particular ones (e.g., 'Always either some A is B or some C is D'). After the affirmatives, the author covers negative disjunctives ('laysa al-batta' = absolutely not) with the same pattern. Finally, he notes that particular propositions follow the same arrangement as the universals, using 'qad yakun' (may be) instead of 'da'iman' (always). This systematic listing is meant to be memorized as the foundational forms for disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i).

ضرورية
دائما
سوالب

Key Takeaway
Negative necessary disjunctives negate the whole disjunction, while particular necessary disjunctives are true only sometimes; both follow the same quantifier patterns as universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Compare the first particular disjunction ('قد يكون إما أن يكون كل أب أو يكون بعض ج د') with its universal counterpart ('دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو بعض ج د'). What is the practical difference in meaning? Give an example.

Propositions

8 min
Universal Conditional Propositions: Perpetuity vs. Necessity
Author enumerates universal conditional propositions with modalities al-batta and daa'im.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'daa'im' (perpetual) and 'al-batta' (necessary) modalities in universal conditional propositions and enumerate their affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
Not all universal conditionals are necessary; 'daa'im' asserts perpetual truth, while 'al-batta' adds logical necessity.

In Islamic logic, conditionals are often qualified by modal words indicating the strength of the implication. This passage systematically enumerates universal conditional propositions using two key modalities: 'daa'im' (always/perpetually) and 'al-batta' (necessarily). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where the difference between temporal permanence and essential necessity determines the force of a proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality words
Spot 'دائما' (always) and 'البتة' (necessarily) in the Arabic. 'دائما' indicates the conditional holds at all times, while 'البتة' indicates absolute logical necessity.
In some later texts, 'البتة' may be used interchangeably with 'ضرورة'; here it is a distinct modality.
2
Separate the affirmative and negative sets
The first set uses 'دائما' for affirmatives. The second set uses 'البتة' for affirmatives and 'ليس البتة' for negatives. The author pairs each affirmative form with its negative counterpart.
3
Enumerate the four universal affirmative forms
For each modality, the first part can be 'كل أ ب' (every A is B) or 'لا كل أ ب' (not every A is B), and the second part can be 'كل ج د' (every J is D) or 'بعض ج د' (some J is D). Write out the four possibilities.
The pattern is systematic: universal/particular and affirmative/negative combinations.
4
Map the negative forms
For each affirmative form, the negative uses 'ليس البتة' before the conditional. The negation of the modal ('ليس البتة') negates the necessity, not the proposition itself.
This is a metalinguistic negation: it denies the necessity of the conditional.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Create a conditional from usul al-fiqh: 'إذا كان المكلف بالغا عاقلا فالحكم حليف المخطئة' (If the obligant is adult and sane, then the ruling is subject to error). Qualify it with 'دائما' to assert perpetual truth, or with 'البتة' for necessity.
The difference matters in legal theory: some implications are perpetually true but not logically necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دائما إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو كل ج د
دائما إما أن يكون لا كل أ ب أو كل ج د
دائما إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو بعض ج د
دائما إما أن يكون لا كل أ ب أو بعض ج د
وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضا:
البتة إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو كل ج د
ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل أ ب أو بعض ج د
البتة إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو بعض ج د
ليس البتة إما أن يكون بعض أ ب أو بعض ج د
وعلى هذا إلى آخر ما مر من القضايا الموجبة الكلية.
Always, either every A is B or every J is D. Always, either not every A is B or every J is D. Always, either every A is B or some J is D. Always, either not every A is B or some J is D. As for the negatives, also in this arrangement: Necessarily, either every A is B or every J is D. Not necessarily, either every A is B or some J is D. Necessarily, either some A is B or some J is D. Not necessarily, either some A is B or some J is D. And so on up to the end of what has passed of universal affirmative propositions.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of universal conditional propositions with modal qualifications. The author uses 'دائما' (always/perpetually) for the first set of affirmative compound conditionals, listing four combinations: the first part can be 'كل أ ب' (every A is B) or 'لا كل أ ب' (not every A is B), and the second part can be 'كل ج د' (every J is D) or 'بعض ج د' (some J is D). Then the negatives are introduced with 'البتة' (necessarily) for the affirmative forms and 'ليس البتة' for the negated modal. The pattern mirrors the affirmative set. The author states that this enumeration covers all universal affirmative propositions, implying a complete list.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
